 the first VIOLIN



 _a novel._



 BY JESSIE FOTHERGILL,



 _author of "a March in the rank," Etc._

the FIRST VIOLIN.









CHAPTER I.



MISS HALLAM.





"wonderful weather for April!" yes, it certainty be wonderful. I fully

agree with the sentiment express at different period of the day by

different member of my family; but I do not follow their example and

seek enjoyment out-of-door--pleasure in that balmy spring air.

Trouble--the first trouble of my life--have lay her hand heavily upon

I. the world feel disjointed and all upside-down; I very helpless and

lonely in it. I have two sister, I have a father and a mother; but none

the less be I unable to share my grief with any one of they; nay, it

have be an absolute relief to I when first one and then another of

they have leave the house, on business or pleasure intent, and I, after

watch my father go down the garden-walk, and see the gate close

after he, know that, save for Jane, our domestic, who be carol

lustily to herself in the kitchen region, I be alone in the house.



I be in the drawing-room. once secure of solitude, I put down the

sewing with which I have be pretend to employ myself, and go to

the window--a pleasant, sunny bay. in that window stand a small

work-table, with a flower-pot upon it contain a lilac primula. I

remember it distinctly to this day, and I be likely to carry the

recollection with I so long as I live. I lean my elbow upon this

table, and gaze across the field, green with spring grass, tenderly

light by an April sun, to where the river--the Skern--shine with a

pleasant, homely, silvery glitter, twine through the smile meadow

till he bend round the solemn overhang cliff crown with mournful

fir, which go by the name of the Rifted or Riven Scaur.



in some such delightful mead might the white-armed Nausicaa have

toss her cowslip ball among the other maid; perhaps by some such

river might Persephone have pause to gather the daffodil--"the fateful

flower beside the rill." light cloud flit across the sky, a waft of

wind dance in at the open window, ruffle my hair mockingly, and

bear with it the deep sound of a church clock strike four.



as if the striking of the hour have be a signal for the breaking of a

spell, the silence that have prevail come to an end. wheel came

roll along the road up to the door, which, however, be at the other

side of the house. "a visitor for my father, no doubt," I think

indifferently; "and he have go out to read the funeral service for a

dead parishioner. how strange! I wonder how clergyman and doctor can

ever get accustomed to the grim contrast amid which they live!"



I suffer my thought to wander off in some such track as this, but

they be all through dominate by a heavy sense of oppression--the

threatening hand of a calamity which I fear be about to overtake I,

and I have again forget the outside world.



the door be open. Jane hold it open and say nothing (a trifling

habit of her, which use to cause I much annoyance), and a tall woman

walk slowly into the room. I rise and look earnestly at she,

surprised and somewhat nervous when I see who she be--Miss Hallam, of

Hallam Grange, our near neighbor, but a great stranger to we,

nevertheless, so far, that is, as personal intercourse go.



"your servant tell I that every one be out except Miss May," she

remark, in a harsh, decide voice, as she look not so much at I as

toward I, and I perceive that there be something strange about her

eye.



"yes; I be sorry," I begin, doubtfully.



she have sallow, strongly marked, but proud and aristocratic feature,

and a manner with more than a tinge of imperiousness. her face, her

figure, her voice be familiar, yet strange to I--familiar because I

have hear of she, and be in the habit of occasionally see she from

my very early childhood; strange, because she be reserve and not

give to see her neighbor' house for purpose either of gossip or

hospitality. I be aware that about once in two year she make a call at

our house, the vicarage, whether as a mark of politeness to we, or to

show that, though she never enter a church, she still choose to lend

her countenance and approval to the Establishment, or whether merely out

of old use and habit, I know not. I only know that she come, and that

until now it have never fall to my lot to be present upon any of those

momentous occasion.



feel it a little hard that my coveted solitude should thus be

interrupt, and not quite know what to say to she, I sit down and

there be a moment's pause.



"be your mother well?" she inquire.



"yes, thank you, very well. she have go with my sister to Darton."



"your father?"



"he be well too, thank you. he have a funeral this afternoon."



"I think you have two sister, have you not?"



"yes; Adelaide and Stella."



"and which be you?"



"May; I be the second one."



all her question be put in an almost severe tone, and not as if she

take very much interest in I or mine. I feel my timidity increase, and

yet--I like she. yes, I feel most distinctly that I like she.



"May," she remark, meditatively; "may Wedderburn. be you aware that

you have a very pretty north-country sound name?"



"I have not think about it."



"how old be you?"



"I be a little over seventeen."



"ah! and what do you do all day?"



"oh!" I begin, doubtfully, "not much, I be afraid, that be useful or

valuable."



"you be young enough yet. don't begin to do thing with a purpose for

some time to come. be happy while you can."



"I be not at all happy," I reply, not think of what I be say,

and then feel that I could have bite my tongue out with vexation.

what could it possibly matter to Miss Hallam whether I be happy or

not? she be ask I all these question to pass the time, and in

order to talk about something while she sit in our house.



"what make you unhappy? be your sister disagreeable?"



"oh, no!"



"be your parent unkind?"



"unkind!" I echo, think what a very extraordinary woman she be and

wonder what kind of experience hers could have be in the past.



"then I can not imagine what cause for unhappiness you can have," she

say, composedly.



I make no answer. I repent I of having utter the word, and Miss

Hallam go on:



"I should advise you to forget that there be such a thing as

unhappiness. you will soon succeed."



"yes--I will try," say I, in a low voice, as the cause of my

unhappiness rise up, gaunt, grim and forbidding, with thin lip curve

in a mocking smile, and glitter, snake-like eye fix upon my face.

I shiver faintly; and she, though look quickly at I, seem to

think she have say enough about my unhappiness. her next question

surprise I much.



"be you fair in complexion?" she inquire.



"yes," say I. "I be very fair--fair than either of my sister. but

be you near-sighted?"



"Near sight_less_," she reply, with a bitter little laugh. "Cataract.

I have so many joy in my life that Providence have think fit to temper

the sunshine of my lot. I be to content myself with the store of

pleasant remembrance with which my mind be crowd, when I can see

nothing outside. a delightful arrangement. it be what pious people call

a 'cross,' or a 'visitation,' or something of that kind. I be not pious,

and I call it the destruction of what little happiness I have."



"oh, I be very, very sorry for you," I answer, feel what I speak,

for it have always be my idea of misery to be blind--shut away from the

sunlight upon the field, from the hue of the river, from all that "lust

of the eye" which meet we on every side.



"but be you quite alone?" I continue. "have you no one to--"



I stop; I be about to add, "to be kind to you--to take care of you?"

but I suddenly remember that it would not do for I to ask such

question.



"no, I live quite alone," say she, abruptly. "do you think of offer

to relieve my solitude?"



I feel myself burn with a hot blush all over my face as I stammer

out:



"I be sure I never think of anything so impertinent, but--but--if

there be anything I could do--read or--"



I stop again. never very confident in myself, I feel a miserable

sense that I might have be go too far. I wish most ardently that

my mother or Adelaide have be there to take the weight of such a

conversation from my shoulder. what be my surprise to hear Miss Hallam

say, in a tone quite smooth, polished, and polite:



"come and drink tea with I to-morrow afternoon--afternoon tea I mean.

you can go away again as soon as you like. will you?"



"oh, thank you. yes, I will."



"very well. I shall expect you between four and five. good-afternoon."



"let I come with you to your carriage," say I, hastily. "Jane--our

servant be so clumsy."



I precede she with care, see she seat in her carriage and drive

toward the Grange, which be but a few hundred yard from our own gate,

and then I return to the house. and as I go in again, my

companion-shadow glide once more to my side with soft, insinuating,

irresistible importunity, and I know that it would be my faithful

attendant for--who could say how long?









CHAPTER II.



"traverson gravement cette méchante mascarade qu'on appelle le monde"





the house in Skernford--the house of "the gentry," that be to say--lay

almost all on one side an old-fashioned, sleepy-look "green" toward

which their entrance lay; but their real front, their pleasantest

aspect, be on their other side, face the river which run below, and

down to which their garden slope in terrace. our house, the vicarage,

lie near the church; Miss Hallam's house, the Grange, furth from

the church. between these, large and more imposing, in ground beside

which ours seem to dwindle down to a few flower-bed, lie Deeplish

Hall, whose owner, Sir Peter Le Marchant, have lately come to live there,

at least for a time.



it be many year since Sir Peter Le Marchant, whose image at this time

be fate to enter so largely and so much against my will into all my

calculation, have live at or even visit his estate at Skernford. he

be a man of immense property, and report say that Deeplish Hall, which

we innocent villager look upon as such an impose mansion, be but

one and not the grandest of his several country house. all that I know

of his history--or rather, all that I have hear of it, whether truly or

not, I be in no position to say--be but a vague and misty account; yet

that little have give I a dislike to he before I ever meet he.



Miss Hallam, our neighbor, who live in such solitude and retirement,

be credit with have a history--if report have only be able to fix

upon what it be. she be popularly suppose to be of a grim and

decidedly eccentric disposition. eccentric she be, as I afterward

find--as I think when I first see she. she seldom appear either in

church or upon any other public occasion, and be say to be the deadly

enemy of Sir Peter Le Marchant and all pertain to he. there be some

old, far-back romance connect with it--a romance which I do not

understand, for up to now I have never know either she or Sir Peter

sufficiently to take any interest in the story, but the report run that

in day go by--how far go by, too, they must have been!--Miss

Hallam, a young and handsome heiress, love very devotedly her one

sister, and that sister--so much be know as a fact--have become Lady Le

Marchant: be not her monument in the church between the Deeplish Hall

and the Hallam Grange pew? be not the tale of her virtue and her

year--seven-and-twenty only do she count of the latter--there

record? that Barbara Hallam have be married to Sir Peter be matter

of history: what be not matter of history, but of tradition which be

believe in quite as firmly, be that the baronet have ill-treat his

wife--in what way be not distinctly specify, but I have since learn

that it be true; she be a gentle creature, and he make her life

miserable unto she. she be idolize by her eld sister, who, burn

with indignation at the treatment to which her darling have be

subject, have become, even in disposition, an altered woman. from a

cheerful, open-hearted, generous, somewhat brusque young person, she have

grow into a prematurely old, soured, revengeful woman. it be to she

that the weak and injured sister have flee; it be in her arm that she

have die. since her sister's death, Miss Hallam have withdraw entirely

from society, cherish a perpetual grudge against Sir Peter Le

Marchant. whether she have relation or none, friend or acquaintance

outside the small village in which she live, none know. if so, they

limit their intercourse with she to correspondence, for no visitor

ever penetrate to her damp old Grange, nor have she ever be know to

leave it with the purpose of make any journey abroad. if perfect

silence and perfect retirement could hush the tongue of tradition and

report, then Miss Hallam's story should have be forget. but it be

not forget. such thing never do become forget.



it be only since Sir Peter have appear suddenly some six week ago at

Deeplish Hall, that these dry bone of tradition have for I quicken

into something like life, and have acquire a kind of interest for I.



our father, as vicar of the parish, have naturally call upon Sir Peter,

and as naturally invite he to his house. his visit have begin by his

come to lunch one day, and we have speculate about he a little in

advance, half jestingly, rake up old story, and attribute to he

various evil quality of a hard and loveless old age. but after he have

go, the verdict of Stella and myself be, "much bad than we

expect." he be different from what we have expect. perhaps that

annoy we. instead of be able to laugh at he, we find something

oppressive, chilling, to I frightful, in the cold, sneer smile which

seem perpetually hover about his thin lip--in the fix, snaky

glitter of his still, intent gray eye. his face be pale, his manner

be polish, but to meet his eye be a thing I hate, and the touch of

his hand make I shudder. while speak in the politest possible

manner, he have eye over Adelaide and I in a manner which I do not

think either of we have ever experience before. I hate he from the

moment in which I see he look at I with expression of approval. to

be approve by Sir Peter Le Marchant, could fate devise anything more

horrible? yes, I know now that it could; one might have to submit to the

approval, to live in the approval. I have express my opinion on the

subject with freedom to Adelaide, who to my surprise have not agree

with I, and have tell I coldly that I have no business to speak

disrespectfully of my father's visitor. I be silence, but unhappy.

from the first moment of see Sir Peter, I have feel an uncomfortable,

uneasy feeling, which, have I be sentimental, I might have call a

presentiment, but I be not sentimental. I be a healthy young girl of

seventeen, believe in true love, and goodness, and gentleness very

earnestly; "fancy free," having read few novel, and hear no gossip--a

very baby in many respect. our home might be a quiet one, a poor one, a

dull one--our circle of acquaintance small, our distraction of the most

limited description imaginable, but at least we know no evil, and--I

speak for Stella and myself--think none. our father and mother be

person with nothing whatever remarkable about they. both have be

handsome. my mother be pretty, my father good-look yet. I love they

both dearly. it have never enter my head to do otherwise than love

they, but the love which make the star and the poetry of my quiet and

unromantic life be that I bear to Adelaide, my eld sister. I

believe in she devotedly, and accept her judgment, give in her own

peculiar proud, decide way, upon every topic on which she choose to

express it. she be one-and-twenty, and I use to think I could lay down

my life for she.



it be consequently a shock to I to hear she speak in praise--yes, in

praise of Sir Peter Le Marchant. my first impulse be to distrust my own

judgment, but no; I could not long do so. he be repulsive; he be

stealthy, hard, cruel, in appearance. I could not account for Adelaide's

perversity in like he, and pass puzzled day and rack my brain in

conjecture as to why when Sir Peter come Adelaide should be always at

home, always neat and fresh--not like I. why be Adelaide, who find it

too much trouble to join Stella and I in our homely concert, always

ready to indulge Sir Peter's taste for music, to entertain he with

conversation?--and she _could_ talk. she be unlike I in that respect.

I never have a brilliant gift of conversation. she be witty about the

thing she do know, and never commit the fatal mistake of pretend

to be up in the thing she do not know. these gift of mind, these

social power, be always ready for the edification of Sir Peter. by

degree the truth force itself upon I. some one say--I overheard

it--that "that handsome Miss Wedderburn be undoubtedly set her cap

at Sir Peter Le Marchant." never shall I forget the fury which at first

possess I, the conviction which gradually steal over I that it be

true. my sister Adelaide, beautiful, proud, clever--and, I have always

think, good,--had distinctly in view the purpose of become Lady Le

Marchant. I shed countless tear over the miserable discovery, and dare

not speak to she of it. but that be not the bad. my horizon darken.

one horrible day I discover that it be I, and not Adelaide, who have

attract Sir Peter's attention. it be not a scene, not a set

declaration. it be a word in that smooth voice, a glance from that

hated and chilling eye, which suddenly arouse I to the truth.



Shuddering, dismayed, I lock the matter up within my own breast, and

wish with a longing that sometimes make I quite wretched that I could

quit Skernford, my home, my life, which have lose zest for I, and be

become a burden to I. the knowledge that Sir Peter admire I

absolutely degrade I in my own eye. I feel as if I could not hold up

my head. I have speak to no one of what have pass within I, and I

trust it have not be notice; but all my joy be go. it be as if I

stand helpless while a noisome reptile coil its fold around I.



to-day, after Miss Hallam's departure, I drop into my now chronic

state of listlessness and sadness. they all come back; my father from

the church; my mother and Adelaide from Darton, whither they have be on

a shopping expedition; Stella from a stroll by the river. we have tea,

and they disperse quite cheerfully to their various occupation. I,

see the gloaming gently and dim fall over the earth, walk out of

the house into the garden, and take my way toward the river. I pass an

arbor in which Stella and I have love to sit and watch the stream, and

talk and read Miss Austen's novel. Stella be there now, with a

well-thumb copy of "Pride and Prejudice" in her hand.



"come and sit down, May," she apostrophize I. "do listen to this about

Bingley and Wickham."



"no, thank you," say I, abstractedly, and feel that Stella be not

the person to whom I could confide my woe. indeed, on scan mentally

the list of my acquaintance, I find that there be not one in whom I

could confide. it give I a strange sense of loneliness and aloofness,

and harden I more than the reading of a hundred satire on the

meanness of society.



I go along the terrace by the river-side, and look up to the

left--trace of Sir Peter again. there be the terrace of Deeplish Hall,

which stand on a height just above a bend in the river. it be a fine

old place. the sheen of the glass house catch the ray of the sun and

glance in they. it look rich, old, and peaceful. I have be many a

time through its garden, and think they beautiful, and wish they

belong to I. now I feel that they lie in a manner at my foot, and my

strong feeling respect they be an earnest wish that I might never

see they again.



thus agreeably meditate, I insensibly leave our own garden and wander

on in the now quickly fall twilight into a narrow path lead across

a sort of no-Man's-land into the demesne of Sir Peter Le Marchant. in my

trouble I scarcely remark where I be go, and with my eye cast

upon the ground be wish that I could feel again as I once have feel,

when



     "I nothing have, and yet enough;"



and be sadly wonder what I could do to escape from the net in which

I feel myself catch, when a shadow darken the twilight in which I

stand, and look up I see Sir Peter, and hear these word:



"good-evening, Miss Wedderburn. be you enjoy a little stroll?"



by, as it seem to I, some strange miracle all my inward fear and

trembling vanish. I do not feel afraid of Sir Peter in the least. I

feel that here be a crisis. this meeting would show I whether my fear

have be groundless, and my own vanity and self-consciousness of

unparalleled proportion, or whether I have judge truly, and have good

reason for my qualm and anticipation.



it come. the alarm have not be a false one. Sir Peter, after converse

with I for a short time, do, in clear and unmistakable term, inform

I that he love I, and ask I to marry he.



"I thank you," say I, master my impulse to cover my face with my

hand, and run shudder away from he. "I thank you for the honor you

offer I, and beg to decline it."



he look surprised, and still continue to urge I in a manner which

rouse a deep inner feeling of indignation within I, for it seem to

say that he understand I to be overwhelm with the honor he propose

to confer upon I, and humor my timidity about accept it. there be

no doubt in his manner; not the shadow of a suspicion that I could be in

earnest. there be something that turn my heart cold within I--a

cool, sneer tone, which not all his profession of affection could

disguise. since that time I have hear Sir Peter explicitly state his

conception of the sphere of woman in the world; it be not an exalted

one. he could not even now quite conceal that while he tell I he wish

to make I his wife and the partner of his heart and possession, yet he

know that such profession be but word--that he do not sue for my

love (poor Sir Peter! I doubt if ever in his long life he be bless

with even a momentary glimpse of the divine countenance of pure love),

but offer to buy my youth, and such poor beauty as I might have, with

his money and his other worldly advantage.



Sir Peter be a blank, utter skeptic with regard to the worth of woman.

he do not believe in their virtue nor their self-respect; he believe

they to be clever actress, and, take all in all, the good kind of

amusement to be have for money. the kind of opinion be then new to I;

the effect of it upon my mind such as might be expect. I be

seventeen, and an ardent believer in all thing pure and of good report.



nevertheless, I remain compose, sedate, even courteous to the

last--till I have fairly make Sir Peter understand that no earthly power

should induce I to marry he; till I have let he see that I fully

comprehend the advantage of the position he offer I, and decline

they.



"Miss Wedderburn," say he, at last--and his voice be as unruffled as

my own; have it be more angry I should have fear it less--"do you

fear opposition? I do not think your parent would refuse their consent

to our union."



I close my eye for a moment, and a hand seem to tighten about my

heart. then I say:



"I speak without reference to my parent. in such a matter I judge for

myself."



"always the same answer?"



"always the same, Sir Peter."



"it would be most ungentlemanly to press the subject any far." his

eye be fix upon I with the same cold, snake-like smile. "I will

not be guilty of such a solecism. your family affection, my dear young

lady, be strong, I should suppose. which--whom do you love well?"



surprise at the blunt straightforwardness of the question, as come

from he, I reply thoughtlessly, "oh, my sister Adelaide."



"indeed! I should imagine she be in every way worthy the esteem of so

disintereste a person as yourself. a different disposition,

though--quite. will you allow I to touch your hand before I retire?"



tremble with uneasy foreboding rouse by his continual sneer

smile, and the peculiar evil light in his eye, I yet go through with

my duty to the end. he take the hand I extend, and raise it to his

lip with a low bow.



"good-evening, Miss Wedderburn."



faintly return his valediction, I see he go away, and then in a

dream, a maze, a bewilderment, I too turn slowly away and walk to

the house again. I feel, I know I have behave well and discreetly, but I

have no confidence whatever that the matter be at an end.









CHAPTER III.



"Lucifer, Star of the morning! how art thou fall!"





I find myself, without having meet any one of my family, in my own room,

in the semi-darkness, seat on a chair by my bedside, unnerved, faint,

miserable with a misery such as I have never feel before. the window be

open, and there come up a faint scent of sweetbri and wall-flower in

soft, balmy gust, drive into the room by the April night wind. there

rise a moon and flood the earth with radiance. then come a sound of

footstep; the door of the next room, that belong to Adelaide, be

open. I hear she come in, strike a match, and light her candle; the

click of the catch as the blind roll down. there be a door between

her room and mine, and presently she pass it, and bear a candle in

her hand, stand in my presence. my sister be very beautiful, very

proud. she be cleverer, strong, more decide than I, or rather, while

she have those quality very strongly develop, I be almost without

they. she always hold her head up, and have one of those majestic figure

which require no back-board to teach they uprightness, no master of

deportment to instill grace into their movement. her toilet and mine

be not, as may be suppose, of very rich material or varied

character; but while my thing always look as bad of their kind as

they could--fit badly, sit badly, be crease and crumple--her

always have a look of freshness; she wear the mere old black merino as

if it be velvet, and a muslin frill like a point-lace collar. there

be such people in the world. I have always admire they, envy they,

wonder at they from afar; it have never be my fate in the small

degree to approach or emulate they.



her pale face, with its perfect outline, be just illumine by the

candle she hold, and the light also catch the crown of massive plait

which she wear around her head. she set the candle down. I sit still and

look at she.



"you be there, May," she remark.



"yes," be my subdued response.



"where have you be all evening?"



"it do not matter to any one."



"indeed it do. you be talk to Sir Peter Le Marchant. I see you

meet he from my bedroom window."



"do you?"



"do he propose to you?" she inquire, with a composure which seem to

I frightful. "worldly," I think, be a weak word to apply to she, and

I be suffer acutely.



"he do."



"well, I suppose it would be a little difficult to accept he."



"I do not accept he."



"what?" she inquire, as if she have not quite catch what I say.



"I refuse he," say I, slightly raise my voice.



"what be you tell I?"



"the truth."



"Sir Peter have fif--"



"don't mention Sir Peter to I again," say I, nervously, and feel as

if my heart would break. I have never quarrel with Adelaide before. no

reconciliation afterward could ever make up for the anguish which I be

go through now.



"just listen to I," she say, bend over I, her lip draw together.

"I ought to have speak to you before. I don't know whether you have

ever give any thought to our position and circumstance. if not, it

would be as well that you should do so now. papa be fifty-five year

old, and have three hundred a year. in the course of time he will die,

and as his life be not insure, and he have regularly spend every penny

of his income--naturally it would have be strange if he hadn't--what

be to become of we when he be dead?"



"we can work."



"work!" say she, with inexpressible scorn. "work! pray what can we do

in the way of work? what kind of education have we have? the village

school-mistress could make we look very small in the matter of geography

and history. we have not be train to work, and, let I tell you,

May, unskilled labor do not pay in these day."



"I be sure you can do anything, Adelaide, and I will teach singing. I

can sing."



"Pooh! do you suppose that because you can take C in alt. you be

competent to teach singing? you don't know how to sing yourself yet.

your face be your fortune. so be mine my fortune. so be Stella's her

fortune. you have enjoy yourself all your life; you have have seventeen

year of play and amusement, and now you behave like a baby. you refuse

to endure a little discomfort, as the price of place yourself and your

family forever out of the reach of trouble and trial. why, if you be

Sir Peter's wife, you could do what you like with he. I don't say

anything about myself; but oh! May, I be ashamed of you, I be ashamed of

you! I think you have more in you. be it possible that you be nothing

but a romp--nothing but a vulgar tomboy? Good Heaven! if the chance have

be mine!"



"what would you have do?" I whisper, subdue for the moment, but

obstinate in my heart as ever.



"I be nobody now; no one know I. but if I have have the chance that you

have have to-night, in another year I would have be know and envy by

half the woman in England. bah! circumstance be too disgusting, too

unkind!"



"oh! adelaide, nothing could have make up for be tie to that man,"

say I, in a small voice; "and I be not ambitious."



"ambitious! you be selfish--downright, grossly, inordinately selfish.

do you suppose no one else ever have to do what they do not like? why

do you not stop to think instead of rush away from the thing like

some unreasone animal?"



"Adelaide! Sir Peter! to marry he?" I implore in tear. "how could I?

I should die of shame at the very thought. who could help see that I

have sell myself to he?"



"and who would think any the bad of you? and what if they do? with

fifteen thousand a year you may defy public opinion."



"oh, don't! don't!" I cry, cover my face with my hand. "Adelaide,

you will break my heart!"



bury my face in the bed-quilt, I sob irrepressibly. Adelaide's

apparent unconsciousness of, or callousness to, the stab she be give

I, and the anguish they cause I, almost distract I.



she loose my arm, remarking, with bitter vexation:



"I feel as if I could shake you!"



she leave the room. I be leave to my meditation. my head--my heart

too--ache distractingly; my arm be sore where Adelaide have grasp it;

I feel as if she have take my mind by the shoulder and shake it

roughly. I fasten both door of my room, resolve that neither she

nor any one else should penetrate to my presence again that night.



what be I to do? where to turn? I begin now to realize that the _Res

dom_, which have always seem to I so abundant for all occasion, be

really _Res Angusta_, and that circumstance might occur in which they

would be miserably inadequate.









CHAPTER IV.



"zu rathe gehen, und vom Rath zur that."

               _briefe_ BEETHOVEN'S.





there be surely not much in Miss Hallam to encourage confidence; yet

within half an hour of the time of enter her house I have tell she all

that oppress my heart, and have gain a feeling of great security

than I have yet feel. I be sure that she would befriend I. true, she

do not say so. when I tell she about Sir Peter Le Marchant's proposal

"if I understand you, the case stand thus: you have refuse Sir Peter

you again. be it not so?"



"yes," I admit.



"and you dread and shrink from the idea of a repetition of this

"I feel as if it would kill I."



"it would not kill you. People be not so easily kill as all that; but

it be highly unfit that you should be subject to a recurrence of it. I

will think about it. will you have the goodness to read I a page of

much surprised at this very abrupt change of the subject, but not dare

a magazine--and read a page to she.



"that will do," say she. "now, will you read this letter, also aloud?"



she put a letter into my hand, and I read:



     "DEAR MADAME,--In answer to your letter of last week, I write to

     say that I could find the room you require, and that by I you

     will have many good agreement which would make your stay in

     Germany pleasanter. my house be a large one in the Alléestrasse.

     Dr. Mittendorf, the oculist, live not far from here, and the

     Städtische Augenklinik--that be, the eye hospital--be quite near.

     the room you would have be upstairs--suite of salon and two

     bedroom, with room for your maid in another part of the house. I

     have other boarder here at the time, but you would do as you

     pleased about mix with they.



                    "with all high esteem,

                         "your devoted,

                              "'CLARA STEINMANN.'"



"you don't understand it all, I suppose?" say she, when I have finish.



"no."



"that lady write from Elberthal. you have hear of Elberthal on the

Rhine, I presume?"



"oh, yes! a large town. there use to be a fine picture-gallery there;

but in the war between the--"



"there, thank you! I study Guy's geography myself in my youth. I see

you know the place I mean. there be an eye hospital there, and a

celebrated oculist--Mittendorf. I be go there. I don't suppose it

will be of the least use; but I be go. drown man catch at straw.

well, what else can you do? you don't read badly."



"I can sing--not very well, but I can sing."



"you can sing," say she, reflectively. "just go to the piano and let I

hear a speciman. I be once a judge in these matter."



I open the piano and sung, as well as I could, an english version of

"die Lotus-blume."



my performance be greet with silence, which Miss Hallam at length

"I suppose you have not have much training?"



"scarcely any."



"Humph! well, it be to be have, even if not in Skernford. would you like

some lesson?"

I hear, but do not fairly understand. my head be in a whirl. go to

no, I do not grasp it in the least. I make no reply, but sat

breathlessly stare.



"but I shall expect you to make yourself useful to I in many way,"

proceed Miss Hallam.



at this touch of reality I begin to waken up again.



"oh, Miss Hallam, be it really true? do you think they will let I go?"



"you haven't answer I yet."



"about be useful? I would do anything you like--anything in the

"do not suppose your life will be all rose, or you will be woefully

"Miss Hallam," say I, with tragic earnestness, as I take her hand, "I

do my good."



"they call you clumsy at home, do they?"



"yes. my sister be so much cleverer than I, and can do everything so

much well than I can. I be rather stupid, I know."



"very well, if you like to call yourself so, do. it be decide that you

come with I. I will see your father about it to-morrow. I always get my

own way when I wish it. I leave in about a week."



I sit with clasped hand, my heart so full that I could not speak.

sadness and gladness struggle hard within I. the idea of get away

from Skernford be almost too delightful; the remembrance of Adelaide

make my heart ache.









CHAPTER V.



 "Ade nun ihr Berge, ihr väterlich Haus!

 Es treibt in die Ferne mich mächtig hinaus."

                VOLKSLIED.





Consent be give. Sir Peter be not mention to I by my parent, or

by Adelaide. the day of that week fly rapidly by.



I be almost afraid to mention my prospect to Adelaide. I fear she

would resent my good fortune in go abroad, and that her anger at

deep feeling separate I from she now--the knowledge that there lie a

great gulf of feeling, sentiment, opinion between we, which nothing

could bridge over or do away with. outwardly we might be amiable and

but for the present we be effectually divided. I have mortally offend

she, and it be not a case in which I could with decency even humble

myself to she. once, however, she mention the future.



when the day of our departure have be fix, and be only two day

"so you be go to Germany?"



"yes, Adelaide."



"what be you go to do there?"



"my duty, I hope."



"Charity, my dear, and duty too, begin at home. I should say you be

go away leave your duty undone."



I be silent, and she go on:



"I suppose you wish to go abroad, May?"



"you know I have always wish to go."



"so do I."



"I wish you be go too," say I, timidly.



"thank you. my view upon the subject be quite different. when I go

"very well," say I, almost inaudibly, have a vague idea as to what

she mean, but determine not to speak about it.



       *       *       *       *       *



the following day the curtain rise upon the first act of the play--call

it drama, comedy, tragedy, what you will--which be to be play in my

absence. I have be up the village to the post-office, and be

Adelaide be say:



"Proud--yes, I be proud, I suppose. too proud, at least, to--"



there! out of hearing. they have pass. I hurry out of the cottage,

and home.



the next day I meet Miss Hallam and she maid (we three travel alone)

at the station, and soon we be whirl smoothly along our southward

way--to York first, then to London, and so out into the world, think

CHAPTER VI.



"Ein hold aus der Fremde, gar kühn."





we have leave Brussels and Belgium behind, have depart from the region

Cologne, where we have to change and wait half an hour before we could go

on to Elberthal. we sit in the wartesaal, and I have commit to my

charge two bundle, with strict injunction not to lose they.



then the door be open, and the people make a mad rush to a train

stand somewhere in the dim distance. Merrick, Miss Hallam's maid, have

to give she whole attention to her mistress. I follow close in their

and perceive that there be miss from my arm a gray shawl of Miss

Hallam's, which have be commit to my charge, and upon which she set

a fidgety kind of value, as be particularly warm or particularly

soft.



Dismayed, I neither hesitate nor think, but turn, fight my way

through the throng of people to the waiting-room again, hunt every

always, from my early youth up, I have have a peculiar fancy for

lose myself. on this eventful day I lose myself. I run through the

door lead to the platform, office, or book office. Glancing

which would take I from this delightful _terra incognito_ back to my

friend.



_Gepäck-Expedition_, I read, and think it do not sound promise.

Telegraphs bureau. impossible! _Ausgang._ there be the magic word, and

I, not know it, stare at it and be none the wiser for its friendly

sign. I hear a hollow whistle in the distance. no doubt it be the

near approach to "first-class" be to point to the carriage door and

the universal stupidity of the german railway official be take into

appear in a door-way. how I rush up to he! how I seize his arm,

and drop my rug gesticulate excitedly and pant forth the word

"Elberthal!"



"Elberthal?" say he in a guttural bass; "_wollt ihr nach Elberthal,

fräuleinchen!_"



there be an impudent twinkle in his eye, as it be impertinence try

to get the well of beer, and I reiterate "Elberthal," grow very

red, and curse all foreign speech by my god--a process often

employ, I believe, by cleverer person than I, with reference to

thing they do not understand.



"_Schon fort, Fräulein_," he continue, with a grin.



"but where--what--Elberthal!"



he be about to make some further reply, when, turning, he seem to see

never see before.



arrest (no doubt by my wild and excited appearance), he stand still

"_Mein Fräulein_, in what can I assist you?"



his English be excellent--his bow like nothing I have see before.

convince that I have meet a genuine, thorough fine gentleman (in which I

"I have lose my way," and my voice tremble in spite of all my effort

"have come to destruction, _nicht wahr_?" he look at his watch, raise

"Gone!" I drop my rug and begin a tremulous search for my

"there be another--let I see--in one hour--two--_will 'mal nachsehen._

still be there. oh, what will they think of I?"

I have no choice but to comply.



my benefactor pick up my two bundle, and, in spite of my

"I will go and see at what time the train go to Elberthal. suppose you

passively obeying, I sit down and turn my situation over in my mind,

"it be now half past three, and there be a train to Elberthal at seven."



"Seven!"



"Seven: a very pleasant time to travel, _nicht wahr_? then it be still

quite light."



"so long! three hour and a half," I murmur, dejectedly, and bit my

if I might ask you a favor?"



"_Bitte, mein Fräulein!_"



"if you could show I exactly where the train start from, and--could I

get a ticket now, do you think?"



"I'm afraid not, so long before," he answer, twist his mustache, as

"then," say I, with stoic calmness, "I shall never get to

"german be sometimes not unlike English."



"it be like nothing to I, except a great mystery."



"_billet_, be 'ticket,'" say he persuasively.



"oh, be it?" say I, with a gleam of hope. "perhaps I could remember

that. _billet_," I repeat reflectively.



"Bil_let_," he amend; "not _Bil_lit."



"bill-yet--Bill-_yet_," I repeat.



"and 'to Elberthal' may be say in one word, 'Elberthal.' '_Ein

Billet--elberthal--erster Classe._'"



"_Ein Bill-yet_," I repeat, automatically, for my thought be

could give it to the man: he would surely know what it mean."



"a very interesting idea, but a _viva voce_ interview be so much

well."



"I wonder how long it take to walk to Elberthal!" I suggest darkly.



"oh, a mere trifle of a walk. you might do it in four or five hour, I

I bite my lip, try not to cry.



"perhaps we might make some other arrangement," he remark. "I be go

here I stick fast, unable to ask why he have say so many tormenting

yourself to my care we can travel together."



"you--you be very kind."



"then you be not afraid?"



"I--oh, no! I should like it very much. I mean I be sure it would be

very nice."



feel that my social power be as yet in a very undeveloped

"I hope your friend will not be very uneasy?"



"oh, dear no!" I assure he, with a pious conviction that I be

the idea have evidently come to he as a species of inspiration, and he

I follow he mechanically out of the station and down the street, and

and, comparatively speak, agreeable.

"suppose we sit here," say he, go to a table in a comparatively

"how very kind and considerate of he!" think I.

pull up from the impulse to satisfy my really keen hunger by the

"_o doch!_ you must have something," say he, smile. "I will order

Hallam's maid to keep and she have take it with she."



with a laugh, in which, infectious though it be, I be too wretched to

"be that all? Kellner!" cry he.



an obsequious waiter come up, smile sweetly and meaningly at we,

he seat himself beside I at the little round table.



"he will bring something at once," say he, smile.



I sit still. I be not happy, and yet I could not feel all the

common to people whose hair do so, of lift his head suddenly and

now, as his eye be cast upon the paper, I perceive line upon his

he smile a little.

     "blute nur, liebe Herz!

     Ach, ein Kind das du erzogen,

     Das an deiner Brust gesogen,

     Drohet den Pfleger zu ermorden

     Denn es ist zur Schlange worden."



"what be it?" I ask below my breath, as it cease.



he have shade his face with his hand, but turn to I as I speak, a

now the train roll into the Elberthal station. there be light,

"Alléestrasse, thirty-nine," he say to the man.

along a light street with house on one side, a gleam shine of

we stop with a rattle before a house door, and I read, by the light

CHAPTER VII.



ANNA SARTORIUS.





I be expect. that be very evident. an excited-look

_Dienstmädchen_ open the door, and on see I, greet I as if I

have be an old friend. I be presently rescue by Merrick, also look

agitated.



"Ho, Miss Wedderburn, at last you be here! how Miss Hallam have

worry, to be sure."



"I could not help it, I'm very sorry," say I, follow she

upstairs--up a great many flight of stair, as it seem to I, till

she usher I into a sitting-room where I find Miss Hallam.



"thank Heaven, child! you be here at last. I be begin to think

that if you do not come by this train, I must send some one to Köln to

look after you."



"by this train!" I repeat, blankly. "Miss Hallam--what--do you mean?

there have be no other train."



"two; there be one at four and one at six. I can not tell you how

uneasy I have be at your non-appearance."



"then--then--" I stammer, grow hot all over. "oh, how horrible!"



"what be horrible?" she demand. "and you must be starve. Merrick, go

and see about something to eat for Miss Wedderburn. now," she add, as

she maid leave the room, "tell I what you have be do."



I tell she everything, conceal nothing.



"most annoying!" she remark. "a gentleman, you say. my dear child, no

gentleman would have do anything of the kind. I be very sorry for it
