"Miss Hallam," I implore, almost in tear, "please do not tell any one

what have happen to I. I will never be such a fool again. I know

now--and you may trust I. but do not let any one know how--stupid I

have be. I tell you I be stupid--I tell you several time. I be sure

you must remember."



"oh, yes, I remember. we will say no more about it."



"and the gray shawl," say I.



"Merrick have it."



I lift my hand and shrug my shoulder. "just my luck," I murmur,

resignedly, as Merrick come in with a tray.



Miss Hallam, I notice, continue to regard I now and then as I eat

with but small appetite. I be too excited by what have pass, and by

what I have just hear, to be hungry. I think it kind, merciful, humane

in she to promise to keep my secret and not expose my ignorance and

stupidity to stranger.



"it be evident," she remark, "that you must at once begin to learn

German, and then if you do get lose at a railway station again, you will

be able to ask your way."



Merrick shake her head with an inexpressibly bitter smile.



"I'd defy any one to learn this 'ere language, ma'am. they call an

accident a _Unglück_; if any one could tell I what that mean, I'd

thank they, that's all."



"don't express your opinion, Merrick, unless you wish to seem

deficient in understanding; but go and see that Miss Wedderburn have

everything she want--or rather everything that can be get--in her room.

she be tired, and shall go to bed."



I be only too glad to comply with this mandate, but it be long ere I

sleep. I keep hear the organ in the cathedral, and that voice of the

invisible singer--see the face beside I, and hear the word,

"then you have decide that I be to be trust?"



"and he be deceive I all the time!" I think, mournfully.



I breakfast by myself the follow morning, in a room call the

speisesaal. I find I be late. when I come into the room, about nine

o'clock, there be no one but myself to be see. there be a long table

with a white cloth upon it, and row of the thick cup and saucer it

have ever be my fate to see, with distinct evidence that the chief

part of the company have already breakfast. basket full of _brödchen_

and pot of butter, a long India-rubber pipe come from the gas to

light a theemaschine--lot of cane-bottom chair, an open piano, two

cage with canary in they; the kettle gently simmer above the

gas-flame; for the rest, silence and solitude.



I sit down, having find a clean cup and plate, and glance timidly at

the theemaschine, not dare to cope with its mystery, until my doubt

be relieve by the entrance of a young person with a trim little

figure, a coquettishly cut and elaborately braided apron, and a white

frille morgenhaube upon her hair, surmount her round,

heavenward-aspire visage.



"_guten morgen, Fräulein_," she say, as she march up to the darkly

mysterious theemaschine and begin deftly to prepare coffee for I, and

to push the Brödchen toward I. she begin to talk to I in break

English, which be very pretty, and while I eat and drank, she

industriously scrape little white root at the same table. she tell I

she be Clara, the niece of Frau Steinmann, and that she be very glad

to see I, but be very sorry I have have so long to wait in köln

yesterday. she like my dress, and be it _echt Englisch_--also, how

much do it cost?



she be a cheery little person, and I like she. she seem to like I

too, and repeatedly say she be glad I have come. she like dance she

say. do I? and she have lately dance at a ball with some one who

dance so well--_aber_, quite indescribably well. his name be Karl

Linders, and he be, _ach!_ really a remarkable person. a bright blush,

and a little sigh accompany the remark. our eye meet, and from that

moment Clara and I be very good friend.



I go upstairs again, and find that Miss Hallam propose, during the

forenoon, to go and find the Eye Hospital, where she be to see the

oculist, and arrange for he to visit she, and shortly after eleven we

set out.



the street that I have so dimly see the night before, show itself by

daylight to be a fair, broad way. down the middle, after the pleasant

fashion of continental town, be a broad walk, plant with two double

row of linden, and on either side this lindenallee be the carriage

road, private house, shop, exhibition, boarding-house. in the

middle, exactly opposite our dwelling, be the New Theater, just draw

to the close of its first season. I look at it without think much

about it. I have never be in a theater in my life, and the name be but

a name to I.



turn off from the pretty allee, and from the green Hofgarten which

bound it at one end, we enter a narrow, ill-paved street, the aspect

of whose gutter and inhabitant alike excite my lively disgust. in

this street be the Eye Hospital, as be presently testify to we by a

board bear the inscription, "Städtische Augenklinik."



we be take to a dimly light room in which many people be wait,

some with bandage over their eye, other with all kind of

extraordinary spectacle on, which make they look like phantom out of a

bad dream--nearly all more or less blind, and the effect be

surprisingly depressing.



presently Miss Hallam and Merrick be admit to an inner room, and I

be leave to await their return. my eye stray over the different face,

and I feel a sensation of relief when I see some one come in without

either bandage or spectacle. the new-comer be a young man of middle

height, and of proportion slight without be thin. there be nothing

the matter with his eye, unless perhaps a slight short-sightedness; he

have, I think, one of the gentle, most attractive face I have ever

see; boyishly open and innocent at the first glance; at the second,

indue with a certain reticent calm and intellectual radiance which take

away from the first youthfulness of his appearance. soft, yet luminous

brown eye, loose brown hair hang round his face, a certain manner

which for I at least have a charm, be the characteristic of this

young man. he carry a violin-case, remove his hat as he come in, and

be see by one of the young man who sit at desk, take name down,

and attend to people in general, be call by he:



"Herr Helfen--Herr Friedhelm Helfen!"



"_ja--hier!_" he answer, go up to the desk, upon which there ensue

a lively conversation, though carry on in a low tone, after which the

young man at the desk present a white card to "Herr Friedhelm Helfen,"

and the latter, with a pleasant "Adieu," go out of the room again.



Miss Hallam and Merrick presently return from the consulting-room, and

we go out of the dark room into the street, which be fill with

spring sunshine and warmth; a contrast something like that between Miss

Hallam's life and my own, I have think since. far before we, hurry

on, I see the young man with the violin-case; he turn off by the

theater, and go in at a side door.



an hour's wandering in the Hofgarten--my first view of the Rhine--a

dull, flat stream it look, too. I have see it since then in mighty

flow. then we come home, and it be decide that we should dine together

with the rest of the company at one o'clock.



a bell ring at a few minute past one. we go down-stair, into the

room in which I have already breakfast, which, in general, be know as

the saal. as I enter with Miss Hallam I be conscious that a knot of

lad or young man stand aside to let we pass, and then giggle and

scuffle behind the door before follow we into the saal.



two or three lady be already seat, and an exceedingly stout lady

ladle out soup at a side table, while Clara and a servant-woman carry

the plate round to the different place. the stout lady turn as she

see we, and greet we. she be Frau Steinmann, our hostess. she wait

until the youth before speak of have come in, and with a great deal of

noise have seat themselves, when she begin, aid by the soup-ladle,

to introduce we all to each other.



we, it seem, be to have the honor and privilege of be the only

english lady of the company. we be introduce to one or two other,

and I be assign a place by a lady introduce as Fräulein Anna

Sartorius, a brunette, rather stout, with large dark eye which look

at I in a way I do not like, a head of curly black hair crop short,

an odd, brusque manner, and a something peculiar, or, as she say,

_selten_ in her dress. this young lady sustain the introduction with

self-possession and calm. it be otherwise with the young gentleman, who

appear decidedly mixed. there be some half dozen of they in all--a

couple of English, the rest german, Dutch, and Swedish. I have never be

in company with so many nationality before, and be impressed with my

situation--needlessly so.



all these young gentleman make bow which be, in their respective

way, triumph of awkwardness, with the exception of one of our

compatriot, who appear to believe that himself and his manner be

form to charm and subdue the opposite sex. we then sit down, and

Fräulein Sartorius immediately open a conversation with I.



"_Sprechen Sie Deutsch, Fräulein?_" be her first venture, and having

receive my admission that I do not speak a word of it, she continue,

in good English:



"now I can talk to you without offend you. it be so dreadful when

english people who don't know german persist in think that they do.

there be an english-woman here who always say _wer_ when she mean

where, and _will_ when she mean who. she say the sound confuse she."



the boy giggle at this, but the joke be lose upon I.



"what be your name?" she continue; "I don't catch what Frau Steinmann

say."



"May Wedderburn," I reply, angry with myself for blush so

excessively as I see that all the boy hold their spoon suspend,

listen for my answer.



"May--_das heisst Mai_," say she, turn to the assemble youth, who

testify that they be aware of it, and the dutch boy, Brinks,

inquire, gutturally:



"you haf one zong in your language what call itself, 'not always Mai,'

haf you not?"



"yes," say I, and all the boy begin to giggle as if something clever

have be say. take all in all, what torture have I not suffer from

those dreadful boy. Shy when they ought to have be bold, and bold

where a modest retiringness would well have become they. giggle

inanely at everything and nothing. noisy and vociferous among themselves

or with inferior; shy, awkward and blush with lady or in refined

society--distress my feeble effort to talk to they by their silly

explosion of laughter when one of they be address. they form the

bane of my life for some time.



"will you let I paint you?" say Fräulein Sartorius, whose big eye have

be survey I in a manner that make I nervous.



"paint I?"



"your likeness, I mean. you be very pretty, and we never see that color

of hair here."



"be you a painter?"



"no, I'm only a _Studentin_ yet; but I paint from model. well, will you

sit to I?"



"oh, I don't know. if I have time, perhaps."



"what will you do to make you not have time?"



I do not feel disposed to gratify her curiosity, and say I do not

know yet what I should do.



for a short time she ask no more question, then



"do you like town or country well?"



"I don't know. I have never live in a town."



"do you like amusement--concert, and theater, and opera?"



"I don't know," I be reluctantly oblige to confess, for I see that the

assemble youth, though not look at I openly, and apparently

entirely engross with their dinner, be listen attentively to

what pass.



"you don't know," repeat Fräulein Sartorius, quickly see through my

thin assumption of indifference, and proceed to draw I out as much

as possible. I wish Adelaide have be there to beat she from the

field. she would have do it well than I could.



"No; because I have never be to any."



"haven't you? how odd! how very odd! ben't it strange?" she add,

appeal to the boy. "Fräulein have never be to a theater or a

concert."



I disdain to remark that my word be be pervert, but the game

instinct rise in I. raise my voice a little, I remark:



"it be evident that I have not enjoy your advantage, but I trust that

the gentleman" (with a bow to the listening boy) "will make allowance

for the difference between we."



the young gentleman burst into a chorus of delighted giggle, and Anna,

shoot a rapid glance at I, make a slight grimace, but look not at

all displeased. I be, though, mightily; but, elate with victory, I

turn to my compatriot at the other end of the table, and ask he at

what time of the year Elberthal be pleasant.



"oh," say he, "it's always pleasant to I, but that's owe to myself.

I make it so."



just then, several of the other lad rise, push their chair back

with a great clatter, bow to the assemble company, and say

"Gesegnete Mahlzeit!" as they go out.



"why be they go, and what do they say?" I inquire of Miss

Sartorius, who reply, quite amiably:



"they be student at the Realschule. they have to be there at two

o'clock, and they say, 'blessed be the meal-time,' as they go out."



"do they? how nice!" I could not help say.



"would you like to go for a walk this afternoon?" say she.



"oh, very much!" I have exclaim, before I remember that I do not

like she, and do not intend to like she. "if Miss Hallam can spare I,"

I add.



"oh, I think she will. I shall be ready at half past two; then we shall

return for coffee at four. I will knock at your door at the time."



on consult Miss Hallam after dinner, I find she be quite willing

for I to go out with Anna, and at the time appoint we set out.



Anna take I a tour round the town, show I the lion, and give I

topographical detail. she show I the big, plain barrack, and the

desert waste of the Exerzierplatz spread before it. she do she good

to entertain I, and I, with a childish prejudice against her abrupt

manner, and the free, somewhat challenging look of her black eye, be

reserve, unresponsive, stupid. I take a prejudice against she--I own

it--and for that and other sin commit against a woman who would have

be my friend if I would have let she, I say humbly, _Mea culpa!_



"it seem a dull kind of a place," say I.



"it need not be. you have advantage here which you cann't get

everywhere. I have be here several year, and as I have no other home

I rather think I shall live here."



"oh, indeed."



"you have a home, I suppose?"



"of course."



"brother and sister?"



"two sister," I reply, mightily ruffle by what I choose to consider

her curiosity and impertinence; though, when I look at she, I see what

I could not but confess to be a real, and not unkind, interest in she

plain face and big eye.



"ah! I have no brother and sister. I have only a little house in the

country, and as I have always live in a town, I don't care for the

country. it be so lonely. the people be so stupid too--not always

though. you be offend with I at dinner, _nicht wahr_?"



"oh, dear, no!" say I, very awkwardly and very untruly. the truth be,

I do not like she, and be too young, too ignorant and _gauche_ to try

to smooth over my dislike. I do not know the pain I be give, and if

I have, should perhaps not have behave differently.



"_Doch!_" she say, smile. "but I do not know what a child you be,

or I should have let you alone."



more offended than ever, I maintain silence. if I be certainly

touchy and ill to please, Fräulein Sartorius, it must be own, do not

know how to apologize gracefully. I have since, with wide knowledge of

her country and its man and woman, get to see that what make she so

inharmonious be, that she have a woman's form and a man's disposition

and love of freedom. as her countrywoman take in the gross be the most

utterly "in bond" of any woman in Europe, this spoil her life in a

manner which can not be understand here, where woman in comparison be

free as air, and give no little of the brusqueness and roughness to her

manner. in an enlighten English home she would have be an

admirable, firm, clever woman; here she be that most dreadful of all

abnormal growth--a woman with a will of her own.



"what do they do here?" I inquire, indifferently.



"oh, many thing. though it be not a large town there be a School of

Art, which bring many painter here. there be a hundred and

fifty--besides student."



"and you be a student?"



"yes. one must have something to do--some _carrière_--though my

countrywoman say not. I shall go away for a few month soon, but I be

wait for the last great concert. it will be the 'Paradise Lost' of

Rubinstein."



"ah, yes!" say I, politely, but without interest. I have never hear of

Rubinstein and the "Verlorenes Paradies." before the furor of 1876, how

many score of provincial English have?



"there be very much music here," she continue. "be you fond of it?"



"ye-es. I cann't play much, but I can sing. I have come here partly to

take singing lesson."



"so!"



"who be the good teacher?" be my next ingenuous question.



she laugh.



"that depend upon what you want to learn. there be so many: violin,

_clavi_, that be piano, flute, 'cello, everything."



"oh!" I reply, and ask no more question about music; but inquire

if it be pleasant at Frau Steinmann's.



she shrug her shoulder.



"be it pleasant anywhere? I don't find many place pleasant, because I

can not be a humbug, so other do not like I. but I believe some people

like Elberthal very well. there be the theater--that make another

element. and there be the soldier and _Kaufleute_--merchants, I mean,

so you see there be variety, though it be a small place."



"ah, yes!" say I, look about I as we pass down a very busy

street, and I glance to right and leave with the image of Eugen

Courvoisier ever distinctly if unconfessedly present to my mental view.

do he live at Elberthal? and if so, do he belong to any of those

various calling? what be he? an artist who paint picture for his

bread? I think that very probable. there be something free and

artist-like in his manner, in his loose wave hair and in his keen

susceptibility to beauty. I think of his emotion at hear that

glorious Bach music. or be he a musician--what Anna Sartorius call

_ein Musiker_? but no. my idea of musician be somewhat hazy, not to

say utterly chaotic; they embrace only two class: those who perform

or give lesson, and those who compose. I have never form to myself

the faint idea of a composer, and my experience of teacher and

performer be limit to one speciman--Mr. Smythe, of Darton, whose

method and performance would, as I have since learn, have make the

hair of a musician stand horrent on end. no--I do not think he be a

musician. an actor? Perish the thought, be my inevitable mental answer.

how should I be able to make any well one? a soldier, then? at that

moment we meet a mount captain of Uhlans, harness clanking,

accouterment rattle. he be apparently an acquaintance of my

companion, for he salute with a grave politeness which sit well upon

he. decidedly Eugen Courvoisier have the air of a soldier. that

account for all. no doubt he be a soldier. in my ignorance of the

strictness of german military regulation as regard the wearing of

uniform, I overlook the fact that he have be in civilian's dress, and

remain delighted with my new idea; Captain Courvoisier. "what be the

German for captain?" I inquire, abruptly.



"_Hauptmann._"



"thank you." Hauptmann Eugen Courvoisier--a noble and a gallant title,

and one which become he. "how much be a thaler?" be my next question.



"it be as much as three shilling in your money."



"oh, thank you," say I, and do a little sum in my own mind. at that

rate then, I owe Herr Courvoisier the sum of ten shilling. how glad I

be to find it come within my mean.



as I take off my thing, I wonder when Herr Courvoisier would "make

out his account." I trust soon.









CHAPTER VIII.



"probe zum verlorenen Paradiese."





Miss Hallam fulfil her promise with regard to my singing lesson. she

have a conversation with Fräulein Sartorius, to whom, unpopular as she

be, I notice people constantly and almost instinctively go when in

need of precise information or a slight dose of common sense and

clear-headedness.



Miss Hallam inquire who be the good master.



"for singing, the Herr Direktor," reply Anna, very promptly. "and then

he direct the good of the musical verein--the club--society,

whatever you name they. at least he might try Miss Wedderburn's voice."



"who be he?"



"the head of anything belong to music in the town--königlicher

musik-direktor. he conduct all the great concert, and though he do

not sing himself, yet he be one of the good teacher in the province.

lot of people come and stay here on purpose to learn from he."



"and what be these verein?"



"every season there be six great concert give, and a seventh for the

benefit of the direktor. the orchestra and chorus together be call a

verein--musik-verein. the chorus be chiefly compose of lady and

gentleman--amateur, you know--_Dilettanten_. the Herr Direktor be very

particular about voice. you pay so much for admission, and receive a

card for the season. then you have all the good teaching--the _Proben_."



"what be a _probe_?" I demand, hastily, remember that Courvoisier

have use the word.



"what you call a rehearsal."



ah! then he be musical. at last I have find it out. perhaps he be one

of the amateur who sing at these concert, and if so, I might see he

again, and if so--but Anna go on:



"it be a very good thing for any one, particularly with such a teacher

as von Francius."



"you must join," say Miss Hallam to I.



"there be a probe to-night to Rubinstein's 'Paradise Lost,'" say Anna.

"I shall go, not to sing, but to listen. I can take Miss Wedderburn, if

you like, and introduce she to Herr von Francius, whom I know."



"very nice! very much oblige to you. certainly," say Miss Hallam.



the probe be fix for seven, and shortly after that time we set off

for the Tonhalle, or concert-hall, in which it be hold.



"we shall be much too early," say she. "but the people be shamefully

late. Most of they only come to _klatsch_, and flirt, or try to flirt,

with the Herr Direktor."



this throw upon my mind a new light as to the Herr Direktor, and I

walk by her side much impress. she tell I that if I accept I

might even sing in the concert itself, as there have only be four

proben so far, and there be still several before the haupt-probe.



"what be the haupt-probe?" I inquire.



"General rehearsal--when Herr von Francius be most unmerciful to his

stupid pupil. I always attend that. I like to hear he make sport of

they, and then the instrumentalist laugh at they. Von Francius never

flatter."



inspire with nightmare-like idea as to this terrible haupt-probe, I

find myself, with Anna, turn into a low-fronted building inscribe

"Städtische Tonhalle," the concert-hall of the good town of Elberthal.



"this way," say she. "it be in the rittersaal. we don't go to the large

saal till the haupt-probe."



I follow she into a long, rather shabby-look room, at one end of

which be a low orchestra, about which be dot the desk of the

absent instrumentalist, and some stiff-look Celli and Contrabassi

keep watch from a wall. on the orchestra be already assemble a goodly

number of young man and woman, all in lively conversation, loud

laughter, and apparently high good-humor with themselves and everything

in the world.



a young man with a fuzz of hair stand off about a sad and

depressed-look countenance be steal "in and out and round about,"

and distribute sheet of score to the company. in the conductor's

place be a tall man in gray clothe, who lean negligently against the

rail, and hold a conversation with a pretty young lady who seem much

pleased with his attention. it do not strike I at first that this be

the terrible direktor of whom I have be hear. he be young, have a

slender, graceful figure, and an exceedingly handsome, though (I

think at first) an unpleasing face. there be something in his

attitude and manner which at first I do not quite like. Anna walk up

the room, and pause before the estrade, say:



"Herr Direktor!"



he turn: his eye fall upon her face, and leave it instantly to look at

mine. gather himself together into a more ceremonious attitude, he

descend from his estrade, and stand beside we, a little to one side,

look at we with a leisurely calmness which make I feel, I know not

why, uncomfortable. meanwhile, Anna take up her parable.



"may I introduce the young lady? Miss Wedderburn, Herr Musik-Direktor

von Francius. Miss Wedderburn wish to join the verein, if you think

her voice will pass. perhaps you will allow she to sing to-night?"



"certainly, _mein Fräulein_," say he to I, not to Anna. he have a long,

rather jewish-look face, black hair, eye, and mustache. the feature

be thin, fine, and point. the thing which most strike I then, at

any rate, be a certain expression which, conquer all other,

dominate they--at once a hardness and a hardihood which impress I

disagreeably then, though I afterward learn, in know the man, to

know much more truly the real meaning of that unflinche gaze and iron

look.



"your voice be what, _mein Fräulein_?" he ask.



"Soprano."



"Sopran? we will see. the soprano sit over there, if you will have the

goodness."



he point to the left of the orchestra, and call out to the

melancholy-looking young man, "Herr Schonfeld, a chair for the young

lady!"



Herr von Francius then ascend the orchestra himself, go to the

piano, and, after a few direction, give we the signal to begin. till

that day--I confess it with shame--I have never hear of the "Verlorenes

Paradies." it come upon I like a revelation. I sing my good,

substitute _do_, _re_, _mi_, etc., for the german word. once or

twice, as Herr von Francius's forefinger beat time, I think I see his

head turn a little in our direction, but I scarcely heed it. when the

first chorus be over, he turn to I:



"you have not sing in a chorus before?"



"no."



"so! I should like to hear you sing something _sola_." he push toward

I a pile of music, and while the other stand look on and whispering

among themselves, he go on, "those be all sopran song. select one,

if you please, and try it."



not at all aware that the incident be consider unprecedented, and be

create a sensation, I turn over the music, seek something I know,

but could find nothing. all in German, and all strange. suddenly I come

upon one entitle "Blute nur, liebe Herz," the sopran solo which I have

hear as I sit with Courvoisier in the cathedral. it seem almost like

an old friend. I open it, and find it have also english word. that

decide I.



"I will try this," say I, show it to he.



he smile. "_'S ist gut!_" then he read the title off the song aloud,

and there be a general titter, as if some very great joke be in

agitation, and be much appreciated. indeed I find that in general

the joke of the Herr Direktor, when he condescend to make any, be

very keenly relish by at least the lady part of his pupil.



not understand the reason of the titter I take the music in my hand,

and wait for a moment until he give I the signal, sing it after the

well wise I could--not very brilliantly, I dare say, but with at least

all my heart pour into it. I have one requisite at least of an artist

nature--I could abstract myself upon occasion completely from my

surrounding. I do so now. it be too beautiful, too grand. I

remember that afternoon at Köln--the golden sunshine stream through

the paint window, the flood of melody pour forth by the invisible

singer; above all, I remember who have be by my side, and I feel as

if again beside he--again influence by the unusual beauty of his face

and mien, and by his clear, strange, command eye. it all come back

to I--the strangest, happy day of my life. I sing as I have never

sing before--as I have not know I could sing.



when I stop, the tittering have cease; silence salute I. the young

lady be all look at I; some of they have put on their

eye-glass; other stare at I as if I be some strange animal from a

menagerie. the young gentleman be whisper among themselves and

take sidelong glance at I. I scarcely heed anything of it. I fix

my eye upon the judge who have be listen to my performance--upon

von Francius. he be pull his mustache and at first make no remark.



"you have sing that song before, _gnädiges Fräulein_?"



"no. I have hear it once. I have not see the music before."



"so!" he bow slightly, and turn once more to the other, say:



"we will begin the next chorus. 'Chorus of the Damned,' now, _meine

Herrschaften_, I would wish to impress upon you one thing, if I can,

that be--silence, _meine Herren_!" he call sharply toward the tenor,

who be giggle inanely among themselves. "a chorus of damned soul,"

he proceed, composedly, "would not sing in the same unruffled manner

as a young lady who warble, 'spring be come--tra, la, la! spring be

come--lira, lira!' in her mamma's drawing-room. try to imagine yourself

struggle in the torture of hell"--(a delight giggle and a sort of

"oh, you dear, wicked man!" expression on the part of the young lady;

a nudging of each other on that of the young gentleman), "and sing as if

you be damned."



Scarcely any one seem to take the matter the least earnestly. the

young lady continue to giggle, and the young gentleman to nudge each

other. little enough of expression, if plenty of noise, be there in

that magnificent and truly difficult passage, the change chorus of

the condemn and the blessed one--with its crowning "WEH!" thunder

down from high soprano to deepest bass.



"lot of noise, and no meaning," observe the conductor, lean himself

against the rail of the estrade, face to his audience, fold his arm

and survey they all one after the other with cold self-possession. it

strike I that he despise they while he condescend to instruct they.

the power of the man strike I again. I begin to like he well. at

least I venerate his thorough understanding of what be to I a

splendid mystery. no softening appear in the master's eye in answer

to the row of pretty appealing face turn to he; no smile upon his

contemptuous lip respond to the eye--black, brown, gray, blue,

yellow--all turn with such affect devotion to his own. compose

himself to an insouciant attitude, he begin in a cool, indifferent

voice, which have, however, certain caustic tone in it which sting I

at least to the quick:



"I never hear anything bad, even from you. my honored Fräulein, my

_gnädigen Herren_, just try once to imagine what you be singe about!

it be not an exercise--it be not a love song, either of which you would

no doubt perform excellently. conceive what be happen! put yourself

back into those mythical time. believe, for this evening, in the story

of the forfeit Paradise. there be strife between the blessed and the

Damned; the obedient and the disobedient. there be thick cloud in the

heaven--smoke, fire, and sulphur--a clashing of sword in the serrie

rank of the angel: can not you see Michael, Gabriel, Raphael, lead

the heavenly host? can not some of you sympathize a little with Satan

and his struggle?"



look at he, I think they must indeed be an unimaginative set! in

that dark face before they be Mephistopheles at least--_der Geist der

stet verneint_--if nothing more violent. his cool, scornful feature

be light up with some of the excitement which he could not drill

into the assemblage before he. have he be gift with the requisite

organ he would have act and sing the chief character in "Faust" _con

amore_.



"_Ach, um Gotteswillen!_" he go on, shrug his shoulder, "try to

forget what you be! try to forget that none of you ever have a wicked

thought or an unholy aspiration--"



("don't they see how he be laugh at they?" I wonder.)



"you, Chorus of the Condemned, try to conjure up every wicked thought

you can, and let it come out in your voice--you who sing the strain of

the blessed one, think of what blessedness be. surely each of you have

his own idea! some of you may agree with Lenore:



     "'bei ihm, bei ihm ist Seligkeit,

     und ohne Wilhelm Holle!'



"if so, think of he; think of she--only sing it, whatever it be.

remember the strong of feeling:



     "'die Engel nennen es Himmelsfreude

     Die Teufel nennen es Höllenqual,

     die Menschen nennen es--LIEBE!'



"and sing it!"



he have not become loud or excited in voice or gesticulation, but his

word, fling at they like so many scornful little bullet, the

indifferent resignation of his attitude, have their effect upon the crew

of giggle, simper girl and awkward, self-conscious young man. some

idea seem vouchsafed to they that perhaps their performance have not

be quite all that it might have be; they begin in a little more

earnest, and the chorus go well.



for my own part, I be deeply move. a vague excitement, a wild, and not

altogether a holy one, have steal over I. I understand now how the man

might have influence. I bent to the power of his will, which reach I

where I stand in the background, from his dark eye, which turn for a

moment to I now and then. it be that will of his which put I as it

be suddenly into the spirit of the music, and reveal I depth in my

own heart at which I have never even guess. excited, with cheek

burning and my heart hot within I, I follow his word and his

gesture, and grow so impatient of the dull stupidity of the other that

tear come to my eye. how could that young woman, in the midst of a

sublime chorus, deliberately pause, arrange the knot of her neck-tie,

and then, after a smile and a side glance at the conductor, go on again

with a more self-satisfy simper than ever upon her lip? what might

not the thing be with a whole chorus of sympathetic singer? the very

dullness which in face prevail reveal to I great region of

possible splendor, almost too vast to think of.



at last it be over. I turn to the direktor, who be still near the

piano, and ask timidly:



"do you think I may join? will my voice do?"



an odd expression cross his face; he answer, dryly:



"you may join the verein, _mein Fräulein_--yes. please come this way

with I. Pardon, Fräulein Stockhausen--another time. I be sorry to say I

have business at present."



a black look from a pretty brunette, who have advance with an engage

smile and an open score to ask he some question, greet this very

compose rebuff of her advance. the black look be direct at

I--guiltless.



without take any notice of the other, he lead Anna and I to a small

inner room, where there be a desk and write material.



"your name, if you will be good enough?"



"Wedderburn."



"your _vorname_, though--your first name."



"my christian name--oh, May."



"M--a--_na_! perhaps you will be so good as to write it yourself, and

the street and number of the house in which you live."



I comply.



"have you be here long?"



"not quite a week."



"do you intend to make any stay?"



"some month, probably."



"Humph! if you wish to make any progress in music, you must stay much

long."



"it--I--it depend upon other people how long I remain."



he smile slightly, and his smile be not unpleasant; it light up the

darkness of his face in an agreeable manner.



"so I should suppose. I will call upon you to-morrow at four in the

afternoon. I should like to have a little conversation with you about

your voice. Adieu, _meine Damen_."



with a slight bow which sufficiently dismiss we, he turn to the desk

again, and we go away.



our homeward walk be a somewhat silent one. Anna certainly ask I

suddenly where I have learn to sing.



"I have not learn properly. I cann't help singe."



"I do not know you have a voice like that," say she again.



"like what?"



"Herr von Francius will tell you all about it to-morrow," say she,

abruptly.



"what a strange man Herr von Francius be!" say I. "be he clever?"



"oh, very clever."



"at first I do not like he. now I think I do, though."



she make no answer for a few minute; then say:



"he be an excellent teacher."









CHAPTER IX.



HERR VON franciu.





when Miss Hallam hear from Anna Sartorius that my singing have evidently

strike Herr von Francius, and of his intend visit, she look

pleased--so pleased that I be surprised.



he come the following afternoon, at the time he have specify. now, in

the broad daylight, and apart from his official, professional manner, I

find the Herr Direktor still different from the man of last night, and

yet the same. he look even young now than on the estrade last night,

and quiet though his demeanor be, attune to a gentlemanly calm and

evenness, there be still the one thing, the cool, hard glance leave, to

cast his eye round our circle last night, and tell we to sing as if we

be damned.



"Miss Hallam, this be Herr von Francius," say I. "he speak English," I

add.



Von Francius glance from she to I with a somewhat inquire

expression.



Miss Hallam receive he graciously, and they talk about all sort of

trifle, while I sit by in seemly silence, till at last Miss Hallam

"can you give I any opinion upon Miss Wedderburn's voice?"



"Scarcely, until I have give it another trial. she seem to have have no

training."



"no, that be true," she say, and proceed to inform he casually that

she wish I to have every advantage I could get from my stay in

Elberthal, and must put the matter into his hand. Von Francius look

pleased.



for my part, I be deeply move. Miss Hallam's generosity to one so

stupid and ignorant touch I nearly.



Von Francius, pause a short time, at last say:



"I must try her voice again, as I remark. last night I be strike with

her sense of the dramatic point of what we be singe--a quality which

I do not too often find in my pupil. I think, _mein Fräulein_, that

with care and study you might take a place on the stage."



"the stage!" I repeat, startle, and thinking of Courvoisier's word.



but von Francius have be reckon without his host. when Miss Hallam

speak of "put the matter into his hand," she understand the word

in her own sense.



"the stage!" say she, with a slight shiver. "that be quite out of the

question. Miss Wedderburn be a young lady--not an actress."



"so! then it be impossible to be both in your country?" say he, with

polite sarcasm. "I speak as simple _Künstler_--artist--I be not

think of anything else. I do not think the _gnädiges Fräulein_ will

ever make a good singer of mere song. she require emotion to bring out

her good power--a little passion--a little scope for act and abandon

before she can attain the full extent of her talent."



he speak in the most perfectly matter-of-fact way, and I tremble. I

fear lest this display of what Miss Hallam would consider little short

of indecent laxity and Bohemianism, would shock she so much that I

should lose everything by it. it be not so, however.



"Passion--abandon! I think you can not understand what you be talk

Fraucius bow, as if he do not quite see what that have to do with

it--"in short, that idea be impossible. I tell you plainly. she may

stage."



"then she may teach?" say he, inquiringly.



"certainly. I believe that be what she wish to do, in case--if

necessary."



"she may teach, but she may not act," say he, reflectively. "so be it,

then! only," he add as if make a last effort, "I would just mention

that, apart from artistic consideration, while a lady may wear herself

out as a poorly pay teacher, a _prima donna_--"



Miss Hallam smile with calm disdain.



"it be not of the least use to speak of such a thing. you and I look at

the matter from quite different point of view, and to argue about it

would only be to waste time."



Von Francius, with a sarcastic, ambiguous smile, turn to I:



"and you, _mein Fräulein_?"



"I--no. I agree with Miss Hallam," I murmur, not really having find

myself able to think about it at all, but conscious that opposition be

useless. and, besides, I do shrink away from the idea conjure up by

that word, the "stage."



"so!" say he, with a little bow and a half smile. "also, I must try to

make the round man fit into the square hole. the first thing will be

another trial of your voice; then I must see how many lesson a week you

will require, and must give you instruction about practice. you must

understand that it be not pleasure or child's play which you be

undertake. it be a work in order to accomplish which you must strain

every nerve, and give up everything which in any way interfere with

it."



"I don't know whether I shall have time for it," I murmur, look

doubtfully toward Miss Hallam.



"yes, May; you will have time for it," be all she say.



"be there a piano in the house?" say von Francius. "but, yes,

certainly. Fräulein Sartorius have one; she will lend it to we for half

an hour. if you be at liberty, _mein Fräulein_, just now--"



"certainly," say I, follow he, as he tell Miss Hallam that he would

see she again.



as he knock at the door of Anna's sitting-room she come out, dress

for walk.



"_Ach, Fräulein!_ will you allow we the use of your piano for a few

minute?"



"_Bitte!_" say she, motion we into the room. "I be sorry I have an

engagement, and must leave you."



"do not let we keep you on any account," say he, with touching

politeness; and she go out.



"_desto besser!_" he observe, shrug his shoulder.



he pull off his glove with rather an impatient gesture, seat

himself at the piano, and strike some chord, in an annoyed manner.



"who be that old lady?" he inquire, look up at I. "any relation of

your?"



"no--oh, no! I be her companion."



"so! and you mean to let she prevent you from follow the career you

have a talent for?"



"if I do not do as she wish, I shall have no chance of follow any

career at all," say I. "and, besides, how do any one know that I have

a talent--for--for--what you say?"



"I know it; that be why I say it. I wish I could persuade that old lady

to my way of thinking!" he add. "I wish you be out of her hand and

in mine. _na!_ we shall see!"



it be not a very long "trial" that he give I; we soon rise from the

piano.



"to-morrow at eleven I come to give you a lesson," say he. "I be go

to talk to Miss Hallam now. you please not come. I wish to see she

alone; and I can manage she well by myself, _nicht wahr_!"



"thank you," say I in a subdued tone.



"you must have a piano, too," he add; "and we must have the room to

ourselves. I allow no third person to be present in my private lesson,

but go on the principle of Paul Heyse's hero, Edwin, either in open

lecture, or _unter vier Augen_."



with that he hold the door open for I, and as I turn into my room,

shake hand with I in a friendly manner, bid I expect he on the

morrow.



certainly, I decide, Herr von Francius be quite unlike any one I have

ever see before; and how awfully cool he be and self-possess. I

like he well, though.



the next morning Herr von Francius give I my first lesson, and after

that I have one from he nearly every day. as teacher and as acquaintance

he be, as it be, two different man. as teacher he be strict, severe,

give much blame and little praise; but when he do once praise I, I

remember, I carry the remembrance of it with I for day as a ray of

sunshine. he seem never surprised to find how much work have be

quality. he be a teacher whom it be impossible not to respect, whom

one obey by instinct. as man, as acquaintance, I know little of he,

though I hear much--idle tale, which it would be as idle to repeat.

they chiefly relate to his domineering disposition and determination to

go his own way and disregard that of other. in this fashion my life

become busy enough.









CHAPTER X.



"LOHENGRIN."





as time go on, the image of Eugen Courvoisier, my unspoken of,

unguesse at, friend, do not fade from my memory. it grow strong. I

think of he every day--never go out without a distinct hope that I

might see he; never come in without vivid disappointment that I have not

see he. I carry three thaler ten groschen so arranged in my purse

that I could lay my hand upon they at a moment's notice, for as the day

go on it appear that Herr Courvoisier have not make up his account,

or if he have, have not choose to claim that part of they owe by I.



I do not see he. I begin dismally to think that after all the whole

thing be at an end. he do not live at Elberthal--he have certainly

never tell I that he do, I remind myself. he have go about his

a reasonless, aimless pang. what do it, could it, ought it to matter to

I whether I ever see he again or not? nothing, certainly, and yet I

trouble myself about it a great deal. I make little drama in my mind

of how he and I be to meet, and how I would exert my will and make he

to take the money. whenever I see an unusually large or handsome house,

I instantly fall to wonder if it be his, and sometimes make

inquiry as to the owner of any particularly eligible residence. I

hear of Brauns, Müllers, Piepers, Schmidts, and the like, as owner of

the same--never the name Courvoisier. he have disappear--I fear

forever.



come in weary one day from the town, where I have be strive to make

myself understand in shop, I be meet by Anna Sartorius on the stair.

she have not yet cease to be civil to I--civil, that is, in she

way--and my unreasone aversion to she be as great as ever.



"this be the last opera of the season," say she, display a pink

to-night."



"but I be not go."



"yes, you be. Miss Hallam have a ticket for you. I be go to chaperon

you."



"I must go and see about that," say I, hastily rush upstairs.



the news, incredible though it seem, be quite true. the ticket lie

there. I pick it up and gaze at it fondly. stadttheater zu Elberthal.

Parquet, no. 16. as I have never be in a theater in my life, this

convey no distinct idea to my mind, but it be quite enough for I

Vincent, the Englishman, Anna Sartorius, and the dutch boy, Brinks.



it be Friday evening, and the opera be "Lohengrin." I know nothing,

then, about different operatic style, and my idea of operatic music

be base upon duet upon select air from "La Traviata," "La

Somnambula," and "Lucia." I think the story of "Lohengrin," as relate

opera be to be a different one from that of most english girl. since,

I have wonder sometimes what would be the result upon the musical

taste of a person who be put through a course of wagnerian opera first,

and then turn over to the italian school--leave Mozart, Beethoven,

Gluck, to take care of themselves, as they may very well do--thus

exactly reverse the usual (English) process.



Anna be very quiet that evening. afterward I know that she must have

be observe I. we be in the first row of the parquet, with the

orchestra alone between we and the stage. I be fully occupy in

look about I--now at the curtain hide the great mystery, now

behind and above I at the box, in a youthful state of ever-increase

hope and expectation.



"we be very early," say Vincent, who be next to I, "very early, and

very near," he add, but he do not seem much distressed at either

circumstance.



then the gas be suddenly turn up quite high. the bustle increase

cheerfully. the old, young, and middle-aged lady who fill the

_logen_ in the _Erster Rang_--hardened theater-goer, who come as

regularly every night in the week during the eight month of the season

the utmost violence, exchange nod and odd little old-fashioned bow

with other lady in all part of the house, lean over to look

whether the parquet be well fill, and remark that there be

more people in the _balcon_ than usual. the musician be drop

pleasant-look young violinist with the brown eye, whose name I have

hear call out at the eye hospital. they all seem very fond of he,

seem to have a series of joke to relate to Herr Helfen, explode

with laughter, and every now and then shake the loose thick hair from

his handsome, genial face. Helfen listen to he with a half smile,

screw up his violin and give he a quiet look now and then. the

inspiring noise of tune up have begin, and I be on the very tiptoe of

expectation.



as I turn once more and look round, Vincent say, laugh, "Miss

Wedderburn, your hat have hit I three time in the face." it be, by the

by, the brown hat which have grace my head that day at Köln.



"oh, have it? I beg your pardon!" say I, laugh too, as I bring my

eye again to bear on the stage. "the seat be too near toge--"



further word be upon my lip, but they be never utter. in rove

that by right belong to the leader of the first violin. Friedhelm

a nod here, a half smile there, a tap on the shoulder in another

eye say as plainly as possibly that he like it. fast friend, on the

motionless, and quiet, I certainly realize as nearly as possible that

sentimental dream have picture he. I feel it all: I also know now

that it be partly the very strength of the feeling that I have--the very

intensity of the admiration which take from I the reflection and reason

that no one know what have happen; I dread lest they should. I do

the most cowardly and treacherous thing that circumstance permit to

and again, while my ear be singe, my heart beat so, and I

think every one in the theater know and be look at I.



"mind you listen to the overture, Miss Wedderburn," say Vincent,

hastily, in my ear, as the first liquid, yearning, long-draw note

sound from the violin.



"yes," say I, raise my face at last, look or rather feel a

look compel from I, to the place where he sit. this time our eye

meet fully. I do not know what I feel when I see he look at I as

unrecognizingly as if I have be a wooden doll in a shop window. be he

look past I? no. his eye meet mine direct--glance for glance; not a

sign, not a quiver of the mouth, not a waver of the eyelid. I hear no

more of the overture. when he be play, and so occupy with his

most distressed at my own disloyalty to a kind friend, or most appal

to find that the man with whom I have spend a whole afternoon in the firm

conviction that he be outwardly, as well as inwardly, my equal and a

gentleman--(how the tear, half of shame, half of joy, rise to my eye

now as I think of my poor, pedantic little scruple then!) the man of

whom I have assuredly think and dream many and many a time and oft

be--a professional musician, a man in a band, a german band, play in

the public orchestra of a provincial town. well! well!



in our village at home, where the population consist of clergyman's

widow, daughter of deceased naval officer, and old woman in general,

and those old woman lady of the genteelest description--the Army and

the Church (for which I have be bring up to have the deep

veneration and esteem, as the two head power in our land--for we do

not take Manchester, Birmingham, and Liverpool into account at

Skernford)--the Army and the Church, I say, look down a little upon

Medicine and the Law, as be perhaps more necessary, but less select

factor in that great sum--the Nation, Medicine and the Law look down

very decidedly upon commercial wealth, and Commerce in her turn turn

up her nose at retail establishment, while one and all--Church and

Army, Law and Medicine, Commerce in the gross and Commerce in the

little--united in point the finger at artist, musician, literati,

_et id omne genus_, consider they, with some few well-know and

orthodox exception, as bohemian, and call they "person." they be

a class with whom we have and could have nothing in common; so utterly

outside our life that we scarcely ever give a thought to their

existence. we read of picture, and wish to see they; hear of musical

wonder, and desire to hear they--as picture, as composition. I do

not think it ever enter our head to remember that a man with a quick

life throb in his vein, with feeling, hope, and fear and

thought, paint the picture, and that in see it we also see

he--that a consciousness, if possible, yet more keen and vivid produce

the combination of sound which bring tear to our eye when we hear

"the band"--beautiful abstraction--play they! certainly we never

consider the performer as anything more than people who could

play--one who blow his breath into a brass tube; another into a wooden

pipe; one who scrape a small fiddle with fine string, another who

scrape a big one with coarse string.



I be seventeen, and not have an original mind, have up to now judge

thing from early teaching and impression. I do not ask to be

excuse. I only say that I be ignorant as ever even a girl of seventeen

be. I do not know the amount of art and culture which lie among those

one who can put some of the meaning of the music into his gesture and

should have be my friend.



"how he must despise I!" I think, my cheek burn, my eye fasten

point I out to they all and say, 'that be an english girl--lady I

can not call she. I find she quite alone and lose at Köln, and I do

all I could to help she. I save she a great deal of anxiety and

inconvenience. she be not above accept my assistance; she confide

her story very freely to I; she be nothing very particular--have nothing

to boast of--no money, no knowledge, nothing superior; in fact, she be

simple and ignorant to quite a surprising extent; but she have just cut

I dead. what do you think of she?'"



until the curtain go up, I sit in torture. when the play begin,

however, even my discomfort vanish in my wonder at the spectacle. it

be the first I have see. try to picture it, oh, wear-out and _blasé_

frequenter of play and opera! try to realize the feeling of an

impressionable young person of seventeen when "Lohengrin" be reveal

glamour of eld upon he--Lohengrin, sail in blue and silver like a

dream, in his swan-draw boat, step majestic forth, and speak in

a voice of pure melody, as he thank the bird and dismiss it:



     "Dahin, woher mich trug dein Kahn

     Kehr wieder mir zu unserm Glück!

     Drum sei getreu dein Dienst gethan,

     Leb wohl, leb wohl, mein lieber Schwan."



Elsa, with the wonder, the gratitude, the love, and alas! the weakness

in her eye! the astonished Brabantine man and woman. they could not

have be more astonished than I be. it be all perfectly real to I.

what do I know about the stage? to I, yonder figure in blue mantle and

inartistically make-up countenance, and large foot. I sit through the

as I nervously contemplate a graceful indifferent remark to Herr

say to Eustace Vincent:



"good-evening: _wie geht's Ihnen?_"



Vincent hold out his hand. "very well, thank. and you? I haven't see

you lately."

"last Thursday week, Courvoisier--why don't you come? we be wait

"I miss the train."



until now he have be speak German, but he say this distinctly in

"no," be all Vincent say.



"do you have anything new? be Herr von Francius there too?"



"yes; he be there too."



I ponder. brink whistle loudly the air of Elsa's "Brautzug," as we

pace across the Lindenallée. we have not many pace to go. the lamp

be light, the people be throng thick as in the daytime. the air

be full of laughter, talk, whistle and hum of the air from the

opera. my ear strain eagerly through the confusion. I could have

catch the faint sound of Courvoisier's voice have it be there,

but it be not. and we come home; Vincent open the door with his

latch-key, say, "it have not be very brilliant, have it? that tenor be

a stick," and we all go to our different room. it be in such wise

that I meet Eugen Courvoisier for the second time.









CHAPTER XI.



"will you sing?"





the theater season close with that evening on which "Lohengrin" be

perform. I run no risk of meet Courvoisier face to face again in

that alarming, sudden manner. but the subject have assume diseased

proportion in my mind. I find myself confront with he yet, and week

after week. my business in Elberthal be music--to learn as much music

and hear as much music as I could: wherever there be music there be

also Eugen Courvoisier--naturally. there be only one _städtische

Kapelle_ in Elberthal. once a week at least--each Saturday--I see he,

and he see I at the unfailing instrumental concert to which every one

in the house go, and to absent myself from which would instantly set

every one wonder what could be my motive for it. my usual companion

be Clara Steinmann, Vincent, the Englishman, and often Frau Steinmann

herself. Anna Sartorius and some other girl student of art usually

bring sketch-book, and be far too much occupy in make study

or caricature of the audience to pay much attention to the music. the

audience be, however, harden; they be use to it. Anna and her

friend be not alone in the practice. there be a dozen or more

artist or _soi-disant_ artist busily engage with their sketch-book.

the concert-room offer a rich field to they. one could at least be

sure of one thing--that they be not take off the person at whom

they look most intently. there must be quite a gallery hide away in

some old sketch-book--of portrait or wicked caricature of the

audience that frequent the concert of the Instrumental Musik Verein.

I wonder where they all be? who have they? what have become of the

light-hearted sketcher? I often recall those homely Saturday evening

concert; the long, shabby saal with its fade out-of-date decoration;

its row of small table with the well-know group around they; the

mixed and motley audience. how easy, after a little while, to pick out

the English, by their look of complacent pleasure at the delightful ease

and unceremoniousness of the whole affair; their gladness at find a

public entertainment where one's clothe be not oblige to be select

with a view to outshine those of every one else in the room; the

student shroud in a mystery, secret and impenetrable, of tobacco

smoke. the spruce-look school-boy from the Gymnasium and Realschule,

the old captain and general, the Fräulein their daughter, the

_gnädigen Frauen_ their wife; dress in the disastrous plaid, check,

and stripe, which somehow none but german woman ever get hold of.

Shades of Le Follet! what costume there be on young and old for an

observe eye! what bonnet, what boot, what stupendously dare

accumulation of color and style and period of dress cram and pile

on the person of one substantial Frau Generalin, or Doctorin or

Professorin! the low orchestra--the tall, slight, yet command figure

of von Francius on the estrade; his dark face with its indescribable

mixture of pride, impenetrability and insouciance; the musician behind

he--every face of they well know to the audience as those of the

audience to they: it be not a mere "concert," which in England be

another word for so much expense and so much vanity--it be a gathering

of friend. we know the music in which the Kapelle be most at home; we

know their strong point and their weak one; the passage in the

Pastoral Symphony where the second violin be a little weak; that

overture where the blaseninstrumente come out so well--the symphony

one hear--the divine wealth of undye art and beauty! those day be

past: despite what I suffer in they they have their joy for I. yes; I

suffer at those concert. I must ever see the one face which for I

blot out all other in the room, and endure the silent contempt which

I believe I see upon it. probably it be my own feeling of inward

self-contempt which make I believe I see that expression there. his

face have for I a miserable, basilisk-like attraction. when I be there

he be there, I must look at he and endure the silent, smile disdain

which I at least believe he bestow upon I. how do he contrive to do

it? how often our eye meet, and every time it happen he look I full

in the face, and never would give I the faint gleam of recognition!

it be as though I look at two diamond, which return my stare

unwinkingly and unseeingly. I manage to make myself thoroughly

miserable--pale and thin with anxiety and self-reproach I let this man,

and the speculation concern he, take up my whole thought, and I

keep silence, because I dread so intensely lest any question should

bring out the truth. I smile drearily when I think that there

certainly be no danger of any one but Miss Hallam ever know it, for

the only person who could have betray I choose now, of deliberate

purpose, to cut I as completely as I have once cut he.



as if to show very decidedly that he do intend to cut I, I meet he one

day, not in the street, but in the house, on the stair. he spring up

the step, two at a time, come to a momentary pause on the landing, and

look at I. no look of surprise, none of recognition. he raise his

hat; that be nothing; in ordinary politeness he would have do it have

he never see I in his life before. the same cold, bright, hard glance

fall upon I, keen as an eagle's, and as devoid of every gentle

influence as the same.



I silently hold out my hand.



he look at it for a moment, then with a grave coolness which chill

I to the soul, murmur something about "not have the honor," bow

slightly, and step forward, walk into Vincent's room.



I be go to the room in which my piano stand, where I have my music

lesson, for they have tell I that Herr von Francius be wait. I

look at he as I go into the room. how different he be from that

other man; darker, more secret, more scornful-looking, with not less

power, but so much less benevolence.



I be _distrait_, and sing exceedingly ill. we have be go through

the solo soprano part of the "Paradise Lost." I believe I sing vilely

that morning. I be not think of Eva's sin and the serpent, but of

other thing, which, despite the story relate in the Book of Genesis,

touch I more nearly. several time already have he make I sing

through Eva's stammering answer to her God's question:



     "ah, Lord!... the Serpent!

     the beautiful, glitter Serpent,

     with his beautiful, glitter word,

     he, Lord, do lead astray

     the weak woman!"



"bah!" exclaim von Francius, when I have sing it some three or four

time, each time bad, each time more distractedly. he fling the music

upon the floor, and his eye flash, startle I from my uneasy

thought back to the present. he be look at I with a dark cloud

upon his face. I stare, stoop meekly, and pick up the music.



"Fräulein, what be you dream about?" he ask, impatiently. "you be

not singe Eva's shame and dawn terror as she feel herself undone.

you be singe--and badly, too--a mere sentimental song, such as any

school-girl might stumble through. I be ashamed of you."



"I--I," stammer I, crimsoning, and ashamed for myself too.



"you be think of something else," he say, his brow clear a

little. "_na!_ it come so sometimes. something have happen to distract

your attention. the amiable Miss Hallam have be a little _more_ amiable

than usual."



"no."



"well, well. _'S ist mir egal._ but now, as you have waste half an hour

in vanity and vexation, will you be good enough to let your thought

return here to I and to your duty? or else--I must go, and leave the

lesson till you be in the right voice again."



"I be all right--try I," say I, my pride rise in arm as I think

of Courvoisier's behavior a short time ago.



"very well. now. you be eva, please remember, the first woman, and you

have go wrong. think of who be question you, and--"



"oh, yes, yes, I know. please begin."



he begin the accompaniment, and I sing for the fifth time Eva's

scatter note of shame and excuse.



"Brava!" say he, when I have finish, and I be the more startled as he

have never before give I the faint sign of approval, but have find

such constant fault with I that I usually have a fit of weeping after my

lesson; weep with rage and disappointment at my own shortcoming.



"at last you know what it mean," say he. "I always tell you your forte

be dramatic singing."



"dramatic! but this be an oratorio."



"it may be call an oratorio, but it be a drama all the same. what more

dramatic, for instance, than what you have just sing, and all that go

before? now suppose we go on. I will take Adam."



having give myself up to the music, I sing my good with earnestness.

when we have finish von Francius close the book, look at I, and

say:



"will you sing the 'Eva' music at the concert?"



"I?"



he bow silently, and still keep his eye fix upon my face, as if to

say, "refuse if you dare."



"I--I'm afraid I should make such a mess of it," I murmur at last.



"why any more than to-day?"



"oh! but all the people!" say I, expostulate; "it be so different."



he give a little laugh of some amusement.



"how odd! and yet how like you!" say he. "do you suppose that the

people who will be at the concert will be half as much alive to your

defect as I be? if you can sing before I, surely you can sing before

so many row of--"



"cabbage? I wish I could think they be."



"nonsense! what would be the use, where the pleasure, in singe to

cabbage? I mean simply inhabitant of Elberthal. what can there be so

formidable about they?"



I murmur something.



"well, will you do it?"



"I be sure I should break down," say I, try to find some sign of

relent in his eye. I discover none. he be not wait to hear

whether I say "yes" or "no," he be wait until I say "yes."



"if you do," he reply, with a friendly smile, "I should never teach

you another note."



"why not?"



"because you would be a coward, and not worth teaching."



"but Miss Hallam?"



"leave she to I."



I still hesitate.



"it be the _premier pas qui coûte_," say he, keep a friendly but

determined gaze upon my undecided face.



"I want to accustom you to appear in public," he add. "by degree,

you know. there be nothing unusual in Germany for one in your position

to sing in such a concert."



"I be not think of that; but that it be impossible that I can sing

well enough--"



"you sing well enough for my purpose. you will be amazed to find what an

impetus to your study, and what a filip to your industry will be give

by once singe before a number of other people. and then, on the

stage--"



"but I be not go on the stage."



"I think you be. at least, if you do otherwise you will do wrong. you

have gift which be in themselves a responsibility."



"I--gift--what gift?" I ask, incredulously. "I be as stupid as a

donkey. my sister always say so, and sister be sure to know; you may

trust they for that."



"then you will take the soprano solo?"



"do you think I can?"



"I don't think you can; I say you must. I will call upon Miss Hallam

this afternoon. and the _gage_--fee--what you call it?--is fifty

thaler."



"what!" I cry, my whole attitude change to one of greedy

expectation. "shall I be pay?"



"why, _natürlich_," say he, turn over sheet of music, and avert

his face to hide a smile.



"oh! then I will sing."



"good! only please to remember that it be my concert, and I be

responsible for the soloist; and pray think rather more about the

beautiful glitter serpent than about the beautiful glitter

thaler."



"I can think about both," be my unholy, time-serve reply.



fifty thaler. untold gold!









CHAPTER XII.



"Prinz Eugen, der edle Ritter."





it be the evening of the haupt-probe, a fine moonlight night in the

middle of May--a month since I have come to Elberthal, and it seem so

much, so very much more.



to my astonishment--and far from agreeable astonishment--Anna Sartorius

inform I of her intention to accompany I to the probe. I put

objection in her way as well as I know how, and say I do not think

outsider be admit. she laugh, and say:



"that be too funny, that you should instruct I in such thing. why, I

have a ticket for all the proben, as any one can have who choose to pay

two thaler at the _sasse_. I have a mind to hear this. they say the

orchestra be go to rebel against von Francius. and I be go to the

concert to-morrow, too. one can not hear too much of such fine music;

and when one's friend sing, too--"



"what friend of yours be go to sing?" I inquire, coldly.



"why, you, you _allerliebster kleiner Engel_," say she, in a tone of

familiarity, to which I strongly object.



I could say no more against her go, but certainly display no

enthusiastic desire for her company.



the probe, we find, be to be in the great saal; it be half light,

and there be perhaps some fifty people, holder of probe-ticket,

seat in the parquet.



"you be go to sing well to-night," say von Francius, as he hand

I up the steps--"for my sake and your own, _nicht wahr_?"



"I will try," say, I, look round the great orchestra, and see how

full it be--so many fresh face, both in chorus and orchestra.



and as I look, I see Courvoisier come in by the little door at the top

of the orchestra step and descend to his place. his face be

clouded--very clouded; I have never see he look thus before. he have no

smile for those who greet he. as he take his place beside Helfen, and

the latter ask he some question, he stare absently at he, then

answer with a look of absence and weariness.



"Herr Courvoisier," say von Francius--and I, be near, hear the

whole dialogue--"you always allow yourself to be wait for."



Courvoisier glance up. I with a new, sudden interest, watch the

behavior of the two man. in the face of von Francius I think to

discover dislike, contempt.



"I beg your pardon; I be detain," answer Courvoisier, composedly.



"it be unfortunate that you should be so often detain at the time when

your work should be begin."



unmove and unchanging, Courvoisier hear and submit to the word,

and to the tone in which they be speak--sarcastic, sneering, and

unbelieving.



"now we will begin," pursue von Francius, with a disagreeable smile, as

he rap with his baton upon the rail. I look at Courvoisier--look

at his friend, Friedhelm Helfen. the former be sit as quietly as

possible, rather pale, and with the same clouded look, but not deep

than before; the latter be flushed, and eyed von Francius with no

friendly glance.



there seem a kind of slumber storm in the air. there be none of

the lively discussion usual at the proben. Courvoisier, first of the

first violin, and from whom all the other seem to take their tone,

sit silent, grave and still. Von Francius, though quiet, be bite. I

feel afraid of he. something must have happen to put he into that

evil mood.



my part do not come until late in the second part of the oratorio. I

have almost forget that I be to sing at all, and be watch von

Francius and listen to his sharp speech. I remember what Anna

Sartorius have say in describe this haupt-probe to I. it be all just

as she have say. he be severe; his speech rouse the phlegmatic

blood, set the professional instrumentalist laugh at their amateur

co-operator, but provoke no reply or resentment. it be extraordinary,

the effect of this man's will upon those he have to do with--upon woman

in particular.



there be one haughty-look blonde--a swede--tall, majestic, with long

yellow curl, and a face full of pride and high temper, who give herself

decide air, and trust to her beauty and insolence to carry off

certain radical defect of harshness of voice and want of ear. I never

forget how she stare I down from head to foot on the occasion of my

first appearance alone, as if to say, "what do you want here?"



it be in vain that she look haughty and handsome. address she as

Fräulein Hulstrom, von Francius give she a sharp lecture, and imitate

the effect of her voice in a particularly soft passage with ludicrous

accuracy. the rest of the chorus be titter audibly, the musician,

with the exception of Courvoisier and his friend, nudge each other and

smile. she bridle haughtily, flash a furious glance at her mentor,

grow crimson, receive a sarcastic smile which baffle she, and subside

again.



so it be with they all. his blame be plentiful; his praise so rare as

to be almost an unknown quantity. his chorus and orchestra be fame

for the minute perfection and precision of their play and singing.

perhaps the performance lack something else--passion, color. Von

Francius, at that time at least, be no genius, though his talent, his

power, and his method be undeniably great. he be, however, not

popular--not the Harold, the "beloved leader" of his people.



it be to-night that I be first show how all be not smooth for he;

that in this art union there be splits--"little rift within the

lute," which, should they extend, might literally in the end "make the

music mute." I hear whisper around I. "Herr von Francius be

angry."--"_Nicht wahr_?"--"Herr Courvoisier look angry too."--"Yes, he

do."--"There will be an open quarrel there soon."--"I think

so."--"They be both clever; one should be less clever than the

of his own, and that all the orchestra be on his side."--"So!" in

accent of curiosity and astonishment--"_Ja wohl!_ and that if von

Francius do not mind, he will see Herr Courvoisier in his place,"

etc., etc., without end. all which excite I much, as the first glimpse

into the affair of those about whom we think much and know little (a

form of life well know to woman in general) always do interest we.



these thing make I forget to be nervous or anxious. I see myself now

as part of the whole, a unit in the sum of a life which interest I.

Von Francius give I a sign of approval when I have finish, but it be

a mechanical one. he be think of other thing.



the probe be over. I walk slowly down the room look for Anna

Sartorius, more out of politeness than because I wish for her company.

I be relieve to find that she have already go, probably not find

all the entertainment she expect, and I be able, with a good

conscience, to take my way home alone.



my way home! not yet. I be to live through something before I could

take my way home.



I go out of the large saal through the long veranda into the street. a

flood of moonlight silver it. there be a laugh, chatter crowd

about I--all the chorus; man and girl, go to their home or their

lodging, in one or two, or in large cheerful group. almost opposite

low floor be use as a shop for antiquity, curiosity, and a

thousand odd and end useful or beautiful to artist, costume, suit

of armor, old china, anything and everything. the window be yet

light. as I pause for a moment before take my homeward way, I see

one be Courvoisier; in the other I think to recognize Friedhelm

as I see by the bright large lamp that burn within, but along the

harmonize with the place, come forward. she look at I as if

"do Herr Courvoisier, a musiker, live here?"



"_Ja wohl!_" answer the young woman, with a look of still great

surprise. "on the third _étage_, straight upstairs. the name be on the

door."



I turn away, and go slowly up the steep wooden uncarpeted staircase.

on the first land a door open at the sound of my footstep, and a

face under the bush of hair.



"Ugh!" say the owner of this amiable visage, and shut the door with a

bang. I look at the plate upon it; it bear the legend, "Hermann

Duntze, Maler." to the second _étage_. another door--another plate:

"Bernhardt Knoop, Maler." the house seem to be a resort of artist.

there be a lamp burn on each landing; and now, at last, with breath

and heart alike fail, I ascend the last flight of stair, and find

beat suffocatingly. some one have scribble in red chalk beneath the

christian name, "Prinz Eugen, der edle Ritter." have it be do in jest

or earnest? I wonder, and then knock. such a knock!



"_Herein!_"



I open the door, and step into a large, long, low room. on the

table, in the center, burn a lamp, and sit there, with the light

fall upon his earnest young face, be Helfen, the violinist, and near

to he sit Courvoisier, with a child upon his knee, a little lad with

immense dark eye, tumble black hair, and flushed, just awakened face.

he be clothe in his night-dress and a little red dressing-gown, and

look like a spot of almost feverish, quite tropic brightness in

contrast with the grave, pale face which bent over he. Courvoisier hold

the two delicate little hand in one of his own, and be look down

the different complexion and a different style of feature too, there

be so great a likeness in the two face, particularly in the broad,

noble brow, as to leave no doubt of the relationship. my musician and

the boy be father and son.



Courvoisier look up as I come in. for one half moment there leap

into his eye a look of surprise and of something more. if it have last

a second long I could have swear it be welcome--then it be go. he
