then turn to I as to some stranger who have come on an errand as yet

unknown to he, and do not speak. the little one, from Helfen's knee,

stare at I with large, solemn eye, and Helfen himself look scarcely

less impressed.



I have no doubt I look frightened--I feel so--frighten out of my

sense. I come tremulously forward, and offer my piece of silver,

say, in the small voice which I have ever use:



"I have come to pay my debt. I do not know where you live, or I should

have do it long before."



he make no motion to take the money, but say--I almost start, so

alter be the voice from that of my frank companion at Köln, to an icy

coldness of ceremony:



"_Mein Fräulein_, I do not understand."



"you--you--the thing you pay for. do you not remember I?"



"remember a lady who have intimate that she wish I to forget she? no,

I do not."



what a horribly complicated revenge! think I, as I say, ever low

and low, more and more shamedfacedly, while the young violinist sit

with the child on his knee, and his soft brown eye stare at I in

wonder:



"I think you must remember. you help I at Köln, and you pay for my

ticket to Elberthal, and for something that I have at the hotel. you tell

I that be what I owe you."



I again tender the money; again he make no effort to receive it, but

say:



"I be sorry that I do not understand to what you refer. I only know it

be impossible that I could ever have tell you you owe I three thaler,

or three anything, or that there could, under any circumstance, be any

question of money between you and I. suppose we consider the topic at

an end."



such a voice of ice, and such a manner, to chill the bold heart, I

have never yet encounter. the cool, unspeakable disdain cut I to the

quick.



"you have no right to refuse the money," say I, desperately. "you have

no right to insult I by--by--" an appropriate peroration refuse

itself.



again the sweet, proud, courteous smile; not only courteous, but

courtly; again the icy little bow of the head, which would have do

credit to a prince in displeasure, and which yet have the deference due

from a gentleman to a lady.



"you will excuse the semblance of rudeness which may appear if I say

that if you unfortunately be not of a very decide disposition, I be.

it be impossible that I should ever have the slight intercourse with

a lady who have once unequivocally refuse my acquaintance. the lady may

honor I by change her mind; I be sorry that I can not respond. I do

not change my mind."



"you must let we part on equal term," I reiterate. "it be unjust--"



"yourself close all possibility of the faint attempt at further

acquaintance, _mein Fräulein_. the matter be at an end."



"Herr Courvoisier, I--"



"at an end," he repeat, calmly, gently, look at I as he have often

look at I since the night of "Lohengrin," with a glance that baffle

and chill I.



"I wish to apologize--"



"for what?" he inquire, with the faint possible look of indifferent

surprise.



"for my rudeness--my surprise--I--"



"you refer to one evening at the opera. you exercise your privilege, as

a lady, of close an acquaintance which you do not wish to renew. I

now exercise mine, as a gentleman, of say that I choose to abide by

that decision, now and always."



I be surprised. despite my own apologetic frame of mind, I be

surprised at his hardness; at the narrowness and ungenerosity which

could so determinedly shut the door in the face of an humble penitent

like I. he must see how I have repent the stupid slip I have make;

he must see how I desire to atone for it. it be not a slip of the

kind one would name irreparable, and yet he behave to I as if I

have commit a crime; freeze I with look and word. be he so

self-conscious and so vain that he could not get over that small slight

to his self-consequence, commit in haste and confusion by an ignorant

girl? even then, even in that moment I ask myself these question, my

astonishment be almost as great as my pain, for it be the very

reverse, the very opposite of what I have picture to myself. once let I

see he and speak to he, I have say to myself, and it would be all

right; every lineament of his face, every tone of his voice, bespoke a

frank, generous nature--one that could forgive. alas! and alas! this be

the truth!



he have come to the door; he stand by it now, hold it open, look at

I so courteously, so deferentially, with a manner of one who have be a

gentleman and live with gentleman all his life, but in a way which at

the same time order I out as plainly as possible.



I go to the door. I could no long stand under that chilling glance,

nor endure the cool, polished contempt of the manner. I behave by no

mean heroically; neither fling my head back, nor mutter any defiance,

nor in any way prove myself a person of spirit. all I could do be to

look appealingly into his face; to search the bright, steady eye,

without find in they any hint of soften or relent.



"will you not take it, please?" I ask, in a quivering voice and with

tremble lip.



"impossible, _mein Fräulein_," with the same chilly little bow as

before.



struggle to repress my tear, I say no more, but pass out, cut to

the heart. the door be close gently behind I. I feel as if it have

close upon a bright belief of my youth. I lean for a moment against

the passage wall and press my hand against my eye. from within come

the sound of a child's voice, "_Mein vater_," and the soft, deep murmur

of Eugen's answer; then I go down-stair and into the open street.



that hate, hateful three thaler ten groschen be still clasp in my

hand. what be I to do with it? throw it into the Rhine, and wash it

away forever? give it to some one in need? fling it into the gutter?

send it he by post? I dismiss that idea for what it be worth. no; I

would obey his prohibition. I would keep it--those very coin, and when

I feel inclined to be proud and conceited about anything on my own

account, or dispose to put down superhuman charm to the account of

other, I would go and look at they, and they would preach I eloquent

sermon.



as I go into the house, up the stair to my room, the front door

open again and Anna Sartorius overtake I.



"I think you have leave the probe?" say I, stare at she.



"so I have, _herzchen_," say she, with her usual ambiguous, mocking

laugh; "but I be not compel to come home, like a good little girl,

the moment I come out of the Tonhalle. I have be visit a friend.

but where have you be, for the probe must have be over for some

time? we hear the people go past; indeed, some of they be stay in

the house where I be. do you take a walk in the moonlight?"



"good-night," say I, too weary and too indifferent even to answer she.



"it must have be a tiring walk; you seem weary, quite _ermüdet_," say

she, mockingly, and I make no answer.



"a haupt-probe be a dismal thing after all," she call out to I from

the top of the stair.



from my inmost heart I agree with she.









CHAPTER XIII.



KAFFEEKLATSCH.





 "_Phillis._ I want none o' thy friendship!

 _Lesbia._ then take my enmity!"



"when a number of lady meet together to discuss matter of importance,

we call it 'kaffeeklatsch,'" Courvoisier have say to I on that

never-forget afternoon of my adventure at Köln.



it be my first kaffeeklatsch which, in a measure, decide my destiny.

Hitherto, that is, up to the end of June, I have not be at any

entertainment of this kind. at last there come an invitation to Frau

Steinmann and to Anna Sartorius, to assist at a "coffee" of unusual

magnitude, and Frau Steinmann suggest that I should go with they and

see what it be like. nothing loath, I consent.



"bring some work," say Anna Sartorius to I, "or you will find it

_langweilig_--slow, I mean."



"shall we not have some music?"



"Music, yes, the sweetest of all--that of our own tongue. you shall

hear every one's candid opinion of every one else--present company

always except, and you will see what the state of elberthal society

really be--present company still except. by a very strange chance the

lady who meet at a klatsch be always good, pious, virtuous, and,

above all, charitable. it be wonderful how well we manage to keep the

black sheep out, and have nothing but lamb immaculate."



"oh, don't!"



"oh, bah! I know the Elberthal _klatscherei_. it have pick I to piece

many a time. after you have partake to-day of its coffee and its cake,

it will pick you to piece."



"but," say I, arrange the ruffle of my very good frock, which I have

be tell it be _de rigueur_ to wear, "I think woman never gossip

so much among man."



Fräulein Sartorius laugh loud and long.



"the man! _Du meine Güte!_ man at a kaffeeklatsch! show I the one that

a man dare even look into, and I'll crown you--and he too--with laurel,

and bay, and the wild parsley. a man at a kaffee--_mag Gott es

bewahren!_"



"oh!" say I, half disappoint, and with a very poor, mean sense of

dissatisfaction at having put on my pretty new dress for the first time

only for the edification of a number of virulent gossip.



"Men!" she reiterate with a harsh laugh as we walk toward the

Goldsternstrasse, our destination. "man--no. we despise their company,

you see. we only talk about they directly or indirectly from the moment

of meeting to that of parting."



"I'm sorry there be no gentleman," say I, and I be. I feel I look

well.



arrive at the scene of the kaffee, we be conduct to a bedroom where

we lay aside our hat and mantle. I be stand before the glass,

draw a comb through my upturned hair, and contemplate with

irrepressible satisfaction the delicate lavender hue of my dress, when I

suddenly see reflect behind I the dark, harshly cut face of Anna

Sartorius. she start slightly; then say, with a laugh which have in it

something a little forced:



"we be a contrast, ben't we? Beauty and the Beast, one might almost

say. _na! 's schad't nix._"



I turn away in a little offended pride. her familiarity annoy I.

what if she be a thousand time cleverer, witty, well read than I?

I do not like she. a shade cross her face.



"be it that you be thoroughly unamiable?" say she, in a voice which

have reproach in it, "or be all english girl so touchy that they

receive a compliment upon their good look as if it be an offense?"



"I wish you would not talk of my 'good look' as if I be a dog or a

horse!" say I, angrily. "I hate to be flatter. I be no beauty, and do

not wish to be treat as if I be."



"do you always hate it?" say she from the window, whither she have

turn. "_Ach!_ there go Herr Courvoisier!"



the name startle I like a sudden report. I make an eager step forward

before I have time to recollect myself--then stop.



"he be not out of sight yet," say she, with a curious look, "if you

wish to see he."



I sit down and make no answer. what prompt she to talk in such a

manner? be it a mere coincidence?



"he be a handsome fellow, _nicht wahr_?" she say, still watch I,

while I think Frau Steinmann never would manage to arrange her cap in

the style that please she. "but a _Taugenichts_ all the same," pursue

Anna as I do not speak. "don't you think so?" she add.



"a _Taugenichts_--I don't know what that be."



"what you call a good-for-nothing."



"oh."



"_Nicht wahr?_" she persist.



"I know nothing about it."



"I do. I will tell you all about he some time."



"I don't wish to know anything about he."



"so!" say she, with a laugh.



without further word or look I follow Frau Steinmann down-stair.



the lady of the house be seat in the midst of a large concourse of

old and young lady, hold she own with a well-season hardihood in

the midst of the awful Babel of tongue. what a noise! it smite upon and

stun my confounded ear. our hostess advance and lead I with a wave

of the hand into the center of the room, when she introduce I to about

a dozen lady: and every one in the room stop talk and working,

and stare at I intently and unwinkingly until my name have be

pronounce, after which some continue still to stare at I, and

comment openly upon it. meanwhile I be conduct to a sofa at the

end of the room, and request in a set phrase, "_Bitte, Fräulein,

nehman sie platz auf dem sofa_," with which long custom have since make

I familiar, to take my seat upon it. I humbly try to decline the

honor, but Anna Sartorius, behind I, whisper:



"sit down directly, unless you want to be think an utter barbarian.

the place have be keep for you."



deeply impressed, and very uncomfortable, I sit down. first one and then

another come and speak and talk to I. their question and remark

be much in this style:



"do you like Elberthal? what be your christian name? how old be you?

have you be or be you engaged to be marry? they break off

engagement in England for a mere trifle, don't they? _Schrecklich!_ do

you get your dress in Elberthal? what do it cost the _elle_? young

english lady wear silk much more than young german lady. you never

go to the theater on Sunday in England--you be all _pietistisch_. how

beautifully you speak our language! really no foreign accent!" (this

repeatedly and unblushingly, in spite of my most flagrant mistake, and

in the face of my most feeble, halting, and stammer effort to make

myself understand.) "do you learn music? singe? from whom? Herr von

Francius? _Ach, so!_" (pause, while they all look impressively at I.

the very name of von Francius call up emotion of no common order.) "I

believe I have see you at the proben to the 'Paradise Lost.' perhaps

you be the lady who be to take the solo? yes! _Du lieber Himmel!_ what

do you think of Herr von Francius? be he not nice?" (_Nett_, though,

signify something feminine and finikin.) "no? how odd! there be no

accounting for the taste of english woman. do you know many people in

Elberthal? no? _schade!_ no officer? not Hauptmann Sachse?" (with voice

grow gradually shriller), "nor Lieutenant Pieper? not know

Lieutenant Pieper! _um Gotteswillen!_ what do you mean? he be so

handsome! such eye! such a mustache! _Herrgott!_ and you do not know

he? I will tell you something. when he go off to the autumn maneuver

at Frankfort (I have it on good authority), twenty young lady go to

see he off."



"disgusting!" I exclaim, unable to control my feeling any long. I

see Anna Sartorius malignantly smile as she rock herself in an

american rocking-chair.



"how! disgusting? you be joke. he have dozen of bouquet. all the

girl be in love with he. they compel the photographer to sell they

his photograph, and they all believe he be in love with they. I believe

Luise Breidenstein will die if he don't propose to she."



"they ought to be ashamed of themselves."



"but he be so handsome, so delightful. he dance divinely, and know

such good riddle, and acts--_ach, himmlisch!_"



"but how absurd to make such a fuss of he!" I cry, hot and indignant.

"the idea of go on so about a man!"



a chorus, a shriek, a Babel of expostulation.



"Listen, Thekla! Fräulein Wedderburn do not know Lieutenant Pieper,

and do not think it right to _schwärm_ for he."



"the darling! no one can help it who know he!" say another.



"let she wait till she do know he," say Thekla, a sentimental young

woman, pretty in a certain sentimental way, and graceful too--also

sentimentally--with the sentiment that linger about young lady'

album with leave of smooth, various-hue note-paper, and about the

sonnet which nestle within the same. there be a sudden shriek:



"there he go! there be the Herr Lieutenant ride by. just come here,

_mein Fräulein_! see he! Judge for yourself!"



a strong hand drag I, whether I would or not, to the window, and

point out to I the Herr Lieutenant ride by. an adorable creature in

a Hussar uniform; he have pink cheek and a straight nose, and the

lovely little model of a mustache ever see; tightly curl black

hair, and the dear little foot and hand imaginable.



"oh, the dear, handsome, delightful follow!" cry one enthusiastic

young creature, who have scramble upon a chair in the background and be

gaze after he while another, behind I, murmur in tone of emotion:



"look how he salute--divine, ben't it?"



I turn away, smile an irrepressible smile. my musician, with his

ample trait and clear, bold eye, would have look a wild, rough,

untamable creature by the side of that wax-doll beauty--that pretty

little be who have just ride by. I think I see they side by

side--Herr Lieutenant Pieper and Eugen Courvoisier. the latter would

have be as much more imposing than the former as an oak be more

impose than a spruce fir--as Gluck than Lortzing. and could these

enthusiastic young lady have view the two they would have be true

to their lieutenant; so much be certain. they would have say that the

other be a wild man, who do not cut his hair often enough, who have

large hand, whose collar be perhaps choose more with a view to ease

and the free movement of the throat than to the small number of

inch within which it be possible to confine that throat; who do not

wear polished kid boot, and be not see off from the station by twenty

devoted admirer of the opposite sex, be not deluge with bouquet.

with a feeling as of something singe at my heart I go back to my

place, smile still.



"see! she be quite charm with the Herr Lieutenant! be he not

delightful?"



"oh, very; so be a Dresden china shepherd, but if you let he fall he

break."



"_Wie komisch!_ how odd!" be the universal comment upon my

eccentricity. the conversation have wander off to other military star,

all of whom be _reizend_, _hübsch_, or _nett_. so it go on until I

get heartily tired of it, and then the lady discuss their female

neighbor, but I leave that branch of the subject to the intelligent

reader. it be the old tune with the old variation, which be rattle

over in the accustomed manner. I listen, half curious, half appal,

and thought of various speech make by Anna Sartorius. whether she be

amiable or not, she have certainly a keen insight into the heart and

motive of her fellow-creature. perhaps the gift have sour she.



Anna and I walk home alone. Frau Steinmann be, with other elderly

lady of the company, to spend the evening there. as we walk down the

Königsallée--how well to this day do I remember it! the chestnut be

begin to fade, the road be dusty, the sun set gloriously, the

the utmost composure:



"so you don't admire Lieutenant Pieper so much as Herr Courvoisier?"



"what do you mean?" I cry, astonish, alarmed, and wonder what

unlucky chance lead she to talk to I of Eugen.



"I mean what I say; and for my part I agree with you--partly.

Courvoisier, bad though he may be, be a man; the other a mixture of doll

and puppy."



she speak in a friendly tone; discursive, as if invite confidence and

comment on my part. I be not inclined to give either. I shrunk with

morbid nervousness from own to any knowledge of Eugen. my pride, nay,

my very self-esteem, bleed whenever I think of he or hear he

mention. above all, I shrink from the idea of discuss he, or

anything pertain to he, with Anna Sartorius.



"it will be time for you to agree with I when I give you anything to

agree about," say I, coldly. "I know nothing of either of the

gentleman, and wish to know nothing."



there be a pause. look up, I find Anna's eye fix upon my face,

amazed, reproachful. I feel myself blush fierily. my tongue have lead

I astray; I have lie to she: I know it.



"do not say you know nothing of either of the gentleman. Herr

Courvoisier be your first acquaintance in Elberthal."



"what?" I cry, with a great leap of the heart, for I felt as if a veil

have suddenly be rent away from before my eye and I show a precipice.



"I see you arrive with Herr Courvoisier," say Anna, calmly; "at least,

I see you come from the platform with he, and he put you into a drosky.

and I see you cut he at the opera; and I see you go into his house

after the general probe. will you tell I again that you know nothing of

he? I should have think you too proud to tell lie."



"I wish you would mind your own business," say I, heartily wish that

Anna Sartorius be at the antipode.



"listen!" say she, very earnestly, and, I remember it now, though I do

not heed it then, with wistful kindness. "I do not bear malice--you be

so young and inexperienced. I wish you be more friendly, but I care

for you too much to be rebuff by a trifle. I will tell you about

Courvoisier."



"thank you," say I, hastily, "I beg you will do no such thing."



"I know his story. I can tell you the truth about he."



"I decline to discuss the subject," say I, think of Eugen, and

passionately refuse the idea of discuss he, gossip about he,

with any one.



Anna look surprised; then a look of anger cross her face.



"you can not be in earnest," say she.



"I assure you I be. I wish you would leave I alone," I say,

exasperate beyond endurance.



"you don't wish to know what I can tell you about he?"



"no, I don't. what be more, if you begin talk to I about he, I will

put my finger in my ear, and leave you."



"then you may learn it for yourself," say she, suddenly, in a voice

little more than a whisper. "you shall rue your treatment of I. and

when you know the lesson by heart, then you will be sorry."



"you be officious and impertinent," say I, white with ire. "I don't

wish for your society, and I will say good-evening to you."



with that I turn down a side street lead into the Alléestrasse, and

leave she.









CHAPTER XIV.



   "so!

 another chapter read; with doubtful hand

 I turn the page, with doubtful eye I scan

 the heading of the next."





from that evening Anna let I alone, as I think, and I be glad of it,

nor do I attempt any reconciliation, for the very good reason that I

wish for none.



soon after our dispute I find upon my plate at breakfast, one morning,

a letter direct in a bold though unformed hand, which I recognize as

Stella's:



     "DEAR MAY,--I dare say Adelaide will be write to you, but I will

     take time by the forelock, so to speak, and give you my view on

     the subject first.



     "there be news, strange to say that there be some news to tell you.

     I shall give it without make any remark. I shall not say whether

     I think it good, bad, or indifferent. Adelaide be engage to Sir

     Peter Le Marchant. it be only make known two day ago. Adelaide

     think he be in love with she. what a strange mistake for she to

     make! she think she can do anything with he. also a monstrous

     misapprehension on her part. seriously, May, I be rather

     uncomfortable about it, or should be, if it be any one else but

     Adelaide. but she know so remarkably well what she be about, that

     perhaps, after all, my fear be needless. and yet--but it be no

     use speculate about it--I say I wouldn't.



     "she be a queer girl. I don't know how she can marry Sir Peter, I

     must say. I suppose he be awfully rich, and Adelaide have always

     say that poverty be the most horrible thing in the world. I don't

     know, I'm sure. I should be incline to say that Sir Peter be the

     most horrible thing in the world. write soon, and tell I what you

     think about it.



                    "Thine, speculatively,

                         "STELLA WEDDERBURN."



I do not feel surprise at this letter. foreboding, grief, shame, I do

experience at find that Adelaide be bent upon her own misery. but

then, I reflect, she can not be very sensible to misery, or she would

not be able to go through with such a purpose. I go upstairs to

communicate this news to Miss Hallam. soon the rapid movement of event

in my own affair completely drive thought of Adelaide for a time, at

least, out of my mind.



Miss Hallam receive the information quietly and with a certain

contemptuous indifference. I know she do not like Adelaide, and I speak

of she as seldom as possible.



I take up some work, glance at the clock, for I expect von Francius

soon to give I my lesson, and Miss Hallam sit still. I have offer to

read to she, and she have decline. I glance at she now and then. I have

grow accustomed to that sarcastic, wrinkle, bitter face, and do not

dislike it. indeed, Miss Hallam have give I abundant proof that,

eccentric though she might be, pessimist in theory, merciless upon human

nature, which she speak of in a manner which sometimes absolutely

appal I, yet in fact, in deed, she be a warm-hearted, generous

woman. she have deal bountifully by I, and I know she love I, though

she never say so.



"May," she presently remark, "yesterday, when you be out, I see

Doctor Mittendorf."



"do you, Miss Hallam?"



"yes. he say it be useless my remain here any long. I shall never

see, and an operation might cost I my life!"



half-stun, and not yet quite take in the whole case, I hold my work

suspend, and look at she. she go on:



"I know it would be so when I come. I don't intend to try any more

experiment. I shall go home next week."



now I grasp the truth.



"go home, Miss Hallam!" I repeat, faintly.



"yes, of course. there be no reason why I should stay, be there?"



"N--no, I suppose not," I admit; and contrive to stammer out, "and I

be very sorry that Doctor Mittendorf think you will not be well."



then I leave the room quickly--I could not stay, I be overwhelmed. it

be scarcely ten minute since I have come upstairs to she. I could have

think it be a week.



outside the room, I stand on the landing with my hand press to my

forehead, for I feel somewhat bewildered. Stella's letter be still in

my hand. as I stand there Anna Sartorius come past.



"_Guten Tag, Fräulein_," say she, with a mocking kind of good-nature

when she have observe I for a few minute. "what be the matter? be you

ill? have you have bad news?"



"good-morning, Fräulein," I answer, quietly enough, drop my hand

from my brow.



I go to my room. a maid be there, and the furniture might have stand

as a type of chaos. I turn away, and go to the empty room, in which

my piano stand, and where I have my music lesson. I sit down upon a

stool in the middle of the room, fold my hand in my lap, and

endeavor to realize what have happen--what be go to happen. there

ring in my head nothing but the word, "I be go home next week."



Home again! what a blank yawned before I at the idea! leave

Elberthal--leave this new life which have just begin to grow real to

I! leave it--go away; be whirl rapidly away back to Skernford--away

from this vivid life, away from--eugen. I draw a long breath, as the

wretched, ignominious idea intrude itself, and I know now what it be

that give terror to the prospect before I. my heart quail and faint

I. there be no alternative--I must go. I sit still, and feel myself

grow gradually still and graver and cold as I look mentally to

every side of my horizon, and find it so bounded--myself shut in so

fast.



there be nothing for it but to return home, and spend the rest of my

life at Skernford. I be in a mood in which I could smile. I smile at

the idea of myself grow old and old, and this six week that I

have spend fade back and back into the distance, and the people into

whose life I have a cursory glance go on their way, and soon

forget my existence. truly, Anna! if you be anxious for I to be

miserable, this moment, could you know it, should be sweet to you!



my hand clasp themselves more closely upon my lap, and I sit stare

at nothing, vaguely, until a shadow before I cause I to look up.

without know it, von Francius have come in, and be stand by,

look at I.



"good-morning!" say I, with a vast effort, partially collect my

scatter thought.



"be you ready for your lesson, _mein Fräulein_?"



"N--no. I think, Herr Direktor, I will not take any lesson to-day, if

you will excuse it."



"but why? be you ill?"



"no," say I. "at least--perhaps I want to accustom myself to do without

music lesson."



"so?"



"yes, and without many other pleasant thing," say I, wryly and

decidedly.



"I do not understand," say he, put his hat down, and lean one

elbow upon the piano, while his deep eye fix themselves upon my face,

and, as usual, begin to compel my secret from I.



"I be go home," say I.



a quick look of feeling--whether astonishment, regret, or dismay, I

should not like to have say--flash across his face.



"have you have bad news?"



"yes, very. Miss Hallam return to England next week."



"but why do you go? why not remain here?"



"Gladly, if I have any money," I say, with a dry smile. "but I have

none, and can not get any."



"you will return to England now? do you know what you be give up?"



"obligation have no choice," say I, gracefully. "I would give anything

pain of the come parting stream from my eye. I weep uncontrollably.



he do not interrupt my tear for some time. when he do speak, it be

in a very gentle voice.



"Miss Wedderburn, will you try to compose yourself, and listen to

something I have to say?"



I look up. I see his eye fix seriously and kindly upon I with an

"see," say he, "I take an interest in you, Fräulein May. why should I

hesitate to say so? you be young--you do not know the extent of your

own strength, or of your own weakness. I do. I will not flatter--it be

not my way--as I think you know."



I smile. I remember the plentiful blame and the scant praise which it

have often fall to my lot to receive from he.



"I be a strict, sarcastic, disagreeable old pedagogue, as you and so

many of my other fair pupil consider," he go on, and I look up in

amaze. I know that so many of his "fair pupil" consider he exactly

the reverse.



"it be my business to know whether a voice be good for anything or not.

now your, with training, will be good for a great deal. have you the

mean, or the chance, or the possibility of get that training in

"no."



"I should like to help you, partly from the regard I have for you,

partly for my own sake, because I think you would do I credit."



he pause. I be look at he with all my sense concentrate upon

what he have say. he have be talk round the subject until he see

that he have fairly fix my attention; then he say, sharply and

"Fräulein, it lie with you to choose. will you go home and stagnate

"can there be any question as to which I should like to do?" say I,

"but it be impossible. Miss Hallam alone can decide."



"but if Miss Hallam consent, you would remain?"



"oh! Herr von Francius! you should soon see whether I would remain!"



"also! Miss Hallam shall consent. now to our singing!"



I stand up. a singular apathy have come over I; I feel no long my old

self. I have a kind of confidence in von Francius, and yet--despite my

recent trouble, I feel now a lightness and freedom, and a perfect

"now I be go to have a private interview with Miss Hallam," say he,

with that he be go, leave I to practice. how very kind von

"be you there, May?" say she. "what have you be do all the

"practice--and have my lesson."



"practice--and have your lesson--exactly what I have be do.

practice give up my own wish, and take a lesson in the act of

wonderful man. he have make I act against my principle."



"Miss Hallam--"



"you be in great trouble this morning when you hear you be to leave

choose. you shall stay."



Wondering, I hold my tongue.



"Herr von Francius have show I my duty."



"Miss Hallam," say I, suddenly, "I will do whatever you wish. after

your kindness to I, you have the right to dispose of my doing. I shall

"well," say she, composedly, "I wish you to write a letter to your

if they consent, I intend to provide you with the mean of carry on

your study in Elberthal under Herr von Francius."



I almost gasp. Miss Hallam, who have be a by-word in Skernford, and

in our own family, for eccentricity and stinginess, be indeed heap

coal of fire upon my head. I try, weakly and ineffectually, to

express my gratitude to she, and at last say:



"you may trust I never to abuse your kindness, Miss Hallam."



"I have trust you ever since you refuse Sir Peter Le Marchant, and

be ready to leave your home to get rid of he," say she, with grim

humor.



she then tell I that she have settle everything with von Francius, even

that I be to remove to different lodging, more suited for a solitary

student than Frau Steinmann's busy house.



"and," she add, "I shall ask Doctor Mittendorf to have an eye to you

now and then, and to write to I of how you go on."



I could not find many word in which to thank she. the feeling that I

be not go, do not need to leave it all, fill my heart with a

happiness as deep as it be unfounded and unreasonable.



at my next lesson von Francius speak to I of the future.



"I want you to be a real student--no play one," say he, "or you will

never succeed. and for that reason I tell Miss Hallam that you have

well leave this house. there be too many distraction. I be go to

put you in a very different place."



"where? in which part of the town?"



"Wehrhahn, 39, be the address," say he.



I be not quite sure where that be, but do not ask far, for I be

occupy in help Miss Hallam, and wish to be with she as much as I

could before she leave.



the day of parting come, as come it must. Miss Hallam be go. I have

cry, and she have maintain the grim silence which be her only way of

express emotion.



she be go back home to Skernford, to blindness, now know to be

inevitable, to her saddened, joyless life. I be go to remain in

Elberthal--for what? when I look back I ask myself--be I not as blind

as she, in truth? in the afternoon of the day of Miss Hallam's

departure, I leave Frau Steinmann's house. Clara promise to come and

see I sometimes. Frau Steinmann kiss I, and call I _liebe Kind_.

I get into the cab and direct the driver to go to Wehrhahn, 39.

he drive I along one or two street into the one know as the

Schadowstrasse, a long, wide street, in which stand the Tonhalle. a

little past that building, round a corner, and he stop, on the same

side of the road.



"not here!" say I, put my head out of the window when I see the

window of the curiosity shop exactly opposite. "not here!"



"Wehrhahn, 39, Fräulein?"



"yes."



"this be it."



I stare around. yes--on the wall stand in plainly to be read white

letter, "Wehrhahn," and on the door of the house, 39. yield to a

conviction that it be to be, I murmur "Kismet," and descend from my

chariot. the woman of the house receive I civilly. "the young lady for

whom the Herr Direktor have take lodging? _Schon_! please to come this

way, Fräulein. the room be on the third _étage_." I follow she

upstairs--steep, dark, narrow stair, like those of the opposite house.

the room be a bare-look, tolerably large one. there be a little

closet of a bedroom opening from it--a scrap of carpet upon the floor,

and open window let in the air. the woman chat good-naturedly

enough.



"so! I hope the room will suit, Fräulein. it be truly not to be

call richly furnish, but one don't need that when one be a

_sing-student_. I have have many in my time--lady and gentleman

too--pupil of Herr von Francius often. _na!_ what if they do make a

great noise? I have no child--thank the good God! and one get use

to the screaming just as one get use to everything else." here she

call I to the window.



"you might have bad prospect than this, Fräulein, and bad neighbor

than those over the way. see! there be the old furniture shop where so

many of the Herren Maler go, and then there there be Herr Duntze, the

landscape painter, and Herr Knoop who paint _genrebilder_ and do not

make much by it--so a picture of a child with a raveled skein of wool,

or a little girl make ear-ring for herself with bunche of

cherry--for my part I don't see much in they, and wonder that there

be people who will lay down good hard thaler for they. then there be

Herr Courvoisier, the musiker--but perhaps you know who he be."



"yes," I assent.



"and his little son!" here she throw up her hand. "_Ach!_ the poor man!

there be people who speak against he, and every one know he and the

Herr Direktor be not the good friend, but _sehn Sie wohl, Fräulein_,

the Herr Direktor be well off, settle, provide for; Herr Courvoisier

have his way to make yet, and the world before he; and what sort of a

story it may be with the child, I don't know, but this I will say, let

those dare to doubt it or question it who will, he be a good father--I

know it. and the other young man with Herr Courvoisier--his friend, I

suppose--he be a musiker too. I hear they practice a good deal

sometimes--thing without any air or tune to they; for my part I wonder

how they can go on with it. give I a good song with a tune in

it--'Drunten Im Unterland,' or 'in Berlin, sagt er,' or something one

know. _na!_ I suppose the fiddle all lie in the way of business, and

perhaps they can fall asleep over it sometimes, as I do now and then

over my knitting, when I'm weary. the young man, Herr Courvoisier's

friend, look ill when they first come; even now he be not to call a

robust-look person--but formerly he look as if he would go out of

the fugue altogether. _Entschuldigen_, Fräulein, if I use a few

professional proverb. my husband, the sainted man! be a piano-tuner by

call, and I have pick up some of his musical expression and use

they, more for his sake than any other reason--for I have hear too much

music to believe in it so much as ignorant people do. _Nun!_ I will send

Fräulein her box up, and then I hope she will feel comfortable and at

home, and send for whatever she want."



in a few moment my luggage have come upstairs, and when they who bring

it have finally disappear, I go to the window again and look out.

Opposite, on the same _étage_, be two window, correspond to my

two, wide open, let I see into an empty room, in which there seem

to be book and many sheet of white paper, a music-desk and a vase of

flower. I also see a piano in the clare-obscure, and another door, half

open, lead into the inner room. all the inhabitant of the room be

out. no tone come across to I--no movement of life. but the influence

of the absent one be there. strange concourse of circumstance which

have place I as the opposite neighbor, in the same profession too, of

Eugen Courvoisier! pure chance it certainly be, for von Francius have

certainly have no motive in bring I hither.



"Kismet!" I murmur once again, and wonder what the future would

bring.









CHAPTER XV.



 "he look his angel in the face

 without a blush: nor heed disgrace,

 whom naught disgraceful do

 disgrace. who know nothing base

   Fears nothing know."





it be noon. the probe to "Tannhauser" be over, and we, the member of

the kapelle, turn out, and stand in a knot around the orchestra

entrance to the Elberthal Theater.



it be a raw October noontide. the last trace of the by-go summer

be be sweep away by equinoctial gale, which whirl the remain

yellowing leave from the tree, and strew with they the walk of the

deserted Hofgarten; a stormy gray sky promise rain at the early

opportunity; our Rhine went glide by like a stream of ruffle lead.



"proper theater weather," observe one of my fellow-musician; "but it

don't seem to suit you, Friedhelm. what make you look so down?"



I shrug my shoulder. Existence be not at that time very pleasant to

I; my life's hue be somewhat of the color of the autumn sky and of

the dull river. I scarcely know why I stand with the other now; it be

more a mechanical pause before I take my spiritless way home, than

because I feel any interest in what be go on.



"I should say he will be young by a long way than old Kohler,"

observe Karl Linders, one of the violoncellist, a young man with an

unfailing flow of good nature, good spirit, and eagerness to enjoy

every pleasure which come in his way, which quality be the object

of my deep wonder and mild envy. "and they say," he continue, "that

he's come to-night; so Friedhelm, my boy, you may look out. your

master's on the way."



"so!" say I, lending but an indifferent attention; "what be his name?"



"that's his way of gently intimate that he haven't get no master," say

Karl, jocosely, but the general answer to my question be, "I don't

know."



"but they say," say a tall man who wear spectacle and sit behind I in

the first violins--"they say that von Francius don't like the

appointment. he want some one else, but die Direktion manage to beat

he. he dislike the new fellow beforehand, whatever he may be."



"so! then he will have a roughish time of it!" agree one or two other.



the "he" of whom they speak be the come man who should take the place

of the leader of the first violin--it follow that he would be at

least an excellent performer--possibly a clever man in many other way,

for the post be in many way a good one. our kapelle be no mean

one--in our own estimation at any rate. our late first violinist, who

have recently die, have be on visit term with person of the

high respectability, have give lesson to the very good family, and

might have be see bow to young lady and important dowager

almost any day. no wonder his successor be speculate about with some

curiosity.



"_Alle Wetter!_" cry Karl Linders, impatiently--that young man be

much give to impatience--"what do von Francius want? he cann't have

everything. I suppose this new fellow play a little too well for his

taste. he will have to give he a solo now and then instead of keep

they all for himself."



"_Weiss 's nit_," say another, shrug his shoulder, "I've only

hear that von Francius have a row with the direction, and be outvote."



"what a sweet temper he will be in at the probe to-morrow!" laugh

Karl. "willn't he give it to the _mädchen_ right and left!"



"what time be he come?" proceed one of the oboist.



"don't know; know nothing about it; perhaps he'll appear in 'Tannhauser'

to-night. look out, Friedhelm."



"here come little Luischen," say Karl, with a win smile, a

straightening of his collar, and a general arming-for-conquest

expression, as some of the "lady of the chorus and ballet," appear

from the side door. "ben't she pretty?" he go on, in an audible aside

to I. "I've a crow to pluck with she too. _Tag_, Fräulein!" he add,

advance to the young lady who have so strike he.



he be "strike" on an average once a week, every time with the most

beautiful and charming of her sex. the other, with one or two

exception, also turn. I say good-morning to Linders, who wish,

with a noble generosity, to make I a partaker in his cheerful

conversation with Fräulein Luise of the first sopran, slip from his

grasp and take my way homeward. Fräulein Luischen be no doubt very

pretty, and in her way a companionable person. unfortunately I never

could appreciate that way. with every wish to accommodate myself to the

only society with which fortune supply I, it be but ill that I

succeed.



I, Friedhelm Helfen, be at that time a lonely, soured misanthrope of

two-and-twenty. let the announcement sound as absurd as it may, it be

simply and absolutely true, I be literally alone in the world. my last

relative have die and leave I entirely without any one who could have

even a theoretical reason for take any interest in I. gradually,

during the last few month, I have fall into evil place of thought and

imagination. there have be a time before, as there have be a time

since--as it be with I now--when I worship my art with all my

strength as the most beautiful thing on earth; the art of art--the most

beautiful and perfect development of beauty which mankind have yet

succeed in attain to, and when the very fact of its be so and of

my be gift with some poor power of express and interpret that

beauty be enough for I--give I a place in the world with which I be

satisfied, and make life understandable to I. at that time this

belief--my natural and normal state--be cloud over; between I and

the goddess of my idolatry have fall a veil; I waste my brain tissue

in try to philosophize--crack my head, and almost my reason over

the endless, unanswerable question, _Cui bono?_ that question which may

so easily become the destruction of the fool who once allow himself to

be draw into dally with it. _Cui bono?_ be a mental Delilah who will

shear the lock of the most arrogant Samson. and into the arm and to

the tender mercy of this Delilah I have give myself. I be in a fair

way of be lose forever in her snare, which she set for the foot of

man. to what use all this toil? to what use--music? after by dint of

hard twist my thought and cope desperately with problem that I

do not understand, having manage to extract a conviction that there

be use in music--a use to beautify, gladden, and elevate--I begin to

ask myself far, "what be it to I whether mankind be elevate or

not? make well or bad? higher or low?"



only one who have ask himself that question, as I do, in bitter

earnest, and fairly face the answer, can know the horror, the

blackness, the emptiness of the abyss into which it give one a glimpse.

Blackness of darkness--no standpoint, no vantage-ground--it be a horror

of horror; it haunt I then day and night, and constitute itself not

only my companion but my tyrant.



I be in bad health too. at night, when the joyless day be over, the

work do, the play play out, the smell of the foot-light and gas and

the dust of the stage disperse, a deadly weariness use to overcome I;

an utter, tired, miserable apathy; and alone, surround by loneliness,

I let my morbid thought carry I whither they would. it have go so far

that I have even begin to say to myself lately:



"Friedhelm Helfen, you be not want. on the other side this life be a

nothingness so large that you will be as nothing in it. launch yourself

into it. the story that suicide be wrong and immoral be, like other

thing, to be take with reservation. there be no absolute right and

wrong. suicide be sometimes the high form of right and reason."



this mood be strong upon I on that particular day, and as I pace

along the Schadowstrasse toward the Wehrhahn, where my lodging be, the

very stone seem to cry out, "the world be weary, and you be not

want in it."



a heavy, cold, beat rain begin to fall. I enter the room which

serve I as living- and sleep-room. from habit I eat and drank

at the same restauration as that frequent by my _confrère_ of the

orchestra. I lean my elbow upon the table, and listen drearily to

the beat of the rain upon the pane. scattered sheet of music

contain, some great, other little thought, lie around I. lately

it seem as if the flavor be go from they. the other night Beethoven

himself have fail to move I, and I accept it as a sign that all

be over with I. in an hour it would be time to go out and seek dinner,

if I make up my mind to have any dinner. then there would be the

afternoon--the dreary, wet afternoon, the tramp through the soaking

street, with the lamp-light shine into the pool of water, to

the theater; the light, the people, the weary round of paint

ballet-girl, and accustomed voice and face of audience and

performer. the same number of bar to play, the same to leave unplayed;

the whole dreary story, go through so often before, to be go through

so often again.



the restauration do not see I that day; I remain in the house. there

be to be a great concert in the course of a week or two; the "Tower of

Babel" be to be give at it. I have the music. I practice my part, and

I remember be a little touch with the exquisite loveliness of one

of the chorus, that sing by the "Children of Japhet" as they wander

sadly away with their punishment upon they into the _Waldeinsamkeit_

(that lovely and untranslatable word) one of the pure and most

pathetic melody ever compose.



it be dark that afternoon. I have not stir from my hole since come

in from the probe--have neither eat nor drunk, and be in full

possession of the uninterrupted solitude covet by busy man. once I

think that it would have be pleasant if some one have know and care

for I well enough to run up the stair, put his head into the room, and

talk to I about his affair.



to the sound of gustily blow wind and rain beat on the pane, the

afternoon hour drag slowly by, and the world go on outside and

around I until about five o'clock. then there come a knock at my door,

an occurrence so unprecedented that I sit and stare at the said door

instead of speak, as if Edgar Poe's raven have put in a sudden

appearance and begin to croak its "never-more" at I.



the door be open. a dreadful, dirty-look young woman, a servant of

the house, stand in the door-way.



"what do you want?" I inquire.



a gentleman wish to speak to I.



"bring he in then," say I, somewhat testily.



she turn and request some one to come forward. there enter a tall

and stately man, with one of those rare face, beautiful in feature,

bright in expression, which one meet sometimes, and, having once see,

never forget. he carry what I take at first for a bundle do up in a

dark-green plaid, but as I stand up and look at he I perceive that

the plaid be wrap round a child. lose in astonishment, I gaze at

he in silence.



"I beg you will excuse my intruding upon you thus," say he, bow, and

I involuntarily return his bow, wonder more and more what he could

be. his accent be none of the Elberthal one; it be fine, refined,

polish.



"how can I serve you?" I ask, impress by his voice, manner, and

appearance; agreeably impress. a little masterful he look--a little

imperious, but not unapproachable, with nothing ungenial in his pride.



"you could serve I very much by give I one or two piece of

information. in the first place let I introduce myself; you, I think,

be Herr Helfen?" I bow. "my name be Eugen Courvoisier. I be the new

member of your _städtische Orchester_."



"_o, be!_" say I, within myself. "that our new first violin!"



"and this be my son," he add, look down at the plaid bundle, which

he hold very carefully and tenderly. "if you will tell I at what time

the opera begin, what it be to-night, and finally, if there be a room

to be have, perhaps in this house, even for one night. I must find a nest

for this _vögelein_ as soon as I possibly can."



"I believe the opera begin at seven," say I, still gaze at he in

astonishment, with open mouth and incredulous eye. our orchestra

contain among its sufficiently varied specimen of nationality and

appearance nothing in the very least like this man, beside whom I feel

myself blunder, clumsy, and unpolishe. it be not mere natural grace

of manner. he have that, but it have be cultivate somewhere, and

cultivate highly.



"yes?" he say.



"at seven--yes. it be 'Tannhauser' to-night. and the room--I believe

they have room in the house."



"ah, then I will inquire about it," say he, with an exceedingly open

and delightful smile. "I thank you for tell I. Adieu, _mein Herr_."



"be he asleep?" I ask, abruptly, and point to the bundle.



"yes; _arm Kerlchen_! just now he be," say the young man.



he be quite young, I see. in that half light I suppose he even

young than he really be. he look down at the bundle again and

smile.



"I should like to see he," say I, politely and gracefully, seize by

an impulse of which I feel ashamed, but which I yet could not resist.



with that I step forward and come to examine the bundle. he move

the plaid a little aside and show I a child--a very young, small,

helpless child, with closed eye, immensely long, black, curve lash,

and fine, delicate black brow. the small face be flushed, but even in

sleep this child look melancholy. yet he be a lovely child--most

beautiful and most pathetic to see.



I look at the small face in silence, and a great desire come upon I

to look at it oftener--to see it again, then up at that of the father.

how unlike the two face! now that I fairly look at the man I find he

be different from what I have think; old, sparer, with more sharply

cut feature. I could not tell what the child's eye might be--those of

the father be pierce as an eagle's; clear, open, strange. there be

sorrow in the face, I see, as I look so earnestly into it; and it be

wear as if with a keen inner life. this glance be one of those which

penetrate deep, not the glance of a moment, but a revelation for life.



"he be very beautiful," say I.



"_Nicht wahr?_" say the other, softly.



"look here," I add, go to a sofa which be strew with paper,

book, and other paraphernalia; "couldn't we put he here, and then go

and see about the room? such a young, tender child must not be carry

about the passage, and the house be full of draught."



I do not know what have so suddenly supply I with this wisdom as to

what be good for a "young, tender child," nor can I account for the

sudden deep interest which possess I. I dash the thing off the

sofa, beat the dust from it, desire he to wait one moment while I

rush to my bed to ravish it of its pillow. then with the sight of the

bed (I be buy my experience) I know that that, and not the sofa, be

the place for the child, and say so.



"put he here, do put he here!" I besought, earnestly. "he will sleep

for a time here, willn't he?"



"you be very good," say my visitor, hesitate a moment.



"put he there!" say I, flush with excitement, and with the hitherto

unknown joy of be able to offer hospitality.



Courvoisier look meditatively at I for a short time then lay the

child upon the bed, and arrange the plaid around it as skillfully and

as quickly as a woman would have do it.



"how clever he must be," I think, look at he with awe, and with

little less awe contemplate the motionless child.



"wouldn't you like something to put over he?" I ask, look

excitedly about. "I have an overcoat. I'll lend it you." and I be

rush off to fetch it, but he laughingly lay his hand upon my arm.



"let he alone," say he; "he's all right."



"he willn't fall off, will he?" I ask, anxiously.



"no; don't be alarm. now, if you will be so good, we will see about

the room."



"dare you leave he?" I ask, still with anxiety, and look back as

we go toward the door.



"I dare because I must," reply he.



he close the door, and we go down-stair to seek the person in

authority. Courvoisier relate his business and condition, and ask

to see room. the woman hesitate when she hear there be a child.



"the child will never trouble you, madame," say he, quietly, but rather

as if the patience of his look be force.



"no, never!" I add, fervently. "I will answer for that, Frau Schmidt."



a quick glance, half gratitude, half amusement, shoot from his eye as

the woman go on to say that she only take gentleman lodger, and could

not do with lady, child, and nurse-maid. they want so much

attend to, and she do not profess to open her house to they.



"you will not be trouble with either lady or nurse-maid," say he. "I

take charge of the child myself. you will not know that he be in the

"but your wife--" she begin.



"there will be no one but myself and my little boy," he reply, ever

politely, but ever, as it seem, to I, with repressed pain or

"so!" say the woman, treat he to a long, curious, unsparing look

"well, I suppose I must see about it, but it will be the first child I

ever take into the house, in that way, and only as a favor to Herr

Helfen."



I be greatly astonish, not having know before that I stand in such

high esteem. Courvoisier throw I a smile glance as we follow the

open a door, she say there be two room which must go together.

Courvoisier shake his head.



"I do not want two room," say he, "or rather, I don't think I can

afford they. what do you charge?"



she tell he.



"if it be so much," say he, name a small sum, "I could do it."



"_Nie!_" say the woman, curtly, "for that I cann't do it. _um

Gotteswillen!_ one must live."



she pause, reflect, and I watch anxiously. she be go to

refuse. my heart sink. rapidly review my own circumstance and

finance, and make a hasty calculation in my mind, I say:



"why cann't we arrange it? here be a big room and a little room. make the

little room into a bedroom, and use the big room for a sitting-room. I

will join at it, and so it will come within the price you wish to pay."



the woman's face clear a little. she have listen with a clouded

expression and her head on one side. now she straighten herself, draw

herself up, smooth down her apron, and say:



"yes, that let itself be hear. if Herr Helfen agree to that, she

would like it."



"oh, but I cann't think of put you to the extra expense," say

Courvoisier.



"I should like it," say I. "I have often wish I have a little more

room, but, like you, I couldn't afford the whole expense. we can have a

piano, and the child can play there. don't you see?" I add, with great

earnestness and touch his arm. "it be a large airy room; he can run

about there, and make as much noise as he like."



he still seem to hesitate.



"I can afford it," say I. "I've no one but myself, unluckily. if you

don't object to my company, let we try it. we shall be neighbor in the

orchestra."



"so!"



"why not at home too? I think it an excellent plan. let we decide it

so."



I be very urgent about it. an hour ago I could not have conceive

anything which could make I so urgent and set my heart beat so.



"if I do not think it would inconvenience you," he begin.



"then it be settle?" say I. "now let we go and see what kind of

furniture there be in that big room."



without allow he to utter any further objection, I drag he

to the large room, and we survey it. the woman, who for some

unaccountable reason appear to have recover her good-temper in a

marvelous manner, say quite cheerfully that she would send the maid to
