smile, and then leave we. the big room be long, low, and rather dark.

Beams be across the ceiling, and two not very large window look

upon the street below, across to two similar window of another

lodge-house, a little to the left of which be the Tonhalle. the floor

be carpetless, but clean; there be a big square table, and some

chair.



"there," say I, draw Courvoisier to the window, and point across:

"there be one scene of your future exertion, the Städtische Tonhalle."



"so!" say he, turn away again from the window--it be as dark as

ever outside--and look round the room again. "this be a dull-look

place," he add, gaze around it.



"we'll soon make it different," say I, rub my hand and gaze

round the room with avidity. "I have long wish to be able to inhabit

this room. we must make it more cheerful, though, before the child come

to it. we'll have the stove light, and we'll knock up some shelf

and we'll have a piano in, and the sofa from my room, _nicht wahr?_ oh,

we'll make a place of it, I can tell you."



he look at I as if strike with my enthusiasm, and I bustle about.

we set to work to make the room habitable. he be out for a short time

at the station and return with the luggage which he have leave there.

while he be away I steal into my room and take a good look at my new

treasure; he still sleep peacefully and calmly on. we be deep in

impromptu carpentering and contrivance for use and comfort, when it

occur to I to look at my watch.



"five minute to seven!" I almost yell, dash wildly into my room to

wash my hand and get my violin. Courvoisier follow I. the child be

awake. I feel a horrible sense of guilt as I see it look at I with

great, soft, solemn, brown eye, not in the least those of its father,

but it do not move. I say apologetically that I fear I have awaken

it.



"oh, no! he's be awake for some time," say Courvoisier. the child see

he, and stretch out its arm toward he.



"_na! jung Taugenichts!_" he say, take it up and kiss it. "Thou

must stay here till I come back. Wilt be happy till I come?"



the answer make by the mournful-look child be a singular one. it put

both tiny arm around the big man's neck, lay its face for a moment

against his, and loose he again. neither word nor sound do it emit

during the process. a feeling altogether new and astonishing overcame

I. I turn hastily away, and as I pick up my violin-case, be amazed

to find my eye dim. my visitor be something unprecedented to I.



"you be not compel to go to the theater to-night, you know, unless

you like," I suggest, as we go down-stair.



"thank, it be as well to begin at once."



on the low landing we meet Frau Schmidt.



"where be you go, _mein Herren_?" she demand.



"to work, madame," he reply, lift his cap with a courtesy which

seem to disarm she.



"but the child?" she demand.



"do not trouble yourself about he."



"be he asleep?"



"not just now. he be all right, though."



she give we a look which mean volume. I pull Courvoisier out.



"come along, do!" cry I. "she will keep you there for half an hour,

and it be time now."



we rush along the street too rapidly to have time or breath to speak,

and it be five minute after the time when we scramble into the

orchestra, and find that the overture be already begin.



though there be certainly not much time for observe one's fellow when

one be help in the overture to "Tannhauser," yet I see the many

curious and astonished glance which be cast toward our new member,

glance of which he take no notice, simply because he apparently do not

see they. he have the fine absence of self-consciousness that I ever

see.



the first act of the opera be over, and it fall to my share to make

Courvoisier know to his fellow-musician. I introduce he to the

director, who be not von Francius, nor any friend of his. then we

retire to one of the small room on one side of the orchestra.



"_Hundewetter!_" say one of the man, shiver. "have you travel far

to-day?" he inquire of Courvoisier, by way of open the conversation.



"from Köln only."



"live there?"



"no."



the man continue his catechism, but in another direction.



"be you a friend of Helfen's?"



"I rather think Helfen have be a friend to I," say Courvoisier,

smile.



"have you find lodging already?"



"yes."



"so!" say his interlocutor, rather puzzle with the new arrival. I

remember the scene well. half a dozen of the man be stand in one

corner of the room, smoking, drink beer, and laugh over some not

very brilliant joke; we three be a little apart. courvoisi, stately

and impose-look, and with that fine manner of his, politely

answer his interrogator, a small, sharp-feature man, who look up

to he and rattle complacently away, while I sit upon the table among

the fiddle-case and beer-glass, my foot on a chair, my chin in

my hand, feel my cheek glow, and a strange sense of dizziness

and weakness all over I, a lightness in my head which I could not

understand. it have quite escape I that I have neither eat nor drunk

since my breakfast at eight o'clock, on a cup of coffee and dry

_brödchen_, and it be now twelve hour later.



the pause be not a long one, and we return to our place. but

"Tannhauser" be not a short opera. as time go on my sensation of

illness and faintness increase. during the second pause I remain in

my place. Courvoisier presently come and sit beside I.



"I'm afraid you feel ill," say he.



I deny it. but though I struggle on to the end, yet at last a deadly

faintness overcome I. as the curtain go down amid the applause,

everything reel around I. I hear the bustle of the other--of the

audience go away. I myself could not move.



"_be ist denn mit ihm?_" I hear Courvoisier say as he stoop over I.



"be that Friedhelm Helfen?" ask Karl Linders, survey I. "_potz

blitz!_ he look like a corpse! he's be at his old trick again,

starve himself. I expect he have touch nothing the whole day."



"let's get he out and give he some brandy," say Courvoisier. "lend

he an arm, and I'll give he one on this side."



together they haul I down to the retire-room.



"_ei!_ he want a schnapps, or something of the kind," say Karl, who

seem to think the whole affair an excellent joke. "look here, _alter

Narr!_" he add; "you've be go without anything to eat, _nicht_?"



"I believe I have," I assent, feebly. "but I'm all right; I'll go

home."



reject Karl's press entreaty to join he at supper at his

favorite Wirthschaft, we go home, purchase our supper on the way.

Courvoisier's first step be toward the place where he have leave the

child. he be go.



"_Verschwunden!_" cry he, stride off to the sleep-room, whither I

follow he. the little lad have be undressed and put to bed in a

small crib, and be sleep serenely.



"that's Frau Schmidt, who cann't do with child and nurse-maid," say

I, laugh.



"it's very kind of she," say he, as he touch the child's cheek

slightly with his little finger, and then, without another word,

return to the other room, and we sit down to our long-delay supper.



"what on earth make you spend more than twelve hour without food?" he

ask I, lay down his knife and fork, and look at I.



"I'll tell you some time perhaps, not now," say I, for there have begin

to dawn upon my mind, like a sun-ray, the idea that life hold an

interest for I--two interest--a friend and a child. to a miserable,

lonely wretch like I, the idea be divine.









CHAPTER XVI.



 though nothing can bring back the hour

 of splendor in the grass, of glory in the flower.

 we will grieve not--rather find

 Strength in what remain behind;

 in the primal sympathy

 which having be, must ever be.

 in the soothe thought that spring

 out of human suffering!

 in the faith that look through death--

 in year, that bring the philosophic mind.

                WORDSWORTH.





from that October afternoon I be a man save from myself. Courvoisier

have say, in answer to my earnest entreaty about join housekeeping:

"we will try--you may not like it, and if so, remember you be at

liberty to withdraw when you will." the answer content I, because I

know that I should not try to withdraw.



our friendship progress by such quiet, imperceptible degree, each one

knot the past more closely and inextricably with the present, that I

could by no mean relate they if I wish it. but I do not wish it. I

only know, and be content with it, that it have fall to my lot to be

bless with that most precious of all earthly possession, the

"friend" that "sticketh close than a brother." our union have grow and

remain not merely "_f und treu_," but immovable, unshakable.



there be first the child. he be two year old; a strange, weird,

silent child, very beautiful--as the son of his father could scarcely

fail to be--but with a different kind of beauty. how still he be, and

how patient! not a fretful child, not give to cry or complaint; fond

of rest in one place, with solemn, thoughtful eye fix, when his

father be there, upon he; when his father be not there, upon the

strip of sky which be to be see, through the window above the

house-top.



the child's name be sigmund; he display a friendly disposition toward

I, indeed, he be passively friendly and--if one may say such a thing

of a baby--courteous to all he come in contact with. he have inherit

his father's polished manner; one see that when he grow up he would be a

"gentleman," in the fine outer sense of the word. his inner life he

keep conceal from we. I believe he have some method of communicate

his idea to eugen, even if he never speak. eugen never could conceal

his own mood from the child; it know--let he feign otherwise never so

cunningly--exactly what he feel, glad or sad, or between the two, and no

acting could deceive he. it be a strange, intensely interesting study

to I; one to which I daily return with fresh avidity. he would let I

take he in my arm and talk to he; would sometimes, after look at

I long and earnestly, break into a smile--a strange, grave, sweet

smile. then I could do no otherwise than set he hastily down and look

away, for so unearthly a smile I have never see. he be, though fragile,

not an unhealthy child; though so delicately form, and intensely

sensitive to nervous shock, have nothing of the coward in he, as be

prove to we in a thousand way; shiver through and through his little

frame at the sight of a certain picture to which he have take a great

antipathy, a picture which hang in the public gallery at the Tonhalle;

he hate it, because of a certain evil-look man portray in it; but

when his father, take his hand, say to he, "go, Sigmund, and look at

that man; I wish thee to look at he," go without turn or waver, and

gaze long and earnestly at the low type, bestial visage portray to

he. eugen have tread noiselessly behind he; I watch, and he

watch, how his two little fist clinch themselves at his side,

while his gaze never waver, never wander, till at last eugen, with a

strange expression, catch he in his arm and half kill he with

kiss.



"_Mein liebling!_" he murmur, as if utterly satisfied with he.



Courvoisier himself? there be a great many strong and positive

quality about this man, which in themselves would have set he

somewhat apart from other man. thus he have crotchety idea about truth

and honor, such as one might expect from so knightly look a

personage. it be Karl Linders, who, at a later period of our

acquaintance, amuse himself by chalk up, "Prinz Eugen, der edle

Ritter," beneath his name. his musical talent--or rather genius, it be

more than talent--be at that time not one fifth part know to I, yet

even what I see excited my wonder. but these, and a long list of other

active characteristic, all fade into insignificance before the

tower passion of his existence--his love for his child. it be

strange, it be touching, to see the bond between father and son. the

child's thought and word, as tell in his eye and from his lip,

form the man's philosophy. I believe eugen confide everything to his

boy. his first thought in the morning, his last at night, be for _der

Kleine_. his leisure be--I can not say "give up" to the boy--but it

be always pass with he.



Courvoisier soon gain a reputation among our comrade for be a sham

and a delusion. they say that to look at he one would suppose that no

more genial, jovial fellow could exist--there be kindliness in his

glance, _bon camaraderie_ in his voice, a genial, open, human

sympathetic kind of influence in his nature, and in all he do. "and

yet," say Karl Linders to I, with gesticulation, "one never can get

he to go anywhere. one may invite he, one may try to be friend with

he, but, no! off he go home! what do the fellow want at home? he

behave like a young miss of fifteen, whose governess willn't let her mix

with vulgar companion."



I laugh, despite myself, at this tirade of Karl. so that be how

Eugen's behavior strike outsider!



"and you be every bit as bad as he be, and as soft--he have make you

so," go on Linders, vehemently. "it ben't right. you two ought to be

leader outside as well as in, but you walk yourself away, and stay at

home! at home, indeed! let green gosling and grandfather stay at

home."



indeed, Herr Linders be not a person who trouble home much; spend

his time between morning and night between the theater and concert-room,

restauration and verein.



"what do you do at home?" he ask, irately.



"that's our concern, _mein lieber_," say I, composedly, think of

young Sigmund, whose existence be unknown except to our two self, and

laugh.



"be you compose a symphony? or an opera buffa? you might tell a

fellow."



I laugh again, and say we lead a peaceable life, as honest citizen

should; and add, lay my hand upon his shoulder, for I have more of a

lean toward Karl, scamp though he be, than to any of the other,

"you might do bad than follow our example, old fellow."



"bah!" say he, with unutterable contempt. "I'm a man; not a milksop.

besides, how do I know what your example be? you say you behave

yourself; but how be I to know it? I'll drop upon you unawares and

catch you, some time. see if I don't."



the next evening, by a rare chance with we, be a free one--there be no

opera and no concert; we have have probe that morning, and be at liberty

to follow the device and desire of our own heart that evening.



these device and desire lead we straight home, follow by a sneer

laugh from Herr Linders, which vastly amuse I. the year be draw to

a close. Christmas be nigh; the weather be cold and unfriendly. our

stove be light; our lamp burn pleasantly on the table; our big room

look homely and charming by these evening light. Master Sigmund be

wide awake in honor of the occasion, and sit upon my knee while his

father play the fiddle. I have not speak of his playing before--it

be, in its way, unique. it be not a violin that he play--it be a

spirit that he invoke--and a strange answer it sometimes give forth to

his summon. to-night he have take it up suddenly, and sit playing,

without book, a strange melody which wring my heart--full of minor

cadence, with an infinite wail and weariness in it. I close my eye

and listen. it be sad, but it be absorbing. when I open my eye

again and look down, I find that tear be run from Sigmund's

eye. he be sob quietly, his head against my breast.



"I say, Eugen! look here!"



"be he cry? poor little chap! he'll have a good deal to go through

before he have learn all his lesson," say eugen, lay down his

violin.



"what be that? I never hear it before."



"I have, often," say he, rest his chin upon his hand, "in the sound

of stream--in the rush of a crowd--upon a mountain--yes, even alone

with the woman I--" he break off abruptly.



"but never on a violin before?" say I, significantly.



"no, never."



"why don't you print some of those impromptus that you be always

make?" I ask.



he shrug his shoulder. Ere I could pursue the question some one

knock at the door, and in answer to our _herein!_ appear a handsome,

laugh face, and a head of wavy hair, which, with a tall, shapely

figure, I recognize as those of Karl Linders.



"I tell you fellow I'd hunt you up, and I always keep my word," say

he, composedly. "you cann't very well turn I out for call upon you."



he advance. courvoisier rise, and with a courteous cordiality offer

his hand and draw a chair up. Karl come forward, look round, smile

and chuckle at the success of his experiment, and as he come opposite

to I his eye fall upon those of the child, who have raise his head and

be stare gravely at he.



never shall I forget the start--the look of amaze, almost of fear, which

shoot across the face of Herr Linders. amazement would be a weak word in

which to describe it. he stop, stand stock-still in the middle of the

room; his jaw fall--he gaze from one to the other of we in feeble

astonishment, then say, in a whisper:



"_donnerwetter!_ a child!"



"don't use bad language before the little innocent," say I, enjoy

his confusion.



"which of you do it belong to? be it he or she?" he inquire in an

awe-strike and alarmed manner.



"his name be Sigmund Courvoisier," say I, with difficulty preserve my

gravity.



"oh, indeed! I--I ben't aware--" begin Karl, look at Eugen in such a

peculiar manner--half respectful, half timid, half ashamed--that I could

no long contain my feeling, but burst into such a shout of laughter

as I have not enjoy for year. after a moment, Eugen join in; we

laugh peal after peal of laughter, while poor Karl stood feebly

look from one to the other of the company--speechless--crestfallen.



"I beg your pardon." he say, at last, "I willn't intrude any long.

Good--"



he be make for the door, but Eugen make a dash after he, turn he

round, and push he into a chair.



"sit down, man," say he, stifle his laughter. "sit down, man; do you

think the poor little chap will hurt you?"



Karl cast a distrustful glance sideways at my nursling and speak not.



"I'm glad to see you," pursue Eugen. "why don't you come before?"



at that Karl's lip begin to twitch with a humorous smile; presently he

too begin to laugh, and seem not to know how or when to stop.



"it beat all I ever see or hear or dream of," say he, at last.

"that's what bring you home in such a hurry every night. let I

congratulate you, Friedel! you make a first-rate nurse; when everything

else fail I will give you a character as _kindermädchen_; clean, sober,

industrious, and not give to run after young man." with which he

roar again, and Sigmund survey he with a somewhat severe, though

scarcely a disapprove, expression. Karl seat himself near he, and,

though not yet venture to address he, cast various glance of

blandishment and persuasion upon he.



half an hour pass thus, and a second knock be follow by the

entrance of Frau Schmidt.



"good evening, gentleman," she remark, in a tone which say

unutterable thing--scorn, contempt, pity--all finely blend into a

withering sneer, as she cast her eye around, and a slight but awful

smile play about her lip. "half past eight, and that bless

baby not in bed yet. I know how it would be. and you all smoking,

too--_natürlich!_ you ought to know well, Herr Courvoisier--you ought,

at any rate," she add, scorn drop into heart-pierce reproach.

"give he to I," she add, take he from I, and apostrophize he.

"you poor, blessed lamb! well for you that I'm here to look after you,

that have have child of my own, and know a little about the sort of

way that you ought to be bring up in."



evident sign of uneasiness on Karl's part, as Frau Schmidt, with the

same extraordinary contortion of the mouth--half smile, half

sneer--bring Sigmund to his father, to say good-night. that process

over, he be bring to I, and then, as if it be a matter which

"understand itself," to Karl. eugen and I, like family man, as we be,

have go through the ceremony with willing grace. Karl back his chair

a little, look much alarmed, shoot a queer glance at we, at the child,

and then appealingly up into the woman's face. we, through our smoke,

watch he.



"he look so very--very--" he begin.



"come, come, _mein Herr_, what do that mean? kiss the little angel,

and be thankful you may. the innocent! you ought to be delighted," say

she, stand with grenadier-like stiffness beside he.



"he willn't bite you, Karl," I say, reassuringly. "he's quite harmless."



thus encourage, Herr Linders stoop forward and touch the cheek of

the child with his lip; then, as if surprised, stroke it with his

finger.



"_Lieber Himmel!_ how soft! like satin, or rise leave!" he murmur, as

the woman carry the child away, shut the door and disappear.



"do she tackle you in that way every night?" he inquire next.



"every evening," say Eugen. "and I little dare open my lip before she.

you would notice how quiet I keep. it's because I be afraid of she."



Frau Schmidt, who have at first object so strongly to the advent of the

child, be now devoted to it, and would have resent exceedingly the

idea of allow any one but herself to put it to bed, dress or undress

it, or look after it in general. this state of thing have creep on very

gradually; she have never say how fond she be of the child, but put

her kindness upon the ground that as a christian woman she could not

stand by and see it mishandle by a couple of _man_, and oh! the

unutterable contempt upon the word "man." under this disguise she

attempt to cover the fact that she delight to have it with she, to

kiss it, fondle it, admire it, and "do for it." we know now that no

soon have we leave the house than the child would be bring down, and

would never leave the care of Frau Schmidt until our return, or until he

be in bed and asleep. she say he be a quiet child, and "do not give

so much trouble." indeed, the little fellow win a friend in whoever see

he. he have make another conquest to-night. Karl Linders, after puff

away for some time, inquire, with an affectation of indifference:



"how old be he--_der kleine Bengel_?"



"two--a little more."



"handsome little fellow!"



"Glad you think so."



"sure of it. but I don't know, Courvoisier--so sure as I live, I know

nothing about it!"



"I dare say not. do I ever say you do?"



I see that Karl wish to ask another question; one which have tremble

upon my own lip many a time, but which I have never ask--which I know

that I never should ask. "the mother of that child--be she alive or

dead? why may we never hear one word of she? why this silence, as of the

grave? be she your wife? do you love she? do she love you?"



question which could not fail to come to I, and about which my

thought would hang for hour. I could imagine a woman be very deeply

in love with Courvoisier. whether he would love very deeply himself,

whether love would form a mainspring of his life and action, or whether

it take only a secondary place--I speak of the love of woman--I could

not guess. I could decide upon many point of his character. he be a

good friend, a high-minded and a pure-minded man; his every-day life,

the turn of his thought and conversation, show I that as plainly as

any great adventure could have do. that he be an ardent musician, an

artist in the true and deepest sense, of a quixotically generous and

unselfish nature--all this I have already prove. that he love his child

with a love not short of passion be patent to I every day. but upon

the past, silence so utter as I never before meet with. not a hint; not

an allusion; not one syllable.



little Sigmund be not yet two and a half. the story upon which his

father maintain so deep a silence be not, could not, be a very old

one. his behavior give I no clew as to whether it have be a joyful or

a sorrowful one. mere silence could tell I nothing. some man be silent

about their grief; some about their joy. I know not in which direction

his disposition lay.



I see Karl look at he that evening once or twice, and I tremble lest

the blundering, good-natured fellow should make the mistake of ask

some question. but he do not; I need not have fear. People be not

in the habit of put obtrusive question to Eugen Courvoisier. the

danger be somehow quietly tide over, the delicate ground avoid.



the conversation wander quietly off to commonplace topic--the state

of the orchestra; tale of its doing; the temper of our different

conductor--Malperg of the opera; Woelff of the ordinary concert, which

take place two or three time a week, when we fiddle and the public

ate, drank, and listen; lastly, von Francius, _königlicher

Musik-direktor_.



Karl Linders give his opinion freely upon the man in authority. he have

nothing to do with they, nothing to hope or fear from they; he fill a

quiet place among the violoncellist, and have attain his twenty-eighth

year without display any violent talent or tendency to distinguish

himself, otherwise than by get as much mirth out of life as possible

and live in a perpetual state of "carlesse contente."



he desire to know what Courvoisier thought of von Francius; for

curiosity--the fault of those idle person who afterward develop into

busybody--be already begin to leave its trace on Herr Linders.

it be less known than guess that the state of thing between

Courvoisier and von Francius be less peace than armed neutrality. the

intense politeness of von Francius to his first violinist, and the

punctilious ceremoniousness of the latter toward his chief, be topic

of speculation and amusement to the whole orchestra.



"I think von Francius would be a fiend if he could," say Karl,

comfortably. "I wouldn't stand it if he speak to I as he speak to some

people."



"oh, they like it!" say Courvoisier; and Karl stare. "girl don't

object to a little bullying; anything rather than be leave quite alone,"

Courvoisier go on, tranquilly.



"girl!" ejaculate Karl.



"you mean the young lady in the chorus, don't you?" ask Courvoisier,

unmovedly. "he do bully they, I don't deny; but they come back again."



"oh, I see!" say Karl, accept the rebuff.



he have not refer to the young lady of the chorus.



"have you hear von Francius play?" he begin next.



"_Natürlich!_"



"what do you think of it?"



"I think it be superb!" say Courvoisier.



baffle again, Karl be silent.



"the power and the daring of it be grand," go on Eugen, heartily. "I

could listen to he for hour. to see he seat himself before the piano,

as if he be sit down to read a newspaper, and do what he do,

without move a muscle, be simply superb--there's no other word. other

man may play the piano; he take the key-board and play with it, and it

say what he like."



I look at he, and be satisfied. he find the same want in von

Francius' "superb" manipulation that I do--the glitter of a diamond,

not the glow of a fire.



Karl have not the subtlety to retort, "ay, but do it say what we like?"

he subside again, merely give a meek assent to the proposition, and

say, suggestively:



"he's not like, though he be such a popular fellow."



"the public be often a great fool."



"well, but you cann't expect it to kiss the hand that slap it in the

face, as von Francius do," say Karl, drive to metaphor, probably for

the first time in his life, and seeming astonish at having discover

a hitherto unknown mental property pertaining to himself.



Courvoisier laugh.



"I'm certain of one thing: von Francius will go on slap the public's

face. I willn't say how it will end; but it would not surprise I in the

least to see the public at his foot, as it be now at those of--"



"Humph!" say Karl, reflectively.



he do not stay much long, but having finish his cigar, rise. he

seem to feel very apologetic, and out of the fullness of his heart his

mouth spake.



"I really wouldn't have intrude if I have known--"



"know what?" inquire Eugen, with well-assume surprise.



"I think you be just by yourself, you know, and--"



"so we be; but we can do with other society. Friedel here get very

tedious sometimes--in fact, _langweilig_. come again, _nicht wahr_?"



"if I sha'n't be in your way," say Karl, look round the room with

somewhat wistful eye.



we assure he to the contrary, and he promise, with unnecessary

emphasis, to come again.



"he will return; I know he will!" say eugen, after he have go.



the next time that Herr Linders arrive, which be ere many day have

pass, he look excited and important; and after the first greeting

be over, he undo a great number of paper which wrap and infolde

a parcel of considerable dimension, and display to our enrapture

view of a white woolly animal of stupendous dimension, fasten upon a

green stand, which stand, when press, cause the creature to give

forth a howl like unto no lowing of oxen nor bleat of sheep ever

hear on earth. this inviting-look creature he hold forth toward

Sigmund, who stare at it.



"perhaps he's get one already?" say Karl, see that the child do not

display any violent enthusiasm about the treasure.



"oh, no!" say eugen, promptly.



"perhaps he don't know what it be," I suggest, rather unkindly,

scarcely able to keep my countenance at the idea of that baby playing

with such a toy.



"perhaps not," say Karl, more cheerfully, kneel down by my

side--Sigmund sit on my knee--and squeeze the stand, so that the

woolly animal howl. "_Sieh!_ sigmund! look at the pretty lamb!"



"oh, come, Karl! be you a lamb? call it an eagle at once," say I,

skeptically.



"it be a lamb, ben't it?" say he, turn it over. "they call it a

lamb at the shop."



"a very queer lamb; not a german breed, anyhow."



"now I think of it, my little sister have one, but she call it a rabbit,

I believe."



"very likely. you might call that anything, and no one could contradict

you."



"well, _der Kleine_ don't know the difference; it's a toy," say Karl,

desperately.



"not a toy that seem to take his fancy much," say I, as Sigmund, with

evident sign of displeasure, turn away from the animal on the green

stand, and refuse to look at it. Karl look despondent.



"he don't like the look of it," say he, plaintively.



"I think I be sure to be right in this. my little sister" (Karl's

little sister have certainly never be so often quote by her brother

before) "play for hour with that thing that she call a rabbit."



Eugen have come to the rescue, and grasp the woolly animal which Karl

have contemptuously throw aside. after convince himself by near

examination as to which be intend for head and which for tail, he

present it to his son, remark that it be "a pretty toy."



"I'll pray for you after that, eugen--often and earnestly," say I.

body, hold it to his side, and look at Eugen with a pathetic

expression.



"pretty plaything, _nicht wahr_?" say eugen, encouragingly.



Sigmund nod silently. the animal emit a howl; the child wince,

but look resign. eugen rise and stand at some little distance,

look on. Sigmund continue to embrace the animal with the same

resign expression, until Karl, stoop, take it away.



"you mustn't _make_ he, just because I bring it," say he. "well

luck next time. I see he's not a common child. I must try to think of

something else."



we command our countenance with difficulty, but preserve they.

Sigmund's feeling have be severely wound. for many day he eye Karl

with a strange, cold glance, which the latter use every art in his

Karl Linders.



we sit together one evening--Eugen and I, after Sigmund have be in bed

"the first night we meet, you faint away from exhaustion and long

so, but you have never keep your word."



"I don't care to eat. People eat to live--except those who live to eat,

and I be not very anxious to live, I don't care for my life, in fact,

I wish I be dead."



"why? an unlucky love?"



"_I, bewahre!_ I never know what it be to be in love in my life," say

I, with perfect truth.

"as true as possible. I think a timely love affair, however unlucky,

would have rouse I and bring I to my sense again."



"general melancholy?"



"oh, I be alone in the world. I have be read, reading, reading; my

brain be one dark and misty muddle of Kant, Schopenhauer, von Hartmann,

and a few other. I read they one after another, as quickly as possible;

the mixture have the same effect upon my mind as the indiscriminate

content of taffy-shop would have upon Sigmund's stomach--it make it

sick. in my crude, ungainly, unfinished fashion I turn over my

information, lay down big generalization upon a foundation of

experience of the small possible dimension, and all upon one side."



he nod. "_ei!_ I know it."



"and after consider the state of the human race--that be to say the

half dozen people I know, and the misery of the human lot as set forth

in the book I have read, and having prove to myself, all up in that

nor could be heaven or hell or any future state, and having decide,

also from that room, that there be no place for I in the world, and

that I be very likely actually fill the place of some other man,

poor than I be, and able to think life a good thing" (Eugen be

the good thing I could do be to leave the world."



"be you go to starve yourself to death? that be rather a tedious

"oh, no! I have not decide upon any mean of efface myself; and it be

really your arrival which bring on that fainting fit, for if you

what send you up to my room just at that very time, on that very day!"



"Von Francius," say eugen, tranquilly. "I have see he, and he be very

busy and refer I to you--that's all."



"well--let we call it von Francius."



"but what's the end of it? be that the whole story?"



"I think I might as well help you a bit," say I, rather awkwardly.

"you be not like other people, you see--it be the child, I think. I

be as much amazed as Karl, if I don't show it so much, and after

"after that?"



"well. there be the child, you see, and thing seem quite different

somehow. I've be very comfortable" (this be my way of put it)

year. shall you make he into a musician too?"



Courvoisier's brow cloud a little.



"I don't know," be all he say. later, I learn the reason of that

"so it be no love affair," say Eugen again. "then I have be wrong

all the time. I quite fancy it be some girl--"

"that's like you, Friedhelm," say he, in a tone which be in some way

different from his usual one. "I never know such a ridiculous,

"never impertinently, I assure you, eugen," say I, earnestly.



he laugh.



"you impertinent! that be amusing, I must say. but surely you have give

I a thought now and then, have wonder whether I have a history, or

spring out of nothing?"



"certainly, and wonder what your story be; but I do not need to know

"I understand. well, but it be rather difficult to say this to such an

unsympathetic person; you willn't understand it. I have be in love,

Friedel."



"so I can suppose."



I wait for the corollary, "and be love in return," but it do not

come. he say, "and receive as much regard in return as I

deserve--perhaps more."



as I could not cordially assent to this proposition, I remain silent.



after a pause he go on: "I be eight-and-twenty, and have live my

I go on my own way, and be satisfied with it--blindly, inanely,

densely satisfied with it; then all at once I be bring to reason--"

he laugh, not a very pleasant laugh. "bring to reason," he resume,

"but how? by wake one morning to find myself a spoiled man, and

spoil by myself, too."



a pause, while I turn this information over in my mind, and then say,

composedly:



"I don't quite believe in your be a spoiled man. grant that you

have make some _fiasco_--even a very bad one--what be to prevent your

straightforward philosophy. one night I be, take it all in all,

again he laugh.



"I once know some one," say I, "who use to say that many a good man

and many a great man be lose to the world simply because nothing

interrupt the course of his prosperity."



"don't suppose that I be an embryo hero of any description," say he,

that his own fault have bring he to what he be."



"but look here! if it be merely a question of name or money," I begin.



"it be not merely that; but suppose it be, what then?"



"it lie with yourself. you may make a name either as a composer or

performer--your head or your finger will secure you money and fame."



"none the less should I be, as I say, a spoiled man," he say, quietly.

"I should be ashamed to come forward. it be I myself who send myself

and my prospect _caput_;[A] and for that sort of obscurity be the good

taste and the right sphere."



[footnote a: _Caput_--a german slang expression with the general

significance of the English "go to smash," but also a hundred other

and wide meaning, impossible to render in brief.]



"but there's the boy," I suggest. "let he have the advantage."



"don't, don't!" he say, suddenly, and wince visibly, as if I have

touch a raw spot. "no; my one hope for he be that he may never be

know as my son."



"but--but--"



"poor little beggar! I wonder what will become of he," he utter,

after a pause, during which I do not speak again.



Eugen puff fitfully at his cigar, and at last knock the ash from it

and avoid my eye, he say, in a low voice:



"I suppose some time I must leave the boy."



"leave he!" I echo, intelligently.



"when he grow a little old--before he be old enough to feel it very

much, though, I must part from he. it will be well."



another pause. no sign of emotion, no quiver of the lip, no groan,

though the heart might be afaint. I sit speechless.



"I have not come to the conclusion lately. I've always know it," he

go on, and speak slowly. "I have know it--and have think about

it--so as to get accustomed to it--see?"



I nod.



"at that time--as you seem to have a fancy for the child--will you give

an eye to he--sometimes, Friedel--that be, if you care enough for me--"



for a moment I do not speak. then I say:



"you be quite sure the parting must take place?"



he assent.



"when it do, will you give he to I--to my charge altogether?"



"what do you mean?"



"if he must lose one father, let I grow as like another to he as I

can."



"Friedhelm--"



"on no other condition," say I. "I will not 'have an eye' to he

Eugen have cover his face with his hand, but speak not.



"I will have he with I altogether, or not at all," I finish, with a

reason--not the wild reason for such a--"



"yes, there be; there be the reason that I willn't have it otherwise,"

"like I? _Nie, mein lieber_; he shall be something rather well than I

"oh, yes! in a year or two from now," say he, almost inaudibly. "the

bad of it be that in a case like this, the year go so fast, so

cursedly fast."



I could make no answer to this, and he add, "give I thy hand upon it,

Friedel."



I hold out my hand. we have rise, and stand look steadfastly into

each other's eye.



"I wish I be--what I might have be--to pay you for this," he say,

hesitatingly, wring my hand and lay his left for a moment on my

shoulder; then, without another word, go into his room, shut the

door after he.



I remain still--sad, gladder than I have ever be before. never have

I so intensely feel the deep, eternal sorrow of life--that sorrow which

can be avoid by none who rightly live; yet never have life tower

before I so rich and so well worth live out, so capable of high

exultation, pure purpose, full satisfaction, and sufficient reward. my

quarrel with existence be make up.









CHAPTER XVII.



 "the merely great be, all in all,

 no more than what the merely small

   Esteem they. Man's opinion

 neither confer nor can remove

   _this_ man's dominion."





three year pass--an even way. in three year there happen little of

importance--little, that be, of open importance--to either of we. I read

that sentence again, and can not help smile; "to either of we." it

show the progress that our friendship have make. yes, it have grow every

day.



I have no past, painful or otherwise, which I could even wish to conceal;

I have no thought that I desire hide from the man who have become my

other self. what there be of good in I, what of evil, he see. it be

over the bad; for, from that first day of meeting, our intimacy go on

steadily in one direction--increase, deepen. he be six year

I have anything approach his capacity in any way. I do not think

that have anything to do with it. our happiness do not depend on

mental supremacy. I love he--because I could not help it; he I,

because--upon my word, I can think of no good reason--probably because

he do.



and yet we be as unlike as possible. he have habit of reckless

incident. we be both great reader, and, despite our sometimes arduous

"here, Friedel; here be some mental dissipation for to-night. drop that

Schopenhauer, and study Heyse. here be 'die Kinder der Welt;' it will

suit our case exactly, for it be what we be ourselves."



"how clean it look!" I observe, innocently.



"so it ought, see that I have just pay for it."



"pay for it!" I almost shout. "pay for it! you don't mean that you

have buy the book!"

capable of steal the book?"

"that's the figure to a pfennig."



"well," say I, with conscious superiority, "you might have have the

touch it; so I order a new one."



"very well. your account will look well when you come to balance and

the world be wise in their generation than the child of light, and

leave that low person to prematurely age himself by begin to balance

his account before they be ripe for it."

most utter rubbish that be ever conceive," say I, nettled. "there be

simply no sense in it. give an income of--"



"_Aber, ich bitte Dich!_" he implore, though laugh; and I be

silent.



but his three volume of "die Kinder der Welt" furnish I with many an

opportunity to "point a moral or adorn a tale," and I believe really

warn he off one or two other similar extravagance. the idea of man

in our position recklessly order three-volume novel because the

over for a long time.



we still inhabit the same room at No. 45, in the Wehrhahn. we have

outstay many other tenant; man have come and go, both from our

house and from those room over the way whose window face ours. we

pass our time in much the same way--hard work at our profession, and,

with Eugen at least, hard work out of it; the education of his boy,

whom he make his constant companion in every leisure moment, and

teach, with a wisdom that I could hardly believe--it seem so like

inspiration--composition, translation, or writing of his own--incessant

employment of some kind. he never seem able to pass an idle moment;

and yet there be time when, it seem to I, his work do not satisfy

he, but rather seem to disgust he.



once when I ask he if it be so, he lay down his pen and say,

"yes."



"then why do you do it?"



"because--for no reason that I know; but because I be an unreasonable

fool."



"an unreasonable fool to work hard?"



"no; but to go on as if hard work now can ever undo what year of

idleness have do."



"do you believe in work?" I ask.



"I believe it be the very high and holy thing there be, and the

grand purifier and cleanser in the world. but it be not a panacea

against every ill. I believe that idleness be sometimes as strong as

work, and strong. you may do that in a few year of idleness which a

life-time of afterwork willn't cover, mend, or improve. you may make hole

in your coat from sheer laziness, and then find that no amount of

stitch will patch they up again."



I seldom answer these mystic monologue. love give a wonderful

sharpness even to dull wit; it have sharpen mine so that I often feel

he indulge in those speech out of sheer desire to work off some grief

or bitterness from his heart, but that a question might, however

innocent, overshoot the mark, and touch a sore spot--the thing I most

dread. and I do not feel it essential to my regard for he to know

every item of his past.



in such case, however, when there be something behind--when one know

it, only do not know what it be (and Eugen have never try to conceal

from I that something have happen to he which he do not care to

tell)--then, even though one accept the fact, as I accept it, without

dispute or resentment, one yet involuntarily build theory, have idea,

or rather the idea shape themselves about the object of interest, and

take their coloring from he, one can not refrain from conjecture,

surmise. mine be necessarily of the most vague and shadowy

description; more negative than active, less theory as to what he have

be or do than inference from what he let fall in talk or conduct as

to what he have not be or do.



in our three year' acquaintance, it be true, there have not be much

opportunity for any striking display on his part of good or bad

quality; but certainly ample opportunity of test whether he be,

take all in all, superior, even with, or inferior to the average man

of our average acquaintance. and, briefly speak, to I he have become

a stand model of a superior man.



I have by this time learn to know that when there be many way of

look at a question, that one, if there be such an one, which be

less earthily practical, more ideal and less common than the other,

would most inevitably be the view take by Eugen Courvoisier, and

advocate by he with warmth, energy, and eloquence to the very last.

the point from which he survey the thing and the doing of life be,

take all in all, a high one than that of other man, and be illumine

with something of the purple splendor of that "light that never be on

sea or land." a less practical conduct, a more ideal view of right and

wrong--sometimes a little fantastic even--always imbue with something

of the knightliness which sit upon he as a natural attribute.

_ritterlich_, Karl Linders call he, half in jest, half in earnest;

and _ritterlich_ he be.



in his outward demeanor to the world with which he come in contact, he

be courteous to man; to a friend or intimate, as myself, an ever-new

delight and joy; to all people, truthful to fantasy; and to woman, on

less exquisitely graceful and delicate--more stout and robust.

"what wilt thou be, Sigmund, when thou art a man?"



"_Ja, lieber Vater_, I will be just like thee."



"how just like I?"



"I will do what thou dost."



"so! thou wilt be a musiker like I and Friedel?"

"'I would I be as honest a man,'" say eugen, slightly alter

"why, my child?"



it be a long earnest look that the child give the man. eugen have say

"that child's life be one strife after the beautiful in art, and nature,

and life--how will he succeed in the search?"



I think of this--it flash subtly through my mind as Sigmund gaze at

"thou art so beautiful--so beautiful! I must be like thee."

"I be his God, you see, Friedel. what will he do when he find out what

a common clay figure it be he worship?"



but he have not the heart to banter the child; only hold the little

his heaven.



it be after this that Eugen say to I when we be alone:



"it must come before he think less of I than he do now, Friedel."

he have accomplish one great feat in those three year--he have win over

eager to acknowledge those of other people.

only between he and von Francius there remain a constant gulf and a

Max von Francius be a man about whom there be various opinion,

express and unexpresse; he be a person who never speak of himself,

and who contrive to live a life more isolated and apart than any one I

have ever know, consider that he go much into society, and mix a

romantic, discourage not so much enthusiasm as the outward

cynosure of all eye in Elberthal, and bear the scrutiny with glacial

indifference.









CHAPTER XVIII.



FRIEDHELM'S STORY.





[illustration: Music, JOACHIM, RAFF. _Op._ 177.]



"make yourself quite easy, Herr Concertmeister. no child that be leave

to my charge be ever know to come to harm."



thus Frau Schmidt to Eugen, as she stand with dubious smile and fold

       "Ade, ade, ade!

     Ja, Scheiden und Meiden thut Weh!"

CHAPTER XIX.



"wish be pilgrim to the vale of tear."

"because I think it will be for the good."

I fire up.



"excuse I, Eugen, it might be vulgar if I be merely chaff you, but

"Pre--pos--ter--ous!"



"if you can mention an instance in her behavior this evening which

"well--well--"



"well--well. if she have wish to snub you she would have send you that

"I think you be mistaken. however, it do not matter; our path lie

so be I. we say no more upon the subject, but I always feel as if a

the link be destine to become yet close. the concert be over at

one afternoon when Eugen and I happen to have nothing to do, we take

     "with shedding from the pine umbrage tinge,"



to an invisible beyond which have charm for I, and be a kind of

a light leap into the father's eye; a look of pain follow it

"and I shall never leave thee," say Sigmund.



Eugen parry the necessity of speak by a kiss.



"I love thee too, Friedel," continue he, take my hand. "we be very

happy together, ben't we?" and he laugh placidly to himself.



Eugen, as if sting by some torment thought, spring up and we leave the

oh, far back, by-go day! there be a soft light over you shed by a

kindly sun. that be a time in which joy run a golden thread through the

back to the restauration at the foot of the _berg_, where Sigmund be

we take our way home. the day be long, and it be yet light when we

"also!" say eugen, after a pause. "a new neighbor; it remind I of one

CHAPTER XX.



 "for though he live aloof from ken,

 the world's unwitnessed denizen,

   the love within he stir

 abroad, and with the heart of man

   his own confer."





the story of my life from day to day be dull enough, same enough for

     "I be catholic in my taste. I suppose all our friend would faint

     twopenny-halfpenny person who have to advertise for engagement.

                    "A."

     "Du, du, liegst mir Im Herzen;

     Du, du, liegst mir Im Sinn,"

     an meine grüne Seite."

I remember one small incident which at the time it happen strike home

CHAPTER XXI.



 "and near still shall far be,

 and word shall plague and vex and buffet thee."





it be December, close upon Christmas. winter at last in real earnest. a

black frost. the earth bind in fetter of iron. the land gray; the sky

steel; the wind a dagger. the tree, leafless and stark, rattle their

shriveled bough together in that wind.



it meet you at corner and freeze the word out of your mouth; it whistle

a low, fiendish, malignant whistle round the house; as vicious and

little loud than the buzz of a mosquito. it sweep, thin, keen and

cutting, down the Königsallée, and blow fine black dust into one's face.



it cut up the skater upon the pond in the Neue Anlage, which be in the

center of the town, and comparatively shelter; but it be in its glory

whistle across the flat field lead to the great skating-ground of

Elberthal in general--the Schwanenspiegel at the Grafenbergerdahl.



the Grafenberg be a low chain of what, for want of a well name, may

be call hill, lie to the north of Elberthal. the country all around

this unfortunate apology for a range of hill be, if possible, flatter

than ever. the Grafenbergerdahl be, properly, no "dale" at all, but a

broad plain of meadow, with the railway cut they at one point, then

diverging and run on under the Grafenberg.



one vast meadow which lie, if possible, a trifle low than the rest,

be flood regularly by the autumn rain, but not deeply. it be frozen

over now, and form a model skating place, and so, apparently, think

the townspeople, for they come out, singly or in body, and from nine

in the morning till dusk the place be crowd, and the merry music of

the iron on the ice cease not for a second.



I discover this place of resort by accident one day when I be take

a constitutional, and find myself upon the border of the great frozen

mere cover with skater. I stand look at they, and my blood warm

at the sight. if there be one thing--one accomplishment upon which I

pride myself, it be this very one--skating.



in a drawing-room I might feel awkward--confuse among clever people,

bashful among accomplished one; shy about music and painting, diffident

as to my voice, and deprecatory in spirit as to the etiquette to be

observe at a dinner-party. give I my skate and put I on a sheet of

ice, and I be at home.



as I pause and watch the skater, it strike I that there be no

reason at all why I should deny myself that seasonable enjoyment. I have

my skate, and the mere be large enough to hold I as well as the

other--indeed, I see in the distance great tract of virgin ice to

which no skater seem yet to have reach.



I go home, and on the follow afternoon carry out my resolution;

though it be after three o'clock before I could set out.



a long, bleak way. first up the merry Jägerhofstrasse, then through the

Malkasten garden, up a narrow lane, then out upon the open, bleak road,

with that bitter wind go ping-ping at one's ear and upon one's

cheek. through a big gate-way, and a court-yard pertaining to an orphan

asylum--along a lane border with apple-tree, through a rustic arch,

and, hurrah! the field be before I--not so thickly cover as

yesterday, for it be get late, and the Elberthalers do not seem to

understand the joy of career over the black ice by moonlight, in the

night wind. it be, however, as yet far from dark, and the moon be

rise in silver yonder, in a sky of a pale but clear blue.



I quickly put on my skate--stumble to the edge, and set off. I take a

few turn, circle among the people--then, see several turn to look

at I, I fix my eye upon a distant clump of reed rise from the

ice, and resolve to make it my goal. I could only just see it, even

with my long-sighted eye, but strike out for it bravely. past group

after group of the skater who turn to look at my scarlet shawl as it

flash past. I glance at they and skim smoothly on, till I come to

the outside circle where there be a skater all alone, his hand thrust

deep into his great-coat pocket, the collar of the same turn high

about his ear, and the inevitable little gray cloth _Studentenhut_

crown the luxuriance of wave dark hair. he be glide round in

complicated figure and circle, do the outside edge for his own

solitary gratification, so far as I could see; active, graceful, and

muscular, with practiced ease and assure strength in every limb. it

need no second glance on my part to assure I who he be--even if the

dark bright eye have not be catch by the flash of my cloak, and

gravely raise for a moment as I fly by. I dash on, breast the

wind. to reach the bunch of reed seem more than ever desirable now. I

would make it my sole companion until it be time to go away. at least

he have see I, and I be safe from any _contretemps_--he would avoid I

as strenuously as I avoid he. but the first fresh lust after pleasure

be go. just one moment's glance into a face have have the power to

alter everything so much. I skate on, as fast, as surely as ever, but,



     "a joy have take flight."



the pleasant sensation of solitude, which I could so easily have feel

among a thousand people have he not be count among they, be go.

the roll of my skate upon the ice have lose its music for I; the wind

feel colder--I sadder. at least I think so. should I go away again now

that this disturbing element have appear upon the scene? no, no, no,

say something eagerly within I, and I bite my lip, and choke back a

kind of sob of disgust as I realize that despite my gloomy reflection

my heart be beat a high, rapid march of--joy! as I skim, all

alone, far away from the crowd, among the dismal wither reed, and

round the little islet of stiffen grass and rush which be frozen

upright in their place.



the daylight fade, and the moon rise. the people be go away. the

distant buzz of laughter have grow silent. I could dimly discern some

few group, but very few, still leave, and one or two solitary figure.

even my preternatural eagerness could not discern who they be! the

darkness, the long walk home, the probe at seven, which I should be too

of Courvoisier, and surely there be no harm in my staying here.



I strike out in another direction, and fly on in the keen air; the

frosty moon shed a weird light upon the black ice; I see the railway

line, polished, gleam too in the light; the belt of dark fir to my

right; the red sand soil freeze hard and silver over with frost. flat

and tame, but still beautiful. I feel a kind of rejoice in it; I feel

it home. I be probably the first person who have be there since the

freezing of the mere, think I, and that idea be soon convert to a

certainty in my mind, for in a second my rapid career be interrupt.

at the furth point from help or human presence the ice give way with
