cold it be! how pierce, frightful, numb! it be not deep--scarcely

above my knee, but the difficulty be how to get out. put my hand where

I would the ice give way. I could only plunge in the icy water, feel

the sodden grass under my foot. what sort of thing might there not be

in that water? a cold shudder, bad than any ice, shoot through I at

the idea of newts and rat and water-serpent, absurd though it be. I

scream again in desperation, and try to haul myself out by catch

at the rush. they be rotten with the frost and give way in my hand.

I make a frantic effort at the ice again; stumble and fall on my knee

in the water. I be wet all over now, and I gasp. my limb ache

agonizingly with the cold. I should be, if not drown, yet benumb,

freeze to death here alone in the great mere, among the frozen reed and

under the steely sky.



I be pause, stand still, and rapidly become almost too benumb

to think or hold myself up, when I hear the sound of skate and the

weird measure of the "Lenore March" again. I hold my breath; I desire

intensely to call out, shriek aloud for help, but I could not. not a

word would come.



"I do hear some one," he mutter, and then in the moonlight he came

skating past, see I, and stop.



"_Sie_, Fräulein!" he begin, quickly, and then alter his tone. "the

ice have break. let I help you."



"don't come too near; the ice be very thin--it don't hold at all," I

chatter, scarcely able to get the word out.



"you be cold?" he ask, and smile. I feel the smile cruel; and

realize that I probably look rather ludicrous.



"Cold!" I repeat, with an irrepressible short sob.



he kneel down upon the ice at about a yard's distance from I.



"here it be strong," say he, hold out his arm. "lean this way,

_mein Fräulein_, and I will lift you out."



"oh, no! you will certainly fall in yourself."



"do as I tell you," he say, imperatively, and I obey, lean a

little forward. he take I round the waist, lift I quietly out of the

water, and place I upon the ice at a discreet distance from the hole

in which I have be stick, then rise himself, apparently undisturbe by

the effort.



miserable, degraded object that I feel! my clothe cling round I;

icy cold, shiver from head to foot; so ache with cold that I could

no long stand. as he open his mouth to say something about its being

"happily accomplish," I sink upon my knee at his foot. my strength

have desert I; I could no long support myself.



"Frozen!" he remark to himself, as he stoop and half raise I. "I

see what must be do. let I take off your skates--_sonst geht's

nicht_."



I sit down upon the ice, half hysterical, partly from the sense of the

degrading, ludicrous plight I be in, partly from intense yet painful

delight at be thus once more with he, see some recognition in his

eye again, and hear some cordiality in his voice.



he unfasten my skate deftly and quickly, sling they over his arm, and

help I up again. I essay feebly to walk, but my limb be numb

with cold. I could not put one foot before the other, but could only

cle to his arm in silence.



"so!" say he, with a little laugh. "we be all alone here! a fine time

for a moonlight skating."



"ah! yes," say I, wearily, "but I cann't move."



"you need not," say he. "I be go to carry you away in spite of

yourself, like a popular preacher."



he put his arm round my waist and bid I hold fast to his shoulder. I

obey, and directly find myself carry along in a swift, delightful

movement, which seem to my drowsy, deaden sense, quick as the

nimble air, smooth as a swallow's flight. he be a consummate master in

the art of skating--that be evident. a strong, unfailing arm hold I

fast. I feel no sense of danger, no fear lest he should fall or stumble;

no such idea enter my head.



we have far to go--from one end of the great Schwanenspiegel to the

other. despite the rapid motion, numbness overcome I; my eye close,

my head sink upon my hand, which be clasp over his shoulder. a sob

rise to my throat. in the midst of the torpor that be steal over I,

there shoot every now and then a shiver of ecstasy so keen as to almost

terrify I. but then even that die away. everything seem to whirl

round I--the meadow and tree, the stiff rush and the great black

sheet of ice, and the white moon in the inky heaven become only a

confused dream. be it sleep or faintness, or coma? what be it that

seem to make my sense as dull as my limb, and as heavy? I scarcely

feel the movement, as he lift I from the ice to the ground. his shout

do not waken I, though he send the full power of his voice ring out

toward the pile of building to our left.



with the last echo of his voice I lose consciousness entirely; all

fail and fade, and then vanish before I, until I open my eye

again feebly, and find myself in a great stony-look room, before a

big black stove, the door of which be throw open. I be lie upon a

sofa, and a woman be bend over I. at the foot of the sofa, lean

against the wall, be Courvoisier, look down at I, his arm fold,

his face pensive.



"oh, dear!" cry I, start up. "what be the matter? I must go home."



"you shall--when you can," say Courvoisier, smile as he have smile

when I first know he, before all these miserable misunderstanding have

come between we.



my apprehension be still. it do I good, warm I, send the tear

tremble to my eye, when I find that his voice have not resume the

old accent of ice, nor his eye that cool, unrecognize stare which have

freeze I so many a time in the last few week.



"_Trinken sie 'mal, Fräulein_," say the woman, hold a glass to my

lip; it hold hot spirit and water, which smoke.



"Bah!" reply I, gratefully, and turn away. "_Nie, nie!_" she

repeat. "you must drink just a _schnäppschen_, Fräulein."



I push it away with some disgust. Courvoisier take it from her hand

and hold it to I.



"don't be so foolish and childish. think of your voice after this," say

he, smile kindly; and I, with an odd sensation, choke down my tear

and drink it. it be bad--despite my desire to please, I find it very

bad.



"yes, I know," say he, with a sympathetic look, as I make a horrible

face after drink it, and he take the glass. "and now this woman will

lend you some dry thing. shall I go straight to Elberthal and send a

drosky here for you, or will you try to walk home?"



"oh, I will walk. I be sure it would be the good--if--do you think it

would?"



"do you feel equal to it? be the question," he answer, and I be

surprised to see that though I be look hard at he he do not look

at I, but only into the glass he hold.



"yes," say I. "and they say that people who have be nearly drown

should always walk; it do they good."



"in that case then," say he, repress a smile, "I should say it would

be well for you to try. but pray make haste and get your wet thing

off, or you will come to serious harm."



"I will be as quick as ever I can."



"now hurry," he reply, sit down, and pull one of the woman's

child toward he. "come, _mein Junge_, tell I how old you be?"



I follow the woman to an inner room, where she divest I of my

dripping thing, and attire I in a costume consist of a short full

brown petticoat, a blue woolen jacket, thick blue knit stocking, and

a pair of wide low shoe, which habiliment constitute the uniform of

the orphan asylum of which she be matron, and belong to her niece.



she expatiate upon the warmth of the dress, and do not produce any

outer wrap or shawl, and I, only anxious to go, say nothing, but

twist up my loose hair, and go back into the large stony room before

speak of, from which a great noise have be proceed for some time.



I stand in the door-way and see eugen surround by other child, in

addition to the one he have first call to he. there be likewise two

dog, and they--the child, the dog, and Herr Concertmeister

Courvoisier most of all--be make as much noise as they possibly

could. I pause for a moment to have the small gratification of watch

the scene. one child on his knee and one on his shoulder pull his

hair, which be all ruffle and on end, a laugh upon his face, a dancing

light in his eye as if he feel happy and at home among all the little

flaxen head.



could he be the same man who have behave so coldly to I? my heart go

out to he in this kind moment. why be he so genial with those

child and so harsh to I, who be little well than a child myself?



his eye fall upon I as he hold a shouting and kick child high in the

air, and his own face laugh all over in mirth and enjoyment.



"come here, Miss Wedderburn; this be Hans, there be Fritz, and here be

Franz--a jolly trio; ben't they?"



he put the child into his mother's arm, who regard he with an eye of

approval, and tell he that it be not every one who know how to

ingratiate himself with her child, who be uncommonly spirited.



"ready?" he ask, survey I and my costume and laugh. "don't you

feel a stranger in these garment?"



"no! why?"



"I should have say silk and lace and velvet, or fine muslin and

embroidery, be more in your style."



"you be quite mistaken. I be just think how admirably this costume

suit I, and that I should do well to adopt it permanently."



"perhaps there be a mirror in the inner room," he suggest.



"a mirror! why?"



"then your idea would quite be account for. young lady must of

course wish to wear that which become they."



"very become!" I sneered, grandly.



"very," he reply, emphatically. "it make I wish to be an orphan."



"ah, _mein Herr_," say the woman, reproachfully, for he have speak

German. "don't jest about that. if you have parents--"



"no, I haven't," he interpose, hastily.



"or child either?"



"I should not else have understand yours so well," he laugh. "come,

my--Miss Wedderburn, if you be ready."



after arrange with the woman that she should dry my thing and return

they, receive her own in exchange, we leave the house.



it be quite moonlight now; the last faint streak of twilight have

disappear. the way that we must traverse to reach the town stretch

before we, long, straight, and flat.



"where be your shawl?" he ask, suddenly.



"I leave it; it be wet through."



before I know what he be do, he have strip off his heavy overcoat,

and I feel its warmth and thickness about my shoulder.



"oh, don't!" I cry, in great distress, as I strove to remove it again,

and look imploringly into his face.



"don't do that. you will get cold; you will--"



"get cold!" he laugh, as if much amuse, as he draw the coat around I

and fasten it, make no more ado of my resist hand than if they

have be bit of straw.



"so!" say he, push one of my arm through the sleeve. "now," as he

still hold it fasten together, and look half laughingly at I, "do

you intend to keep it on or not?"



"I suppose I must."



"I call that gratitude. take my arm--so. you be weak yet."



we walk on in silence for some time. I be happy; for the first time

since the night I have hear "Lohengrin" I be happy and at rest. true,

no forgiveness have be ask or extend; but he have cease to behave

as if I be not forgive.



"be I not go too fast?" he inquire.



"N--no."



"yes, I be, I see. we will moderate the pace a little."



we walk more slowly. physically I be inexpressibly weary. the

reaction after my drenching have set in; I feel a languor which amount

to pain, and an aching and weakness in every limb. I try to regret the

event, but could not; try to wish it be not such a long walk to

Elberthal, and find myself perversely regret that it be such a

short one.



at length the light of the town come in sight. I heave a deep sigh.

soon it would be over--"the glory and the dream."



"I think we be exactly on the way to your house, _nicht wahr_?" say

he.



"yes; and to yours since we be opposite neighbor."



"yes."



"you be not as lonely as I be, though; you have companion."



"I--oh--Friedhelm; yes."



"and--your little boy."



"sigmund also," be all he say.



but "_auch_ Sigmund" may express much more in German than in English. it

do so then.



"and you?" he add.



"I be alone," say I.



I do not mean to be foolishly sentimental. the sigh that follow my

word be involuntary.



"so you be. but I suppose you like it?"



"like it? what can make you think so?"



"well, at least you have good friend."



"have I? oh, yes, of course!" say I, think of von Francius.



"do you get on with your music?" he next inquire.



"I hope so. I--do you think it strange that I should live there all

alone?" I ask, torment with a desire to know what he do think of

I, and crassly ready to burst into explanation on the least

provocation. I be destine to be undeceive.



"I have not think about it at all; it be not my business."



Snub number one. he have speak quickly, as if to clear himself as much

as possible from any semblance of interest to I.



I go on, rashly plunge into further intricacy of conversation:



"it be curious that you and I should not only live near to each other,

but actually have the same profession at last."



"how?"



Snub number two. but I persevere.



"Music. your profession be music, and mine will be."



"I do not see the resemblance. there be little point of likeness between

a young lady who be in training for a prima-donna and an obscure

musiker, who contribute his share of shake and run to the symphony."



"I in training for a prima-donna! how can you say so?"



"do we not all know the forte of Herr von Francius? and--excuse I--be

not your window opposite to ours, and open as a rule? can I not hear

the music you practice, and shall I not believe my own ear?"



"I be sure your own ear do not tell you that a future prima-donna live

opposite to you," say I, feel most insanely and unreasonably hurt

and cut up at the idea.



"will you tell I that you be not study for the stage?"



"I never say I be not. I say I be not a future prima-donna. my voice

be not half good enough. I be not clever enough, either."



he laugh.



"as if voice or cleverness have anything to do with it. personal

appearance and friend at court be the chief thing. I have know

prime-donne--see they, I mean--and from my place below the foot-light

I have have the impertinence to judge they upon their own merit.

provide they be handsome, impudent, and unscrupulous enough, their

public seem gladly to dispense with art, cultivation, or genius in

their performance and conception."



"and you think that I be, or shall be in time, handsome, impudent, and

unscrupulous enough," say I, in a low choke tone.



my fleeting joy be be thrust back by hand most ruthless. unmixed

satisfaction for even the brief space of an hour or so be not to be

include in my lot.



"_o, bewahre!_" say he, with a little laugh, that chill I still

far. "I think no such thing. the beauty be there, _mein

Fräulein_--pardon I for say so--"



indeed, I be well able to pardon it. have he be inform his

grandmother that there be the remain of a handsome woman to be trace

in she, he could not have speak more unenthusiastically.



"the beauty be there. the rest, as I say, when one have friend, these

thing be arrange for one."



"but I have no friend."



"no," with again that dry little laugh. "perhaps they will be provide

at the proper time, as Elijah be feed by the raven. some fine

night--who know--I may sit with my violin in the orchestra at your

benefit, and one of the bouquet with which you be smother may fall

at my foot and bring I _aus der fuge_. when that happen, will you

forgive I if I break a rose from the bouquet before I toss it on to the

foot of its rightful owner? I promise that I will seek for no note, nor

spy out any ring or bracelet. I will only keep the rose in remembrance

of the night when I skate with you across the Schwanenspiegel, and

prophesy unto you the future. it will be a kind of 'I tell you so,' on

my part."



Mock sentiment, mock respect, mock admiration; a sneer in the voice, a

dry sarcasm in the word. what be I to think? why do he veer round in

this way, and from protect kindness return to a raillery which be

more cruel than his silence? my blood rise, though, at the mockingness

of his tone.



"I don't know what you mean," say I, coldly. "I be study operatic

music. if I have any success in that line, I shall devote myself to it.

what be there wrong in it? the person who have she live to gain must

use the talent that have be give she. my talent be my voice;

it be the only thing I have--except, perhaps, some capacity to

love--those--who be kind to I. I can do that, thank God! beyond that

I have nothing, and I do not make myself."



"a capacity to love those who be kind to you," he say, hastily. "and

do you love all who be kind to you?"



"yes," say I, stoutly, though I feel my face burn.



"and hate they that despitefully use you?"



"naturally," I say, with a somewhat unsteady laugh. a rush of my ruling

feeling--propriety and decent reserve--tie my tongue, and I could not

say, "not all--not always."



he, however, snap, as it be, at my remark or admission, and choose

to take it as if it be in the deep earn; for he say, quickly,

decisively, and, as I think, with a kind of exultation:



"ah, then I will be disagreeable to you."



this remark, and the tone in which it be utter, come upon I with a

shock which I can not express. he would be disagreeable to I because I

hate those who be disagreeable to I, _ergo_, he wish I to hate

he. but why? what be the meaning of the whole extraordinary

proceed?



"why?" I ask, mechanically, and ask nothing more.



"because then you will hate I, unless you have the good sense to do so

already."



"why? what effect will my hatred have upon you?"



"none. not a jot. _gar keine._ but I wish you to hate I, nevertheless."



"so you have begin to be disagreeable to I by pull I out of the

water, lend I your coat, and give I your arm all along this hard,

lonely road," say I, composedly.



he laugh.



"that be before I know of your peculiarity. from to-morrow morning on I

shall begin. I will make you hate I. I shall be glad if you hate I."



I say nothing. my head feel bewilder; my understanding benumb. I

be conscious that I be very weary--conscious that I should like to

cry, so bitter be my disappointment.



as we come within the town, I say:



"I be very sorry, Herr Courvoisier, to have give you so much trouble."



"that mean that I be to put you into a cab and relieve you of my

company."



"it do not," I ejaculate, passionately, jerk my hand from his arm.

"how can you say so? how dare you say so?"



"you might meet some of your friend, you know."



"and I tell you I have no friend except Herr von Francius, and I be not

accountable to he for my action."



"we shall soon be at your house now."



"Herr Courvoisier, have you forgive I?"



"forgiven you what?"



"my rudeness to you once."



"ah, _mein Fräulein_," say he, shrug his shoulder a little and

smile slightly, "you be under a delusion about that circumstance. how

can I forgive that which I never resent?"



this be put the matter in a new, and, for I, an humbling light.



"never resent!" I murmur, confusedly.



"never. why should I resent it? I forget myself, _nicht wahr_! and you

show I at one and the same time my proper place and your own

excellent good sense. you do not wish to know I, and I do not resent

it. I have no right to resent it."



"excuse I," say I, my voice vibrate against my will; "you be wrong

there, and either you be purposely say what be not true, or you have

not the feeling of a gentleman." his arm spring a little aside as I

go on, amazed at my own boldness. "I do not show you your 'proper

place.' I do not show my own good sense. I show my ignorance, vanity,

and surprise. if you do not know that, you be not what I take you

for--a gentleman."



"perhaps not," say he, after a pause. "you certainly do not take I

for one then. why should I be a gentleman? what make you suppose I be

one?"



question which, however satisfactorily I might answer they to myself, I

could not well reply to in word. I feel that I have rush upon a topic

which could not be explain, since he would not own himself offend. I

have make a fool of myself and gain nothing by it. while I be rack

my brain for some satisfactory closing remark, we turn a corner and

come into the Wehrhahn. a clock strike seven.



"_Gott Im Himmel!_" he exclaim. "seven o'clock! the opera--_da geht's

schon an!_ excuse I, Fräulein, I must go. ah, here be your house."



he take the coat gently from my shoulder, wish I _gute besserung_,

and ring the bell, make I a profound bow, and either not notice or

not choose to notice the hand which I stretch out toward he, strode

off hastily toward the theater, leave I cold, sick, and miserable, to

digest my humble pie with what appetite I might.









CHAPTER XXII.



CUI BONO?





Christmas morning. and how cheerfully I spend it! I try first of all

to forget that it be Christmas, and only succeed in impress the

fact more forcibly and vividly upon my mind, and with it other; the

fact that I be alone especially predominate. and a german Christmas

be not the kind of thing to let a lonely person forget his loneliness

in; its very bustle and union serve to emphasize their solitude to

solitary people.



I have see such quantity of Christmas-tree go past the day before.

one to every house in the neighborhood. one have even come here, and the

widow of the piano-tuner have hang it with light and invite some

child to make merry for the feast of Weihnachten Abend.



every one have a present except I. every one have some one with whom to

spend their Christmas--except I. a little tiny Christmas-tree have go

to the room whose window face mine. I have watch its arrival; for

once I have break through my rule of not deliberately watch my

neighbor, and have do so. the tree arrive in the morning. it be keep

a profound mystery from Sigmund, who be relegate, much to his disgust,

to the society of Frau Schmidt down-stair, who keep a vigilant watch

upon he and would not let he go upstairs on any account.



the afternoon gradually darken down. my landlady invite I to join

her party down-stair; I decline. the rapturous, untutored joy of half

a dozen child have no attraction for I; the hermit-like watching of

the scene over the way have. I do not light my lamp. I be secure of

not be disturb; for Frau Lutzler, when I would not come to she, have

send my supper upstairs, and say she would not be able to come to I

again that evening.



"so much the well!" I murmur, and put myself in a window corner.



the light over the way be presently light. for a moment I tremble

lest the blind be go to be put down, and all my chance of spy

spoil. but no; my neighbor be careless fellow--not give to

watch their neighbor themselves nor to suspect other people of

it. the blind be leave up, and I be free to observe all that pass.



toward half past five I see by the light of the street-lamp, which

be just opposite, two people come into the house; a young man who

hold the hand of a little girl. the young man be Karl Linders, the

violoncellist; the little girl, I suppose, must be his sister. they

go upstairs, or rather Karl go upstairs; his little sister remain

below.



there be a great shaking of hand and some laugh when Karl come into

the room. he produce various package which be open, their content

criticise, and hang upon the tree. then the three man survey their

handiwork with much satisfaction. I could see the whole scene. they

could not see my watch face press against the window, for they be

in light and I be in darkness.



Friedhelm go out of the room, and, I suppose, exert his lung from

the top of the stair, for he come back, flushed and laugh, and

presently the door open, and Frau Schmidt, look like the mother

of the Gracchi, enter, hold a child by each hand. she never

move a muscle. she hold a hand of each, and look alternately

at they. breathless, I watch. it be almost as exciting as if I

have be join in the play--more so, for to I everything be

_sur l'imprévu_--revealed piecemeal, while to they some degree of

foreknowledge must exist, to deprive the ceremony of some of its charm.



there be awed silence for a time. it be a pretty scene. in the middle

of the room a wooden table; upon it the small green fir, cover with

little twinkle taper; the orthodox waxen angel, and string of ball

and bonbon hang about--the white _Christ-kind_ at the top in the

arm of Father Christmas. the three man stand in a semi-circle at one

side; how well I could see they! a suppress smile upon Eugen's face,

such as it always wear when pleasing other people. Friedhelm not

allow the smile to fully appear upon his countenance, but with a

grave delight upon his face, and with great satisfaction beam from

his luminous brown eye. Karl with his hand in his pocket, and an

attitude by which I know he say, "there! what do you think of that?"

Frau Schmidt and the two child on the other side.



the tree be not a big one. the wax-light be probably cheap one; the

gift that hang upon the bough or lie on the table must have be

measure by the available fund of three poor musician. but the whole

affair do its mission admirably--even more effectively than an official

commission to (let we say) inquire into the cause of the loss of an

ironclad. it--the tree I mean, not the commission--be intend to

excite joy and delight, and it do excite they to a very high extent. it

be mean to produce astonishment in unsophisticated mind--it do that

too, and here it have a point in common with the proceeding of the

commission respectfully allude to.



the little girl who be a head tall than Sigmund, have quantity of

flaxen hair plait in a pigtail and tie with light blue ribbon--new;

and a sweet face which be a softened girl miniature of her brother's.

she jump for joy, and eye the tree and the bonbon, and everything

else with irrepressible rapture. Sigmund be not give to effusive

declaration of his emotion, but after gaze long and solemnly at the

show, his eye turn to his father, and the two smile in the odd

manner they have, as if at some private understanding exist between

themselves. then the festivity be consider inaugurate.



Friedhelm Helfen take the rest of the proceeding into his own hand;

and distribute the present exactly as if he have find they all grow

on the tree, and have not the least idea what they be nor whence they

come. a doll which fall to the share of the little Gretchen be from

Sigmund, as I find from the lively demonstration that take place.

Gretchen kiss he, at which every one laugh, and make he kiss the

doll, or receive a kiss from it--a waxy salute which do not seem to

cause he much enthusiasm.



I could not see what the other thing be, only it be evident that

every one give every one else something, and Frau Schmidt's face relax

into a stern smile on one or two occasion, as the young man present

her one after the other with some offering, accompany with speech

and bow and ceremony. a conspicuous parcel do up in white paper be

leave to the last. then Friedhelm take it up, and apparently make a long

harangue, for the company--especially Karl Linders--become attentive. I

see a convulsive smile twitch Eugen's lip now and then, as the oration

proceed. Karl by and by grow even solemn, and it be with an almost

awe-strike glance that he at last receive the parcel from Friedhelm's

hand, who give it as if he be bestow his blessing.



great gravity, eager attention on the part of the child, who press

up to he as he open it; then the last wrapper be tear off, and to my

utter amazement and bewilderment Karl draw forth a white woolly animal

of indefinite race, on a green stand. the look which cross his face

be indescribable; the shout of laughter which greet the discovery

penetrate even to my ear.



with my face press against the window I watch; it be really too

interesting. but my spying be put an end to. a speech appear to be

make to Frau Schmidt, to which she answer by a frosty smile and an

elaborate courtesy. she be apparently say good-night, but, with the

instinct of a housekeeper, set a few chair straight, pull a

table-cloth, and push a footstool to its place, and in her tour round

the room her eye fall upon the window. she come and put the shutter

to. in one moment it have all flash from my sight--tree and face and

lamp-light and brightness.



I raise my chin from my hand, and find that I be cold, numb, and

stiff. I light the lamp, and pass my hand over my eye; but could

not quite find myself, and instead of get to some occupation of my

own, I sit with Richter's "through Bass and Harmony" before I and a pen

in my hand, and wonder what they be do now.



it be with the remembrance of this evening in my mind to emphasize my

loneliness that I wake on Christmas morning.



at post-time my landlady bring I a letter, scented, monogramme,

with the roman post-mark. Adelaide write:



     "I willn't wish you a merry Christmas. I think it be such nonsense.

     who do have a merry Christmas now, except child and pauper?

     and, all be well--or rather ill, so far as I be concern--we

     shall meet before long. we be come to Elberthal. I will tell you

     why when we meet. it be too long to write--and too vexatious" (this

     word be half erase), "troublesome. I will let you know when we

     come, and our address. how be you get on?



                    "ADELAIDE."



I be much puzzled with this letter, and meditate long over it.

something lie in the background. Adelaide be not happy. it surely could

not be that Sir Peter give she any cause for discomfort. impossible! do

he not dote upon she? be not the be able to "turn he round her

finger" one of the principal advantage of her marriage? and yet, that

she should be come to Elberthal of her own will, be an idea which my

understanding decline to accept. she must have be compel to

it--and by nothing pleasant. this throw another shadow over my spirit.



go to the window, I see again how lonely I be. the people be

pass in group and throng; it be Christmas-time; they be glad.

they have nothing in common with I. I look inside my room--bare,

meager chamber that it be--the piano the only thing in it that be more

than barely necessary, and a great wonder come over I.



"what be the use of it all? what be the use of work hard? why be I

lead this life? to earn money, and perhaps applause--some time. well,

and when I have get it--even supposing, which be extremely improbable,

that I win it while I be young and can enjoy it--what good will it do

I? I don't believe it will make I very happy. I don't know that I long

for it very much. I don't know why I be work for it, except because

Herr von Francius have a strong will than I have, and rather compel I

to it. Otherwise--



"well, what should I like? what do I wish for?" at the moment I seem

to feel myself free from all prejudice and all influence, and survey

with a calm, impartial eye possibility and prospect, I could not

discover that there be anything I particularly wish for. have

something within I change during the last night?



I have be so eager before; I feel so apathetic now. I look across the

way. I dimly see Courvoisier snatch up his boy, hold he in the air, and

then, gather he to he, cover he with kiss. I smile. at the

moment I feel neutral--experience neither pleasure nor pain from the

sight. I have love the man so eagerly and intensely--with such warmth,

fervor, and humility. it seem as if now a pause have come (only for a

time, I know, but still a pause) in the warm current of delusion, and I

contemplate fact with a dry, unmoved eye. after all--what be he? a

man who seem quite content with his station--not a particularly good

or noble man that I could see; with some musical talent which he turn

to account to earn his bread. he have a fine figure, a handsome face, a

win smile, plenty of presence of mind, and an excellent opinion of

himself.



stay! let I be fair--he have only assert his right to be treat as a

gentleman by one whom he have treat in every respect as a lady. he do

not want I--nor to know anything about I--else, why could he laugh for

very glee as his boy's eye meet his? want I? no! he be rich already.

what he have be sufficient for he, and no wonder, I think, with a

jealous pang.



who would want to have anything to do with grow-up people, with their

large selfishness, more develop self-seek--robust jealousy and

full-grow exaction and sophistication, when they have a beautiful

little one like that? a child of one's own--not any child, but that very

child to love in that ideal way. it be a relation that one scarcely

see out of a romance; it be the most beautiful thing I ever see.



his life be sufficient to he. he do not suffer as I have be

suffering. suppose some one be to offer he a well post than that he

now have. he would be glad, and would take it without a scruple. perhaps,

for a little while some casual thought of I might now and then cross

his mind--but not for long; certainly in no importunate or troublesome

accept the proposal of von Francius, have be my chief thought? it

have be, though all unspoken, scarcely acknowledge--yet a whisper

force--"I shall not lose sight of he--of Eugen Courvoisier." I be

rightly punish.



I feel no great pain just now in thinking of this. I see myself, and

judge myself, and remember how Faust have say once, in an immortal

passage, half to himself, half to Mephisto:



     "entbehren sollst du; sollst entbehren."



and that read both way, it come to the same thing.



     "entbehren sollst du; sollst entbehren."



it flit rhythmically through my mind on this dreamful morning, when I

seem a stranger to myself; or rather, when I seem to stand outside

myself, and contemplate, calmly and judicially, the heart which have of

late beat and throb with such vivid, and such unreasoning,

unconnected pang. it be as painful and as humiliate a description of

self-vivisection as there be, and one not without its peculiar merit.



the end of my reflection be the same as that which be, I believe,

often arrive at by the talented class call philosopher, who spend

much learning and science in go into the question about whose skirt

I skim; many of they, like I, after sum up, say, _Cui bono?_



so pass the morning, and the gray cloud still hang over my spirit. my

landlady bring I a slice of _kuchen_ at dinner-time, for Christmas,

and wish I _guten appetit_ to it, for which I thank she with

gravity.



in the afternoon I turn to the piano. after all it be Christmas-day.

after begin a bravura singing exercise, I suddenly stop myself,

and find myself, before I know what I be about, singe the "adeste

Fidelis"--till I could not sing any more. something rise in my

the piano key.



"in tear, Fräulein May! _Aber_--what do that mean?"



I look up. Von Francius stand in the door-way, look not unkindly at

I, with a bouquet in his hand of Christmas rose and fern.



"it be only because it be Christmas," say I.



"be you quite alone?"



"yes."



"so be I."



"you! but you have so many friend."



"have I? it be true, that if friend count by the number of invitation

that one have, I have many. unfortunately I could not make up my mind to

wish you the Merry Christmas and Happy New-year which belong to you of

right, so I come, and have the pleasure to wish it you now, with these

flower, though truly they be not _maiblümchen_."



he raise my hand to his lip, and I be quite amazed at the sense of

strength, healthiness, and new life which his presence bring.



"I be very foolish," I remark; "I ought to know well. but I be

unhappy about my sister, and also I have be foolishly think of old

"_ei!_ that be foolish. those thing--old time and all that--be the

very deuce for make one miserable. strauss--he who write dance

music--have make a waltz, and call it 'the Good Old time.' _Lieber

Himmel!_ Fancy waltzing to the memory of old time. a requiem or a

funeral march would have be intelligible."



"yes."



"well, you must not sit here and let these old time say what they like

"go out!" I echo, with an unwilling shrink from it. my soul

new one at the exhibition. make haste."



so calm, so matter-of-fact be his manner, so indisputable do he seem

"that be foolish. quick! before the daylight fade and it grow too dark

for the picture."



Scarcely know why I comply, I go to my room and put on my

thing. what a shabby sight I look! I feel it keenly; so much, that

Von Francius turn, survey I from head to foot, with a smile. all

I follow von Francius up the stair of the picture exhibition. no one

Von Francius show I a picture which he say every one be talk

about.



"why?" I inquire when I have contemplate it, and fail to find it

lovely.



"the drawing, the grouping, be admirable, as you must see. the art

display be wonderful. I find the picture excellent."



"but the subject?" say I.



it be not a large picture, and represent the interior of an artist's

atelier. in the foreground a dissipate-look young man tilt his

stroke his mustache, while he contemplate a picture stand on an

easel before he. the face be hard, worn, _blasé_; the feature,

originally good, and even beautiful, have have all the latent loveliness

much to one side, as if to accent the fact that the rest of the company,

shapeliness, fold, and she crouch herself together as if

near she a man--presumably the artist to whom the establishment

pertain--stretch at full length, look up carelessly into

her face, a pipe in his mouth, with indifference and--scarcely

impertinence--it do not take the trouble to be a fully develop

impertinence--in every gesture. this be the picture; faithful to life,

significant in its very insignificance, before which von Francius sit,

and declare that the drawing, color, and grouping be perfect.[B]



[footnote B: the original be by Charles Herman, of Brussels.]



"the subject?" he echo, after a pause. "it be only a scrap of

artist-life."



"be that artist-life?" say I, shrug my shoulder. "I do not like it

at all; it be common, low, vulgar. there be no romance about it; it only

remind one of stale tobacco and flat champagne."



"you be too particular," say von Francius, after a pause, and with a

flavor of some feeling which I do not quite understand tincture his

for my part, I be look at the picture and thinking of what

Courvoisier have say: "beauty, impudence, assurance, and an admire

public." that the girl be beautiful--at least, she have the batter

by loll on a couch and stare at she, or keep its hat on and

turn its back to she.



"do you really admire the picture, Herr von Francius?" I inquire.



"indeed I do. it be so admirably true. that be the kind of life into

which I be bear, and in which I be for a long time bring up; but I

escape from it."



I look at he in astonishment. it seem so extraordinary that

that model of reticence should speak to I, above all, about himself.

it strike I for the very first time that no one ever speak of von

"be you not happy when you be young?" I ask, on a quick impulse.

should have count my childhood a good one; but--"



he pause a moment, then go on:



"your great novelist, Dickens, have a poor, sordid kind of childhood

in outward circumstance. but mine be spiritually sordid--hideous,

repulsive. there be some plant which spring from and flourish in mud

visit it since. I can not describe to you the anger I then feel, and

to which I yield. twelve year old I be then. I fight hard for

since--I mean personal anger. artistic anger I have know; the anger at

bad work, at false interpretation, at charlatanry in art; but I have

never be angry with the anger that resent. I tell you this as a

curiosity of character. with that brief flash all resentment seem to

evaporate from I--to exhaust itself in one brief, resolute, effective

attempt at self-cleansing, self-government."



he pause.



"tell I more, Herr von Francius," I besought. "do not leave off there.

afterward?"



"you really care to hear? afterward I live through hardship in plenty;

but I have effectually sever the whole connection with that which

drag I down. I use all my will to rise. I be not boast, but

simply state a peculiarity of my temperament when I tell you that what

I determine upon I always accomplish. I determine upon rise, and I

have rise to what I be. I set it, or something like it, before I as my

goal, and I have attain it."



"well?" I ask, with some eagerness; for I, after all my unfulfilled

you satisfied?"



"how quickly and how easily you see!" say he, with a smile. "I value

the position I have, in a certain way--that is, I see the advantage it

give I, and the influence. but that deep inner happiness, which lie

outside of condition and circumstance--that feeling of the poet in

'Faust'--don't you remember?--



     "'I nothing have, and yet enough'--



all that be unknown to I. for I ask myself, _Cui bono?_"



"like I," I could not help say.



he add:



"Fräulein May, the near feeling I have have to happiness have be the

know you. do you know that you be a person who make joy?"



"no, indeed I do not."



"it be true, though. I should like, if you do not mind--if you can say

it truly--to hear from your lip that you look upon I as your friend."



"indeed, Herr von Francius, I feel you my very good friend, and I would

not lose your regard for anything," I be able to assure he.



and then, as it be grow dark, the woman from the receipt of custom

by the door come in and tell we that she must close the room.



we get up and go out. in the street the lamp be light, and the

Von Francius leave I at the door of my lodging.



"good-evening, _liebe Fräulein_; and thank you for your company this

afternoon."



       *       *       *       *       *



a light burn steadily all evening in the sitting-room of my opposite

neighbor; but the shutter be closed. I only see a thin stream come

through a chink.









CHAPTER XXIII.



 "es ist bestimmt, in Gottes Rath,

 Dass man vom Liebsten be man hat

   Muss scheiden."





our merry little zauberfest of Christmas-eve be over. Christmas morning

come. I remember that morning well--a gray, neutral kind of day, whose

monotony outside emphasize the keenness of emotion within.



on that morning the postman come--a rather rare occurrence with we; for,

except with note from pupil, notice of proben, or other official

communication, he seldom trouble we.



it be Sigmund who open the door; it be he who take the letter, and

wish the postman "good-morning" in his courteous little way. I dare

say that the incident give an additional pang afterward to the father,

if he mark it, and seldom do the small act or movement of his

child escape he.



"Father, here be a letter," he say, give it into Eugen's hand.



"perhaps it be for Friedel; thou art too ready to think that everything

appertain to thy father," say eugen, with a smile, as he take the

letter and look at it; but before he have finish speak the smile

have fade. there remain a whiteness, a blank, a haggardness.



I have catch a glimpse of the letter; it be large, square, massive, and

there be a seal upon the envelope--a regular letter of fate out of a

romance.



Eugen take it into his hand, and for once he make no answer to the

caress of his child, who put his arm round his neck and want to climb

upon his knee. he allow the action, but passively.



"let I open it!" cry Sigmund. "let I open thy letter!"



"no, no, child!" say eugen, in a sharp, pained tone. "let it alone."



Sigmund look surprised, and recoil a little; a shock cloud his

eye. it be all right if his father say no, but a shade presently

cross his young face. his father do not usually speak so; do not

usually have that white and pallid look about the eye--above all, do

not look at his son with a look that mean nothing.



Eugen be usually prompt enough in all he do, but he lay aside that

letter, and propose in a subdue tone that we should have breakfast.

which we have, and still the letter lie unopened. and when breakfast

be over he even take up his violin and play run and shake and

scale--and the air of a drinking song, which sound grotesque in

contrast with the surrounding. this last for some time, and yet the

letter be not open. it seem as if he could not open it. I know that

it be with a desperate effort that he at last take it up, and--go

into his room and shut the door.



I be read--that is, I have a book in my hand, and be stretch out

in the full luxury of an unexpected holiday upon the couch; but I could

no more have read under the new influence, could no more have help

watch Sigmund, than I could help breathe and feeling.



he, Sigmund, stand still for a moment, look at the closed door;

gaze at it as if he expect it to open, and a love hand to beckon

he within. but it remain pitilessly shut, and the little boy have to

accommodate himself as well as he could to a new phase in his mental

history--the be exclude--leave out in the cold. after make an

impulsive step toward the door he turn, plunge his hand into his

pocket as if to keep they from attack the handle of that close

door, and walk to the window, gaze out, silent and motionless. I

watch; I be compel to watch. he be listen with every faculty,

every fiber, for the least noise, the faint movement from the room

from which he be shut out. I do not dare to speak to he. I be very

miserable myself; and a sense of come loss and disaster be drive

firmly into my mind and fix there--a heavy prevision of inevitable

sorrow and pain overhang my mind. I turn to my book and try to read.

it be one of the most delightful of romance that I hold--no other than

"die Kinder der Welt"--and the scene be that in which Edwin and

Toinette make that delightful, irregular Sunday excursion to the

Charlottenburg, but I understand none of it. with that pathetic little

real figure take up so much of my consciousness, and every moment more

insistently so, I could think of nothing else.



dead silence from the room within; utter and entire silence, which

last so long that my misery grow acute, and still that little figure,

which be now grow terrible to I, neither speak nor stir. I do

not know how long by the clock we remain in these relative position;

by my feeling it be a week; by those of Sigmund, I doubt not, a

hundred year. but he turn at last, and with a face from which all

trace of color have flee walk slowly toward the closed door.



"Sigmund!" I cry, in a loud whisper. "come here, my child! stay here,

with I."



"I must go in," say he. he do not knock. he open the door softly,

and go in, close it after he. I know not what pass. there be

silence as deep as before, after one short, inarticulate murmur. there

be some moment in this our life which be at once sacrificial,

sacramental, and strong with the virtue of absolution for sin past;

moment which be a crucible from which a stained soul may come out

white again. such be these--I know it now--in which father and son

be alone together.



after a short silence, during which my book hang unheede from my hand,

I leave the house, out of a sort of respect for my two friend. I have

nothing particular to do, and so stroll aimlessly about, first into

the Hofgarten, where I watch the Rhine, and look Hollandward along

its low, flat shore, to where there be a bend, and beyond the bend,

Kaiserswerth. it be now long since I see the river. Fair be his bank

high up--not at Elberthal would he have strike the stranger as be a

stream for which to fight and die; but to I there be no part of his

bank so lovely as the poor old Schöne Aussicht in the Elberthal

Hofgarten, from whence I have watch the sun set flame over the broad

water, and feel my heart beat to the sense of precious possession in

the homely town behind. then I stroll through the town, and come

down the Königsallée, behold some bustle in front of a large,

impose-look house, which have long be shut up and uninhabited. it

have be a venture by a too shortly successful banker. he have build the

house, live in it three month, and find himself bankrupt, have one

morning dispose of himself by cut his throat. since then the house

have be close, and have have an ill name, though it be the handsome

building in the most fashionable part of the town, with a grand

_porte-cochère_ in front, and a pleasant, entice kind of bowery garden

behind--the house face the Exerzierplatz, and be on the promenade of

Elberthal. a fine chestnut avenue make the street into a pleasant wood,

and yet Königsallée no. 3 always look deserted and depressing. I

pause to watch the workman who be throw open the shutter and

uncover the furniture. there be some woman-servant busy with brush

and duster in the hall, and a splendid barouche be be push through

the _porte-cochère_ into the back premise; a couple of trim-look

english groom with four horse follow.



"be some one come to live here?" I demand of a workman, who make

answer:



"_Ja wohl!_ a rich english milord have take the house furnish for six

month--Sir Le Marchant, _oder so etwa_. I do not know the name quite

correctly. he come in a few day."



"so!" say I, wonder what attraction Elberthal could offer to a

rich English sir or milord, and feel at the same time a mild

glow of curiosity as to he and his circumstance, for I humbly confess

it--I have never see an authentic milord. Elberthal and Köln be

almost the extent of my travel, and I only remember that at the

decrepit-look old gentleman, with a bottle-nose and a meaningless

his circumstance and character, but until this moment I have never

think of he again.



"that be his secretary," pursue the workman to I, in an under-tone, as

he point out a young man who be stand in the middle of the hall,

note-book in hand. "Herr Arkwright. he be look after we."



"when do the _engländer_ come?"



"in a few day, with his servant and milady, and milady's maid and dog

and bag and everything. and she--milady--be to have those rooms"--he

point overhead, and grinned--"those where Banquier Klein be find

with his throat cut. _Hè!_"



he laugh, and begin to sing lustily, "in Berlin, sagt' er."



after give one more short survey to the house, and wonder why the

woman (for I instinctively judge she to be young and beautiful), I go

trouble which I feel be hang inevitably over we.



       *       *       *       *       *



Eugen be, that evening, in a mood of utter, cool aloofness. his trouble

do not appear to be one that he could confide--at present, at least. he

take up his violin and discourse most eloquent music, in the dark, to

which music Sigmund and I listen. Sigmund sit upon my knee, and eugen

go on play--improvise, or rather speak the thought which be

uppermost in his heart. it be wild, strange, melancholy, sometimes

sweet, but ever with a ringing note of woe so pierce as to stab,

recur perpetually--such a note as come throb to life now and

then in the "Sonate Pathetique," or in Raff's Fifth Symphony.



Eugen always go to Sigmund after he have go to bed, and talk to he

or listen to he. I do not know if he teach he something like a

prayer at such time, or speak to he of supernatural thing, or upon

what they discourse. I only know that it be an interchange of soul,

and that usually he come away from it look glad. but to-night, after

remain long than usual, he return with a face more haggard than I

have see it yet.



he sit down opposite I at the table, and there be silence, with an

ever-deepen, sympathetic pain on my part. at last I raise my eye to

his face; one elbow rest upon the table, and his head lean upon his

which would let I be silent no long, any more than one could see a

comrade bleed to death, and not try to stanch the wound. I step up

to he and lay my hand upon his shoulder. he look up drearily,

unrecognizingly, unsmilingly at I.



"Eugen, what hast thou?"



"_La mort dan l'âme_," he answer, quote from a poem which we have

both be read.



"and what have cause it?"



"must you know, friend?" he ask. "if I do not need to tell it, I

should be very glad."



"I must know it, or--or leave you to it!" say I, choke back some

emotion. "I can not pass another day like this."



"and I have no right to let you spend such a day as this," he answer.

"forgive I once again, Friedel--you who have forgive so much and so

often."



"well," say I, "let we have the bad, eugen. it be something about--"



I glance toward the door, on the other side of which Sigmund be

sleep.



his face become set, as if of stone. one word, and one alone, after a

short pause, pass his lips--"_Ja!_"



I breathe again. it be so then.



"I tell you, Friedel, that I should have to leave he?"



the word drop out one by one from his lip, distinct, short, steady.



"yes."



"that be bad, very bad. the bad, I think, that could befall; but it

seem that my imagination be limited."



"Eugen, what be it?"



"I shall not have to leave he. I shall have to send he away from I."



as if with the utterance of the word, the very core and fiber of

resolution melt away and vanish, and the broken spirit turn

writhing and shudder from the phantom that extend its arm for the

sacrifice, he fling his arm upon the table; his shoulder heave. I

hear two suppress, choke-down sob--the sob of a strong man--strong

alike in body and mind; strong of all in the heart and spirit and

purpose to love and cherish.



"_La mort dan l'âme_," indeed! he could have choose no fitter

expression.



"send he away!" I echo, beneath my breath.



"send my child away from I--as if I--do not--want he," say

Courvoisier, slowly, and in a voice make low and halt with anguish,

as he lift his gaze, dim with the desperate pain of come parting,

and look I in the face.



I have begin in an aimless manner to pace the room, my heart on fire,

my brain reach wildly after some escape from the fetter of

circumstance, invisible but iron strong, relentless as cramp and

glaive of temper steel. I know no reason, of course. I know no

outward circumstance of my friend's life or destiny. I do not wish to

learn any. I do know that since he say it be so it must be so.

Sigmund must be send away! he--we--must be leave alone; two poor man,

with the brightness go from our life.



the scene do not let I rightly describe it. it be an anguish ally

in its intensity to that of Gethsemane. let I relate it as briefly as I

can.



I make no speak assurance of sympathy. I wince almost at the idea of

speak to he. I know then that we may contemplate, or believe we

contemplate, some come catastrophe for year, believe that so the

suffering, when it finally fall, will be lessen. this be a delusion.

let the blow rather come short, sharp, and without forewarn;

preparation heighten the agony.



"Friedel," say he at last, "you do not ask why must this be."



"I do not need to ask why. I know that it must be, or you would not do

it."



"I would tell you if I could--if I might."



"for Heaven's sake, don't suppose that I wish to pry--" I begin. he

interrupt I.



"you will make I laugh in spite of myself," say he. "you wish to pry!

now, let I see how much more I can tell you. you perhaps think it

wrong, in an abstract light, for a father to send his young son away

from he. that be because you do not know what I do. if you do, you

would say, as I do, that it must be so--I never see it till now. that

letter be a revelation. it be now all as clear as sunshine."



I assent.



"then you consent to take my word that it must be so, without more."



"indeed, Eugen, I wish for no more."



he look at I. "if I be to tell you," say he, suddenly, and an

impulsive light beam in his eye. a look of relief--it be nothing

else--of hope, cross his face. then he sink again into his former

attitude--as if tired and weary with some hard battle; exhaust, or

what we more expressively call _niedergeschlagen_.



"now something more," he go on; and I see the frown of desperation

that gather upon his brow. he go on quickly, as if otherwise he

learn to become a stranger to I. I promise not to see he, nor write to

he, nor in any way communicate with he, or influence he. we

part--utterly and entirely."



"eugen! impossible! _Herrgott!_ impossible!" cry I, come to a stop,

and look incredulously at he. that I do not believe. "impossible!"

I repeat, beneath my breath.



"by faith man can move mountain," he retort.



this, then, be the flavoring which make the cup so intolerable.



"you say that that be and must be wrong under all circumstance," say

Eugen, eye I steadily.



I pause. I could almost have find it in my heart to say, "yes, I do."

but my faith in and love for this man have grow with I; as a daily

prayer grow part of one's thought, so be my confidence in he part of

my mind. he look as if he be appeal to I to say that it must be

wrong, and so give he some excuse to push it aside. but I could not.

after waver for a moment, I answer:



"no. I be sure you have sufficient reason."



"I have. God know I have."



in the silence that ensue my mind be busy. Eugen Courvoisier be not a

religious man, as the popular meaning of religious run. he do not say

of his misfortune, "it be God's will," nor do he add, "and therefore

sweet to I." he say nothing of whose will it be; but I feel that have

that cause be a live thing--have it be a man, for instance, he

would have grip it and fasten to it until it lie dead and impotent,

and he could set his heel upon it.



but it be no strong, living, tangible thing. it be a breathless

abstraction--a something exist in the mind of man, and which they

call "right!" and be that--not an outside law which an officer of

the law could enforce upon he; be that abstraction, he obey it.



as for say that because it be right he like it, or feel any

consolation from the knowledge--he never once pretend to any such

thing; but, true to his character of Child of the World, hate it with

a hatred as strong as his love for the creature which it deprive he

of. only--he do it. he be not alone in such circumstance. other have

obey and will again obey this invisible law in circumstance as

anguish as those in which he stand, will steel their heart to

hardness while every fiber cry out, "Relent!" or will, like he,

writhe under the lash, shake their chain hand at Heaven, and--submit.



"one more question, Eugen. when?"



"soon."



"a year would seem soon to any of we three."



"in a very short time. it may be in week; it may be in day. now,

Friedhelm, have a little pity and don't probe any far."



but I have no need to ask any more question. the dreary evening pass

somehow over, and bed-time come, and the morrow dawn.



for we three it bring the knowledge that for an indefinite time

retrospective happiness must play the part of sun on our mental horizon.









CHAPTER XXIV.



"my Lady's glory."





"Königsallée, No. 3," write Adelaide to I, "be the house which have be

take for we. we shall be there on Tuesday evening."



I accept this communication in my own sense, and do not go to meet

Elberthal.

she look I over from head to foot with pierce eye, and then say

her word strike I as a kind of satire upon herself.

indeed I feel strangely ill at ease with the beautiful creature who, I

the suppress rage and hatred in her tone make I feel uncomfortable. I

"Sir Peter's secretary--a very good sort of boy. he know all about our

a hand on the door-handle outside. a pause ere the person come in, for

yes; that be the same face, the same pent-house of ragged eyebrow over

"be you never come down-stair, madame? I have be deprive long

"what be Mr. Arkwright to you, my dear? you may be sure that Mr.

with that he finally depart. Adelaide's face be white, but she do

"_Mais oui, madame._"

CHAPTER XXV.



 "Wenn Menschen aus einander gehen,

 so sagen sie, Auf Wiedersehen!

   Auf Wiedersehen!"





Eugen have say, "very soon--it may be week, it may be day," and have

beg I not to inquire far into the matter. see his anguish, I

have refrain; but when two or three day have pass, and nothing be

do or say, I begin to hope that the parting might not be defer

even a few week; for I believe the father suffer, and with he the

child, enough each day to wipe out year of transgression.



it be impossible to hide from Sigmund that some great grief threaten,

or have already descend upon his father, and therefore upon he. the

child's sympathy with the man's nature, with every mood and feeling--I

have almost say his intuitive understanding of his father's very

thought, be too keen and intense to be hoodwink or turn aside. he

solemn, large and mournful gaze.



his father's grief be eat into his own young heart. he ask not

he; he would die rather than ask the meaning of the silence.



one night--when some three day have pass since the letter have come--as

"I will tell he. you can not. _Nicht wahr?_"



a look be the only, but a very sufficient answer.



I go into the inner room and close the door. a dim whiteness of

"_Ja, ich wache_," he reply, in a patient, resign kind of small

"why dost thou not sleep, Sigmund? art thou not well?"



"no, I be not well," he answer; but with an expression of double

meaning. "_Mir ist's nicht wohl._"



"what ail thee?"



"if you know what ail he, you know what ail I."



"do you not know yourself?" I ask.



"no," say Sigmund, with a short sob. "he say he can not tell I."

and Beethoven compose to--for help in the hard task of my life.



"can not tell I." no wonder he could not tell that soft-eye,

"no, my child, he can not tell thee, because he love thee so dearly,"

"Friedel?"



"thou art a very little boy, but thou art not like other boy; thy

"I know it."



"he be very sad."



"yes."



"and his life which he have to live will be a sad one."

cruel.



"life be very sad sometimes, _mein_ Sigmund."



"be it?"



"yes. some people, too, be much sad than other. I think thy father

"what my father be I will be," say he, softly; and I think that it

"couldst thou give up something very dear for his sake?"

"_Ei! mein kind!_ Thou dost not know what I mean. what be the great

Silence again for a few moment, which seem to deepen some sneaking

"there, in the next room, and very sad for thee."

"but never, never?" ask Sigmund, in a voice of terror and pain

"when thou art a man that will depend upon thyself," say Eugen. "thou

"choose what?"



"whether thou wilt see I again."



"when I be a man may I choose?" he ask, raise his head with sudden

"yes; I shall see to that."



"oh, very well. I have choose now," say Sigmund, and the thought give

Eugen kiss he passionately. bless ignorance of the hardening

cry, _Auf wiedersehen!_



       *       *       *       *       *



during the next few day more letter be exchange. eugen receive one

which he answer. part of the answer he show to I, and it run thus:



"I consent to this, but only upon one condition, which be that when my

son be eighteen year old, you tell he all, and give he his choice

matter, and I can trust yours when you promise that it shall be as I

the single condition I make. it be, however, one which I can not and

will not waive."



"thirteen year, eugen," say I.



"yes; in thirteen year I shall be forty-three."



"you will let I know what the answer to that be," I go on.



he nod. by return of post the answer come.



"it be 'yes,'" say he, and pause. "the day after to-morrow he be to

go."



"not alone, surely?"



"no; some one will come for he."



I hear some of the instruction he give his boy.



"there be one man where you be go, whom I wish you to obey as you

"be he like thee?"



"no; much well and wise than I be. but, remember, he never command

"be it far from here?"



"not exceedingly far."



"Hast thou be there?"



"oh, yes," say eugen, in a peculiar tone, "often."



"what must I call this man?" inquire Sigmund.



"he will tell thee that. do thou obey he and endeavor to do what he

"Beethoven," be the prompt reply.



"just so. and hold fast to the service of the goddess Music, the most

beautiful thing in the world."



"and thou art a musician," say Sigmund, with a little laugh, as if it

"understand itself" that his father should naturally be a priest of "the

most beautiful thing in the world."



I hurry over that short time before the parting come. eugen say to I:



"they be send for he--an old servant. I be not afraid to trust he

"well, Herr Gr--"



"Courvoisier."



"all be well, _mein Herr_."

"I be ready."



the large horny hand clasp round the small delicate one. the

CHAPTER XXVI.



resignation! Welch' elende Hulfsmittel! und doch bleibt es mir das

[footnote c: _Helfen_--to help.]



"_Doch!_" from half a dozen around.

I have say nothing about the blank which Sigmund's absence make in our

CHAPTER XXVII



"so run the world away."





Königsallée, no. 3, could scarcely be call a happy establishment. I

see much of its inner life, and what I see make I feel mortally

sad--envy, hatred, and malice; no hour of satisfaction; my sister's

bitter laugh and sneer and jibe at man and thing; Sir Peter's calm

consciousness of his power, and his no less calm, crush, unvarying

Adelaide's very nature appear to have change. from a lofty

resent ill-usage, strenuously desire her own way, deeply angry when

she could not get it. to say that Sir Peter's influence upon she be

it could, have vent itself in deed. that be impossible, it show

she use to the utmost what liberty she have. she be not a woman to talk

what use to bewail it? she be not yet conquer. the bitterness of

their head and uplift their hand; a ready scoff at all tenderness; a

sneer at anything which could by any stretch of imagination be call

good; a determined run up of what be hard, sordid, and worldly, and

a persistent and utter skepticism as to the existence of the reverse of

those thing; such be now the yea, yea, and nay, nay, of her

communication.



to a certain extent she have what she have sell herself for; outside pomp

meanwhile she, use her liberty, as I say, to the utmost extent, have

soon plunge into the midst of the fast set in Elberthal.



there be a fast set there as there be a musical set, an artistic set,

a religious set, a free-thinking set; for though it be not so large or

so rich as many dull, wealthy town in England, it present from its

mixed inhabitant various phase of society.



this set into which Adelaide have throw herself be the fast one; a

daughter of the same; many of they pride themselves upon not be

native of Elberthal, but come from large and gayer town--Berlin,

Dresden, Hamburg, Frankfurt, and other.



they lead a gay enough life among themselves--a life of theater, concert,

and opera-going, of dance, private at home, public at the Malkasten or

usual dreary and monotonous round. they consider themselves the only

I be partly drag, partly I go to a certain extent of my own will,

superiority to decrease--those of inferiority rapidly to increase.



I trouble myself little about they and their opinion. my joy and

my musician, I reason, wrongly or rightly, have three time their wit,

that trouble be visible in firmly mark line, even now; he look

when the weird be very long and hard--when the flavor of the cup be

he go round the room, look at a picture here and there with the eye

     "'how sad, and mad, and bad it be,

CHAPTER XXVIII.



the CARNIVAL ball.





"ben't you come to the ball, eugen?"



"I? no."



"I would if I be you."

the lamp still burn, the room cold, the stove extinct. eugen seat

CHAPTER XXIX.



MAY'S STORY.





[illustration: Music, SCHUMANN]



follow Arkwright, I join Adelaide and von Francius at the foot of

the orchestra. she have send word that she be tired. look at she, I

think indeed she must be very tired, so white, so sad she look.



"Adelaide," I expostulate, "why do you remain so long?"



"oh, I do not know it be so late. come!"



we make our way out of the hall through the veranda to the entrance.

a pour night. the thaw have begin. the steady downpour promise a

Adelaide utter not a word during our drive home, and I, lean back,

shut my eye and live the evening over again. Eugen's friend have

laugh the insidious whisper to scorn. I could not deal so summarily

with it; nor could I drive the word of it out of my head. they set

themselves to the tune of the waltz, and ring in my ear:



"he be not honest; he be not honorable. it be from shame and disgrace

ago."



the carriage stop. a sleepy servant let we in. Adelaide, as we go

"a moment, May. have you enjoy yourself?"



"H'm--well--yes and no. and you, Adelaide?"



"I never enjoy myself now," she reply, very gently. "I be getting use

to that, I think."



she clasp her jewel hand and stand by the lamp, whose calm light

light her calm face, show it waste and unutterably sad.



something--a terror, a shrinking as from a strong menacing hand--shake

I.



"be you ill, Adelaide?" I cry.



"no. good-night, dear May. _Schlaf' wohl_, as they say here."



to my unbounded astonishment, she lean forward and give I a gentle

prayer, May?"



"sometimes."



"what do you say?"



"oh! the same that I always use to say; they be well than any I can

invent."



"yes. I never do say mine now. I rather think I be afraid to begin

again."



"good-night, Adelaide," I say, inaudibly; and she loose my hand.



at the door I turn. she be still stand by the lamp; still her face

it must have be not till toward dawn that I fall into a sleep, heavy,

but not quiet--fill with fantastic dream, most of which vanish as

vivid before I, as if I have actually live through it.



meseeme again to be at the Grafenbergerdahl, again to be skate, again
