and as I sit thus, it seem that some one come and sit beside I

without speak, and I do not turn to look at he; but ever as I sit

there and feel that he be beside I, the sadness lift from my heart,

until it grow so full of joy that tear rise to my eye. then he who be

beside I place his hand upon mine, and I look at he. it be Eugen

Courvoisier. his face and his eye be full of sadness; but I know

that he love I, though he say but one word, "Forgive!" to which I

answer, "can you forgive?" but I know that I allude to something much

deep than that silly little episode of having cut he at the theater.

he bow his head; and then I think I begin to weep, cover my face

with my hand; but they be tear of exquisite joy, and the peace at my

heart be the most entire I have ever feel. and he loosen my hand, and

draw I to he and kiss I, say "my love!" and as I feel--yes,

actually feel--the pressure of his lip upon mine, and feel the spring

shine upon I, and hear the very echo of the twitter of the bird,

see the light fall upon the water, and smell the scent of the acacias,

and see the Lotus-blume as she--



     "Duftet und weinet und zittert

     Vor Liebe und Liebesweh,"



I awake, and confront a gray February morning, feel a raw chilliness

in the air, hear a cold, pitiless rain drive against the window; know

that my head ache, my heart harmonize therewith; that I be awake, not

in a dream; that there have be no spring morning, no acacias, no

nightingale; above all, no love--remember last night, and rouse to

the consciousness of another day, the necessity of wake up and live

on.



nor could I rest or sleep. I rise and contemplate through the window

the drive rain and the soak street, the sorrowful naked tree, the

plain of the parade ground, which look a mere waste of mud and

half-melt ice; the long plain line of the Caserne itself--a cheering

prospect truly!



when I go down-stair I find Sir Peter, in heavy travel overcoat,

stand in the hall; a carriage stand at the door; his servant be

put in his master's luggage and rug. I pause in astonishment. Sir

Peter look at I and smile with the dubious benevolence which he be

in the habit of extend to I.



"I be very sorry to be oblige to quit your charming society, Miss

Wedderburn, but business call I imperatively to England; and, at

least, I be sure that my wife can not be unhappy with such a companion

as her sister."



"you be go to England?"



"I be go to England. I have be call so hastily that I can make no

arrangement for Adelaide to accompany I, and indeed it would not be at

all pleasant for she, as I be only go on business; but I hope to

return for she and bring her home in a few week. I be leave Arkwright

with you. he will see that you have all you want."



Sir Peter be smile, ever smile, with the smile which be my horror.



"a brilliant ball, last night, be it not?" he add, extend his

hand to I, in farewell, and look at I intently with eye that

fascinate and repel I at once.



"very, but--but--you be not there?"



"be I not? I have a strong impression that I be. ask my lady if she

think I be there. and now good-bye, and _au revoir_!"



he loosen my hand, descend the step, enter the carriage, and be

drive away. his departure ought to have raise a great weight from my

mind, but it do not; it impress I with a sense of come disaster.



Adelaide breakfast in her room. when I have finish I go to she. her

behavior puzzle I. she seem elated, excited, at the absence of Sir

Peter, and yet, suddenly turn to I, she exclaim, eagerly:



"oh, May! I wish I have be go to England, too! I wish I could leave

this place, and never see it again."



"be Sir Peter at the ball, Adelaide?" I ask.



she turn suddenly pale; her lip tremble; her eye waver, as she say

in a low, uneasy voice:



"I believe he be--yes; in domino."



"what a sneak thing to do!" I remark, candidly. "he have tell we

particularly that he be not come."



"that very statement should have put we on our guard," she remark.



"on our guard? against what?" I ask, unsuspectingly.



"oh, nothing--nothing! I wonder when he will return! I would give a

world to be in England!" she say, with a heartsick sigh; and I, feel

very much bewildered, leave she.



in the afternoon, despite wind and weather, I sally forth, and take my

way to my old lodging in the Wehrhahn. cross a square lead to the

street I be go to, I meet Anna Sartorius. she bow, look at I

mockingly. I return her salutation, and remember last night again

with painful distinctness. the air seem full of mystery and

uncertainty; they clung about my mind like cobwebs, and I could not

get rid of their soft, stifle influence.



having arrive at my lodging, I mount the stair. Frau Lutzler meet

I.



"_na_, _na_, Fräulein! you do not patronize I much now. my room be

become too small for you, I reckon."



"indeed, Frau Lutzler, I wish I have never be in any large one," I

answer she, earnestly.



"so! well, 'tis true you look thin and worn--not as well as you use to.

and be you--but I hear you be, so where's the use of tell lie

about it--at the Maskenball last night? and how do you like it?"



"oh, it be all very new to I. I never be at one before."



"_nicht?_ then you must have be astonish. they say there be a

Mephisto so good he would have deceive the devil himself. and you,

Fräulein--I hear that you look very beautiful."



"so! it must have be a mistake."



"_Doch nicht!_ I have always maintain that at certain time you be

far from bad-look, and dress and get up for the stage, would be

absolutely handsome. nearly any one can be that--if you be not too

near the foot-light, that be, and don't go behind the scene."



with which neat slaying of a particular compliment by a general one, she

release I, and let I go on my way upstairs.



here I have some book and some music. but the room be cold; the book

fail to interest I, and the music do not go--the piano be like

I--out of tune. and yet I feel the need of some musical expression

of the mood that be upon I. I bethink myself of the Tonhalle,

next door, almost, and that in the rittersaal it would be quiet and

undisturbed, as the ball that night be not to be hold there, but in

one of the large room of the Caserne.



without pause to think a second time of the plan, I leave the house and

go to the Tonhalle, only a few step away. in consequence of the rain

and bad weather almost every trace of the carnival have disappear. I

find the Tonhalle desert save by a bar-maid at the restauration. I

ask she if the rittersaal be open, and she say yes. I pass on. as

I draw near the door I hear music; the piano be already be play.

could it be von Francius who be there? I do not think so. the touch

be not his--neither so practiced, so brilliant, nor so sure.



satisfy, after listen a moment, that it be not he, I resolve to

go in and pass through the room. if it be any one whom I could send

away I would do so, if not, I could go away again myself.



I enter. the room be somewhat dark, but I go in and have almost

come to the piano before I recognize the player--Courvoisier. overcome

with vexation and confusion at the _contretemp_, I pause a moment,

undecided whether to turn back and go out again. in any case I resolve

not to remain in the room. he be seat with his back to I, and still

continue to play. some music be on the desk of the piano before he.



I might turn back without be observe. I would do so. hardly,

though--a mirror hang directly before the piano, and I now see that

while he continue to play, he be quietly look at I, and that his

keen eye--that hawk's glance which I know so well--must have recognize

I. that decide I. I would not turn back. it would be a silly,

senseless proceeding, and would look much more invidious than my

remain. I walk up to the piano, and he turn, still play.



"_Guten Tag, mein Fräulein._"



I merely bow, and begin to search through a pile of song and music

upon the piano. I would at any rate take some away with I to give some

color to my proceeding. meanwhile he play on.



I select a song, not in the least know what it be, and roll it

up, be turn away.



"be you busy, Miss Wedderburn?"



"N--no."



"would it be ask too much of you to play the pianoforte

accompaniment?"



"I will try," say I, speak briefly, and slowly draw off my

glove.



"if it be disagreeable to you, don't do it," say he, pause.



"not in the very least," say I, avoid look at he.



he open the music. it be one of Jensen's "Wanderbilder" for piano and

violin--the "Kreuz be Wege."



"I have only try it once before," I remark, "and I be a dreadful

bungler."



"_Bitte sehr!_" say he, smile, arrange his own music on one of the

stand and add, "now I be ready."



I find my hand tremble so much that I could scarcely follow the

music. truly this man, with his change from silence to talkativeness,

from ironical hardness to cordiality, be a puzzle and a trial to I.



"Das Kreuz be Wege" turn out rather lame. I say so when it be over.



"suppose we try it again," he suggest, and we do so. I find my

finger linger and forget their part as I listen to the

pierce beauty of his note.



"that be dismal," say he.



"it be a dismal subject, be it not?"



"Suggestive, at least. 'the Cross by the Wayside.' well, I have a mind

for something more cheerful. do you leave the ball early last night?"



"no; not very early."



"do you enjoy it?"



"it be all new to I--very interesting--but I don't think I quite

enjoy it."



"ah, you should see the ball at Florence, or Venice, or Vienna!"



he smile as he lean back, as if think over past scene.



"yes," say I, dubiously, "I don't think I care much for such thing,

though it be interesting to watch the little drama go on around."



"and to act in it," I also think, remember Anna Sartorius and her

whisper, and I look at he. "not honest, not honorable. hide from

shame and disgrace."



I look at he and do not believe it. for the moment the torturing

idea leave I. I be free from it and at peace.



"be you go to practice?" he ask. "I fear I disturb you."



"oh, no! it do not matter in the least. I shall not practice now."



"I want to try some other thing," say he, "and Friedhelm's and my

piano be not loud enough for I, nor be there sufficient space between

our wall for the sound of a symphony. do you not know the mood?"



"yes."



"but I be afraid to ask you to accompany I."



"why?"



"you seem unwilling."



"I be not: but I should have suppose that my unwillingness--if I have

be unwilling--would have be an inducement to you to ask I."



"_Herrgott!_ why?"



"since you take a vow to be disagreeable to I, and to make I hate

you."



a slight flush pass rapidly over his face, as he pause for a moment

and bit his lip.



"_mein Fräulein_--that night I be in bitterness of spirit--I hardly

know what I be saying--"



"I will accompany you," I interrupt he, my heart beat. "only how

can I begin unless you play, or tell I what you want to play?"



"true," say he, laugh, and yet not move from his place beside

the piano, upon which he have lean his elbow, and across which he now

look at I with the self-same kindly, genial glance as that he have

cast upon I across the little table at the Köln restaurant. and yet not

the self-same glance, but another, which I would not have exchange for

that first one.



if he would but begin to play I feel that I should not mind so much; but

when he sit there and look at I and half smile, without begin

anything practical, I feel the situation at least try.



he raise his eye as the door open at the other end of the saal.



"ah, there be Friedhelm," say he, "now he will take second."



"then I will not disturb you any long."



"on the contrary," say he, lay his hand upon my wrist. (my dream of

the morning flash into my mind.) "it would be well if you remain,

then we could have a trio. Friedel, come here! you be just in time.

Fräulein Wedderburn will be good enough to accompany we, and we can try

the Fourth Symphony."



"what you call 'spring'?" inquire Helfen, come up smilingly. "with

all my heart. where be the score?"



"what you call Spring?" be it possible that in winter--on a cold and

unfriendly day--we be go to have spring, leafy bloom, the desert

fill with leaping spring, and blossoming like a rose? full of wonder,

surprise, and a certain excitement at the idea, I sit still and think

of my dream, and the rain beat against the window, and a draughty wind

flutter the tinselly decoration of last night. the floor be strew

with fragment of garment tear in the crush--paper and silken flower,

here a rosette, there a buckle, a satin bow, a tinsel spangle. bench

and table be pile about the room, which be half dark; only to

westward, through one window, be visible a pale gleam, which might by

comparison be call light.



the two young man turn over the music, laugh at something, and

chaff each other. I never in my life see two such entire friend as

these; they seem to harmonize most perfectly in the midst of their

unlikeness to each other.



"Excuse that we keep you wait, _mein Fräulein_," say Courvoisier,

place some music before I. "this fellow be so slow, and will put

everything into order as he use it."



"well for you that I be, _mein lieber_," say Helfen, composedly. "if

any one have the enterprise to offer a prize to the most extravagant,

untidy fellow in Europe, the palm would be your--by a long way too."



"Friedel bind his music and number it," observe Courvoisier. "it be

one of the most beautiful and affect of sight to behold he with

scissor, paste-pot, brush and binding. it occur periodically about

four time a year, I think, and move I almost to tear when I see it."



"_Der edle Ritter_ leave his music unbound, and borrow mine on every

possible occasion when his own property be scatter to the four wind

of heaven."



"_Aber! aber!_" cry Eugen. "that be too much! I call Frau Schmidt to

witness that all my music be put in one place."



"I never say it ben't. but you never can find it when you want it, and

the confusion be delightfully increase by your constantly rush off

to buy a new _partitur_ when you cann't find the old one; so you have

three or four of each."



"this be all to show off what he consider his own good quality; a

certain slow, methodical plodding and a good memory, which be natural

gift, but which he boast of as if they be acquire virtue. he bind

his music because he be a pedant and a prig, and cann't help it; a bad

fellow to get on with. now, _mein bester_, for the 'fruhling.'"



"but the Fräulein ought to have it explain," expostulate Helfen,

laugh. "every one have not the misfortune to be so well acquaint

with you as I be. he have rather insane fancy sometimes," he add,

turn to I, "without rhyme or reason that I be aware, and he choose

to assert that Beethoven's Fourth Symphony, or the chief motive of it,

occur to he on a spring day, when the master be, for a time, quite

charm from his bitter humor, and have, perhaps, some one by his side

who put his heart in tune with the spring song of the bird, the green

of the grass, the scent of the flower. so he call it the 'Fruhling

Symphonie,' and will persist in play it as such. I call the idea

rather far-fetched, but then that be nothing unusual with he."



"having say your remarkably stupid say, which Miss Wedderburn have far

too much sense to heed in the least, suppose you allow we to begin,"

say Courvoisier, give the other a push toward his violin.



but we be destine to have yet another coadjutor in the shape of Karl

Linders, who at that moment stroll in, and be hail by his friend

with jubilation.



"come and help! your 'cello will give just the mellowness that be

want," say eugen.



"I must go and get it then," say Karl, look at I.



Eugen, with an indescribable expression as he intercept the glance,

introduce we to one another. Karl and Friedhelm Helfen go off to

another part of the Tonhalle to fetch Karl's violoncello, and we be

leave alone again.



"perhaps I ought not to have introduce he. I forget 'Lohengrin,'" say

Eugen.



"you know that you do not," say I, in a low voice.



"no," he answer, almost in the same tone. "it be think of that

which lead I to introduce poor old Karl to you. I think, perhaps, that

you would accept it as a sign--will you?"



"a sign of what?"



"that I feel myself to have be in the wrong throughout--and forgive."



as I sit, amazed and a little awed at this almost literal fulfillment of

my dream, the other return.



Karl contribute the tone of his mellowest of instrument, which he

play with a certain pleasant breadth and brightness of color, and

my dream come ever truer and truer. the symphony be as spring-like

as possible. we try it nearly all through; the hymn-like and yet

fairy-like first movement; the second, that song of universal love,

joy, and thanksgiving, with Beethoven's masculine hand evident

throughout. to the note there seem to fall a sunshine into the room,

and we could see the field cast their covering of snow, and wither

tree burst into bloom; brook swell with warm rain, bird busy at

nest-making; clump of primrose on velvet leave, and the subtle scent

of violet; youth and maiden with love in their eye; and even a hint

of later warmth, when hedge should be white with hawthorn, and the

woodland slope look, with their sheet of hyacinth, as if some of

heaven's blue have be spill upon earth's grass.



as the last strong, melodious modulation cease, Courvoisier point to

one of the window.



"Friedhelm, you wretche unbeliever, behold the refutation of your

theory. the symphony have bring the sun out."



"for the first time," say Friedhelm, as he turn his earnest young

face with its fringe of loose brown hair toward the sneak sun-ray,

which be certainly look shyly in. "as a rule the very heavens weep

at the performance. don't you remember the last time we try it, it

begin to rain instantly?"



"Miss Wedderburn's co-operation must have secure its success then on

this occasion," say eugen, gravely, glance at I for a moment.



"Hear! hear!" murmur Karl, screw up his violoncello and smile

furtively.



"oh, I be afraid I hinder rather than help," say I, "but it be very

beautiful."



"but not like spring, be it?" ask Friedhelm.



"well, I think it be."



"there! I know she would declare for I," say Courvoisier, calmly, at

which Karl Linders look up in some astonishment.



"shall we try this 'traumerei,' Miss Wedderburn, if you be not too

tired?"



I turn willingly to the piano, and we play Schumann's little

"dream."



"ah," say eugen, with a deep sigh (and his face have grow sad), "ben't

that the essence of sweetness and poetry? here's another which be

lovely. 'Noch ein Paar,' _nicht wahr?_"



"and it will be 'noch ein Paar' until our finger drop off," scold

Friedhelm, who seem, however, very willing to await that consummation.

we go through many of the Kinderscenen and some of the Kreissleriana,

and just as we finish a sweet little "bittende Kind," the twilight

grow almost into darkness, and Courvoisier lay his violin down.



"Miss Wedderburn, thank you a thousand time!"



"oh, _bitte sehr_!" be all I could say. I want to say so much more;

to say that I have be make happy; my sadness dispel, a dream half

fulfil, but the word stick, and have they come ever so flowingly I

could not have utter they with Friedhelm Helfen, who know so much,

look at we, and Karl Linders on his good behavior in what he

consider superior company.



I do not know how it be that Karl and Friedhelm, as we all come from

the Tonhalle, walk off to the house, and Eugen and I be leave to walk

alone through the soak street, empty of all their reveler, and

along the dripping Königsallée, with its leafless chestnut, to Sir

Peter's house. it be cold, it be wet--cheerless, dark, and dismal, and

I be very happy--very insanely so. I give a glance once or twice at my

companion. the brightness have leave his face; it be stern and wear

again, and his lip set as if with the repression of some pain.



"Herr Courvoisier, have you hear from your little boy?"



"no."



"no?"



"I do not expect to hear from he, _mein Fräulein_. when he leave I we

part altogether."



"oh, how dreadful!"



no answer. and we speak no more until he say "good-evening" to I at

the door of no. 3. as I go in I reflect that I might never meet he

thus face to face again. be it an opportunity miss, or be it a brief

glimpse of unexpected joy?









CHAPTER XXX.



the truth.





as day go on and grow into week, and week pair off until a month

pass, and I still see the same stricken look upon my sister's face,

my heart grow full of foreboding.



one morning the astonishing news come that Sir Peter have go to

America.



"America!" I ejaculate (it be always I who act the part of chorus

and do the exclamation and questioning), and I look at Harry

Arkwright, who have communicate the news, and who hold an open letter in

his hand.



"yes, to America, to see about a railway which look very bad. he have no

end of their bond," say Harry, fold up the letter.



"when will he return?"



"he don't know. meanwhile we be to stay where we be."



Adelaide, when we speak of this circumstance, say, bitterly:



"everything be against I!"



"against you, Adelaide?" say I, look apprehensively at she.



"yes, everything!" she repeat.



she have never be effusive in her behavior to other; she be now, if

possible, still less so, but the uniform quietness and gentleness with

which she now treat all who come in contact with she, puzzle and

trouble I. what be it that prey upon her mind? in look round for

a cause my thought light first on one person, then on another; I

dismiss the idea of all, except von Francius, with a smile. shortly I

abandon that idea too. true, he be a man of very different caliber

from the other; a man, too, for whom Adelaide have conceive a decided

friendship, though in these latter day even that seem to be die

out. he do not come so often; when he do come they have little to say

to each other. perhaps, after all, the cause of her sadness lie no

deep than her every-day life, which must necessarily grow more

mournful day by day. she could feel intensely, as I have lately become

aware, and have, too, a warm, quick imagination. it might be that a

simple weariness of life and the anticipation of long year to come of

such a life lie so heavily upon her soul as to have work that gradual

change.



sometimes I be satisfied with this theory; at other it dwindle into a

miserably inadequate measure. when Adelaide once or twice kiss I,

smile at I, and call I "dear," it be on my lip to ask the

meaning of the whole thing, but it never pass they. I dared not speak

when it come to the point.



one day, about this time, I meet Anna Sartorius in one of the picture

exhibition. I would have bow and pass she, but she stop and

speak to I.



"I have not see you often lately," say she; "but I assure you, you

will hear more of I some time--and before long."



without reply, I pass on. Anna have cease even to pretend to look

friendly upon I, and I do not feel much alarm as to her power for or

against my happiness or peace of mind.



regularly, once a month, I write to Miss Hallam and occasionally have a

few line from Stella, who have become a protégée of Miss Hallam's too.

they appear to get on very well together, at which I do not wonder;

for Stella, with all her youthfulness, be of a cynical turn of mind,

which must suit Miss Hallam well.



my great friend in Elberthal be good little Dr. Mittendorf, who have

bring his wife to call upon I, and to whose house I have be invite

several time since Miss Hallam's departure.



during this time I work more steadily than ever, and with a deep

love of my art for itself. Von Francius be still my master and my

friend. I use to look back upon the day, now nearly a year ago, when I

first see he, and see he, distrust and only half like he, and

wonder at myself; for I have now as entire a confidence in he as can

by any mean be place in a man. he have thoroughly win my esteem,

respect, admiration--in a measure, too, my affection. I like the power

of he; the strong hand with which he carry thing in his own way; the

idiomatic language, and quick, curt sentence in which he enunciate his

opinion. I feel he like a strong, kind, and thoughtful eld brother,

and have have abundant evidence in his deed and in some brief

unemotional word of his that he feel a great regard of the fraternal

kind for I. it have often comfort I, that friendship--pure,

disinterested and manly on his side, grateful and unwavering on mine.



I still retain my old lodging in the Wehrhahn, and be determined to

do so. I would not be tie to remain in Sir Peter Le Marchant's house

unless I choose. Adelaide wish I to come and remain with she

altogether. she say Sir Peter wish it too; he have write and say

she might ask I. I ask what be Sir Peter's motive in wish it? be

it not a desire to humiliate both of we, and to show we that we--the

girl who have scorn he, and the woman who have sell herself to

he--be in the end dependent upon he, and must follow his will and

submit to his pleasure?



she redden, sigh, and own that it be true; nor do she press I

any far.



a month, then, elapse between the carnival in February and the next

great concert in the latter end of March. it be rather a special

concert, for von Francius have succeed, in spite of many obstacle, in

bring out the Choral Symphony.



he conduct well that night; and he, Courvoisier, Friedhelm Helfen,

Karl Linders, and one or two other, form in their white heat of

enthusiasm a leaven which leaven the whole lump. orchestra and chorus

alike do a little more than their possible, without which no great

enthusiasm can be carry out. as I watch von Francius, it seem to

I that a new soul have enter into the man. I do not believe that a

year ago he could have conduct the Choral Symphony as he do that

night. can any one enter into the broad, eternal clang of the great

"world-story" unless he have a private story of his own which may serve

he in some measure as a key to its mystery? I think not. it be a night

of triumph for Max von Francius. not only be the glorious music cheer

and applaud, he be call to receive a meed of thank for have once

more give to the world a never-die joy and beauty.



I be in the chorus. down below I see Adelaide and her devoted

attendant, Harry Arkwright. she look whiter and more subdued than

ever. all the splendor of the praise of "joy" could not bring joy to her

heart--



     "Diesen Kuss der ganzen Welt"



bring no warmth to her cheek, nor lessen the load on her breast.



the concert over, we return home. Adelaide and I retire to she

dressing-room, and she maid bring we tea. she seat herself in

silence. for my part, I be excited and hot, and feel my cheek glow.

I be so stir that I could not sit still, but move to and fro,

wish that all the world could hear that music, and repeat line

from the "Ode to Joy," the grand march-like measure, feel my heart

uplifted with the exaltation of its opening strain:



     "Freude, schöner Gotterfunken!

     Tochter aus Elysium!"



as I pace about thus excitedly, Adelaide's maid come in with a note.

Mr. Arkwright have receive it from Herr von Francius, who have desire

he to give it to Lady Le Marchant.



Adelaide open it and I go on with my chant. I know now how dreadful

it must have sound to she.



     "freude trinken alle Wesen

     an den brüsten der Natur--"



"May!" say Adelaide, faintly.



I turn in my walk and look at she. white as death, she hold the

paper toward I with a steady hand, and I, the song of joy slay upon my

lip, take it. it be a brief note from von Francius.



"I let you know, my lady, first of all that I have accept the post of

Musik-Direktor in ----. it will be make known to-morrow."



I hold the paper and look at she. now I know the reason of her pallid

look. I have indeed be blind. I might have guess well.



"have you read it?" she ask, and she stretch her arm above her

head, as if pant for breath.



"Adelaide!" I whisper, go up to she; "Adelaide--oh!"



she fall upon my neck. she do not speak, and I, speechless, hold she to

my breast.



"you love he, Adelaide?" I say, at last.



"with my whole soul!" she answer, in a low, very low, but vehement

voice. "with my whole soul."



"and you have own it to he?"



"yes."



"tell I," say I, "how it be."



"I think I have love he since almost the first time I see he--he make

quite a different impression upon I than other man do--quite. I hardly

know myself. he master I. no other man ever do--except--" she

but I like the mastery. it be delicious; it be rest and peace. it

go on for long. we know--each know quite well that we love, but he

never speak of it. he see how it be with I and he help I--oh, why

be he so good? he never try to trap I into any acknowledgment. he

never make any use of the power he know he have except to keep I right.

but at the Maskenball--I do not know how it be--we be alone in all

the crowd--there be something say--a look. it be all over. but he be

true to the last. he do not say, 'throw everything up and come to I.'

he say, 'give I the only joy that we may have. tell I you love I.'

and I tell he. I say, 'I love you with my life and my soul, and

everything I have, for ever and ever.' and that be true. he say, 'thank

you, milady. I accept the condition of my knighthood,' and kiss my

hand. there be some-one follow we. it be Sir Peter. he hear all,

and he have punish I for it since. he will punish I again."



a pause.



"that be all that have be say. he do not know that Sir Peter know,

for he have never allude to it since. he have spare I. I say he be a

noble man."



she raise herself, and look at I.



dear sister! with your love and your pride, your sin and your folly,

inexpressibly dear to I! I press a kiss upon her lip.



"Von Francius be good, Adelaide; he be good."



"Von Francius would have tell I this himself, but he have be afraid

'take it.' he have do so."



"then you have decide?" I stammer.



"to part. he have strength. so have I. it be my own fault. May--I could

bear it if it be for myself alone. I have have my eye open now. I

see that when people do wrong they drag other into it--they punish

those they love--it be part of their own punishment."



a pause. fact, I feel, be pitiless; but the glow of friendship for

von Francius be like a strong fire. in the midst of the keen pain

sharp anguish, or like the finding a strong comrade in a battle.



Adelaide have be very self-restrained and quiet all this time, but now

"oh, I love he, I love he! it be dreadful! how shall I go through with

it?"



ay, there be the rub! not one short, sharp pang, and over--all fire

quench in cool mist of death and unconsciousness, but long year to

come of daily, hourly, pay the price; incessant compunction, active

punishment. a prospect for a martyr to shirk from, and for a woman who

have make a mistake to--live through.



we need not further word. the secret be tell, and the worst know.

we part. Von Francius be from this moment a sacred be to I.

my lesson. I go to my lodging and have they there. Adelaide say

nothing, ask not a question concern he, nor mention his name,

and the silence on his side be almost as profound as that on her. it

seem as if they fear that should they meet, speak, look each other

in the eye, all resolution would be sweep away, and the end hurry

resistless on.









CHAPTER XXXI.



"and behold, though the way be light and the sun do shine, yet my

hath tell I--thus--or thus.'"





Karl Linders, our old acquaintance, be now our fast friend. many

[footnote d: the german custom on an engagement taking place be to

announce it with the above word, signify "M. and N. announce

(recommend) themselves as betroth." this appear in the newspaper--as

a marriage with we.]

CHAPTER XXXII.



 "didst thou, or didst thou not? just tell I, friend!

 not that _my_ conscience may be satisfied,

 _I_ never for a moment doubted thee--

 but that I may have wherewithal in hand

 to turn against they when they point at thee:

 a whip to flog they with--a rock to crush--

 Thy word--thy simple downright 'no, I do not.'



   *       *       *       *       *



   why! how!

 what's this? he do not, will not speak. oh, God!

 Nay, raise thy head and look I in the eye!

 Canst not? what be this thing?"





it be the last concert of the season, and the end of April, when

evening be grow pleasantly long and the air balmy. those last

concert, and the last night of the opera, which close at the end of

April, until September, be always crowd. that night I remember we

the concert be for the benefit of von Francius, and be probably the

last one at which he would conduct we. he be leave to assume the post

of Königlicher Musik-Direktor at ----. now that the time come there be

not a man among we who be not heartily sorry to think of the parting.



Miss Wedderburn be one of the soloist that evening and her sister and

Karl Linders come on late. I see that just before he appear by the

the third piece on the list be the Violin Concerto, and when its turn

"_Meine Herrschaften_, I be sorry to say that I have receive a telegram

I can not regret that you should have an opportunity of listen to one

who will be a very effectual substitute--Herr Concertmeister

he step back. courvoisier rise. there be a dead silence in the hall.

Eugen stand in the well-know position of the prophet without honor,

"I be sorry to say that I dare not venture upon the great Concerto; it

be so long since I attempt it. I shall have pleasure in try to play

beethoven the dead and undisputed.



it be a minor measure, and one unknown to the public, for it have not

yet be publish. Von Francius have lend eugen the score a few day

and so, indeed, he prove to we that night. never, before or since, from

the work be in itself a fine one; original, strong, terse and racy,

and this, interpret by Eugen, whose mood and whose inner life it

to I the scene and the sound come freshly as if hear yesterday. I see

eye and see the light in they, and see von Francius shade his face with

then I see how it be all over, and Eugen, pale with the depth of

emotion with which he have play the passionate music, retire, and

there come a burst of enthusiastic applause--applause renew again and

again--it be a veritable _succès fou_.



but he would make no response to the plaudit. he remain obstinately

seat, and there be no elation, but rather gloom upon his face. in
