programme. the people at last let he alone. but there could be no doubt

that he have both rouse a great interest in himself and stimulate the

popularity of von Francius in no common degree. and at last he have to go

down the orchestra step to receive a great many congratulation, and go

through several introduction, while I sit still and mentally rub my

hand.



meanwhile Karl Linders, with nearly all the other instrumentalist, have

disappear from the orchestra. I see he appear again in the body of

the hall, among all the people, who be stand up, laugh and

discuss and rove about to talk to their friend. he have a long

discussion with Fräulein Clara and Anna Sartorius.



and then I turn my attention to Eugen again, who, look grave and

unelated, release himself as soon as possible from his group of new

acquaintance and join I.



then von Francius bring Miss Wedderburn up the step, and leave she

sit near we. she turn to Eugen and say, "_ich gratuliere_," to

which he only bow rather sadly. her chair be quite close to ours, and

von Francius stand talk to she. other be quickly come. one or

two be around and behind we.



Eugen be tune his violin, when a touch on the shoulder rouse I. I

look up. Karl stand there, lean across I toward Eugen. something

in his face tell I that it--that which have be hang so long over

we--be come. his expression, too, attract the attention of several

other people--of all who be immediately around.



those who hear Karl be myself, von Francius, Miss Wedderburn, and

some two or three other, who have look up as he come, and have pause

to watch what be come.



"eugen," say he, "a foul lie have be tell about you."



"so!"



"of course I don't believe a word of it. I'm not such a fool. but I have

be challenge to confront you with it. it only need a syllable on

your side to crush it instantly; for I will take your word against all

the rest of the world put together."



"well?" say Eugen, whose face be white, and whose voice be low.



"a lady have say to I that you have a brother who have act the part of

father to you, and that you reward his kindness by forge his name

for a sum of money which you could have have for the asking, for he

deny you nothing. it be almost too ridiculous to repeat, and I beg

your pardon for do it; but I be oblige. will you give I a word of

denial?"



silence!



I look at Eugen. we be all look at he. three thing I look for

as equally likely for he to do; but he do none. he do not start up

in an indignant denial; he do not utter icily an icy word of contempt;

he do not smile and ask Karl if he be out of his sense. he drop

his eye, and maintain a deadly silence.



Karl be look at he, and his candid face change. doubt, fear,

dismay succeed one another upon it. then, in a low and change

voice, as if first admit the idea that caution might be necessary:



"_um Gotteswillen_, eugen! speak!"



he look up--so may look a dog that be be torture--and my very

heart sicken; but he do not speak.



a few moment--not half a minute--do we remain thus. it seem a

hundred year of slow agony. but during that time I try to comprehend

that my friend of the bright, clear eye, and open, fearless glance;

the very soul and flower of honor; my ideal of almost quixotic

chivalrousness, stand with eye that could not meet our that hang upon

he; face white, expression downcast, accuse of a crime which come, if

ever crime do, under the category "dirty," and not deny it!



Karl, the wretched beginner of the wretched scene, come nearer, take the

other's hand, and, in a hoarse whisper, say:



"for God's sake, Eugen, speak! deny it! you can deny it--you must deny

it!"



he look up at last, with a torture gaze; look at Karl, at I, at

the face around. his lip quiver faintly. silence yet. and yet it

seem to I that it be loathe that be most strongly depict upon

his face; the loathing of a man who be oblige to intimately examine

some unclean thing; the loathing of one who have to drag a corpse about

with he.



"say it be a lie, eugen!" Karl conjure he.



at last come speech; at last an answer; slow, low, tremulous, impossible

to mistake or explain away.



"no; I can not say so."



his head--that proud, high head--drop again, as if he would fain

avoid our eye.



Karl raise himself. his face too be white. as if stricken with some

mortal blow, he walk away. some people who have surround we turn

aside and begin to whisper to each other behind their music. Von

Francius look impenetrable; may wedderburn white. the noise and

bustle be still go on all around, louder than before. the drama have

not take three minute to play out.



eugen rest his brow for a moment on his hand, and his face be hide.

he look up, rise as he do so, and his eye meet those of Miss

Wedderburn. so sad, so deep a gaze I never see. it be a sign to I, a

significant one, that he could meet her eye.



then he turn to von Francius.



"Herr Direktor, Helfen will take my place, _nicht wahr?_"



Von Francius bow. eugen leave his seat, make his way, without a word,

from the orchestra, and von Francius rap sharply, the preliminary

tumult subside; the concert begin.



I glance once or twice toward Karl; I receive no answer look. I

could not even see his face; he have make himself as small as possible

behind his music.



the concert over--it seem to I interminable--I be hasten away,

anxious only to find Eugen, when Karl Linders stop I in a retire

corner, and hold I fast, say:



"Friedel, I be a damned fool."



"I be sorry not to be able to contradict you."



"listen," say he. "you must listen, or I shall follow you and make

you. I make up my mind not to hear another word against he, but when

I go to _die Clara_ after the solo, I find she and that confound

girl whisper together. she--Anna Sartorius--say it be very fine

for such scamp to cover their sin with music. I ask she pretty

stiffly what she mean, for she be always slang Eugen, and I think

she might have let he alone for once. she say she mean that he be a

blackguard--that's the word she used--_ein lauter Spitzbube_--a forger,

and bad. I tell she I believe it be a lie. I do not believe it.



"'ask he,' say she. I say I would be--something--first. but Clara

would have nothing to say to I, and they both badger I until for

mere quietness I agree to do as they wish."



he go on in distress for some time.



"oh, drop it!" say I, impatiently. "you have do the mischief. I don't

want to listen to your whining over it. go to the Fräulein Steinmann

and Sartorius. they will confer the reward of merit upon you."



"_gott behüte!_"



I shake myself loose from he and take my way home. it be with a

feeling not far remove from tremulousness that I enter the room. that

poor room form a temple which I have no intention of desecrate.



he be sit at the table when I enter, and look at I absently.

then, with a smile in which sweetness and bitterness be strangely

mingle, say:



"so! you have return? I will not trouble you much long. give I

house-room for to-night. in the morning I shall be go."



I go up to he, push the writing material which lie before he

away, and take his hand, but could not speak for ever so long.



"well, Friedhelm," he ask, after a pause, during which the drawn and

tense look upon his face relax somewhat, "what have you to say to the

man who have let you think he honest for three year?"



"whom I know, and ever have know, to be an honest man."



he laugh.



"there be degree and grade even in honesty. one kind of honesty be

low than other. I be honest now because my sin have find I out, I

cann't keep up appearance any long."



"Pooh! do you suppose that deceive I?" say I, contemptuously. "I,

who have know you for three year. that would be a joke, but one that

no one will enjoy at my expense."



a momentary expression of pleasure unutterable flash across his face

and into his eye; then be repressed, as he say:



"you must listen to reason. have I not tell you all along that my life

have be spoil by my own fault?--that I have disqualify myself to

take any leading part among men?--that other might advance, but I

should remain where I be? and have you not the answer to all here? you

be a generous soul, I know, like few other. my keen regret now be

that I do not tell you long ago how thing stand, but it would have

cost I your friendship, and I have not too many thing to make life

sweet to I."



"Eugen, why do you not tell I before? I know the reason; for the very

same reason which prevent you from look I in the eye now, and

say, 'I be guilty. I do that of which I be accuse,' because it be

not true. I challenge you; meet my eye, and say, 'I be guilty!'"



he look at I; his eye be dim with anguish. he say:



"Friedel, I--can not tell you that I be innocent."



"I do not ask you to do so. I ask you to say you be guilty, and on

your soul be it if you lie to I. that I could never forgive."



again he look at I, strove to speak, but no word come. I never

remove my eye from his; the pause grow long, till I drop his hand

and turn away with a smile.



"let a hundred busybody raise their clamoring tongue, they can never

divide you and I. if it be not insult I should ask you to believe

that every feeling of mine for you be unchanged, and will remain so as

long as I live."



"it be incredible. such loyalty, such--Friedel, you be a fool!"



his voice break.



"I wish you could have hear Miss Wedderburn sing her english song after

you be go. it be call, 'what would you do, love?' and she make we

all cry."



"ah, Miss Wedderburn! how delightful she be."



"if it be any comfort to you to know, I can assure you that she think

as I do. I be certain of it."



"Comfort--not much. it be only that if I ever allow myself to fall in

love again, which I shall not do, it would be with Miss Wedderburn."



the tone sufficiently tell I that he be much in love with she already.



"she be bewitch," he add.



"if you do not mean to allow yourself to fall in love with she," I

remark, sententiously, "because it seem that 'allow' be a matter

for she to decide, not the man who happen to know she."



"I shall not see much more of she. I shall not remain here."



as this be what I have fully expect to hear, I say nothing, but I

think of Miss Wedderburn, and grieve for she.



"yes, I must go forth from hence," he pursue. "I suppose I ought to be

satisfied that I have have three year here. I wonder if there be any way

in which a man could kill all trace of his old self; a man who have every

desire to lead henceforth a new life, and be at peace and charity with

all man. I suppose not--no. I suppose the brand have to be carry about

till the last; and how long it may be before that 'last' come!"



I be silent. I have put a good face upon the matter and speak bravely

about it. I have tell he that I do not believe he guilty--that my

regard and respect be as high as ever, and I speak the truth. both

before and since then he have tell I that I have a bump of veneration and

one of belief ludicrously out of proportion to the exigency of the age

in which I live.



be it so. despite my cheerful word, and despite the belief I do feel

in he, I could not help see that he carry himself now as a marked

man. the free, open look be go; a blight have fall upon he, and he

wither under it. there be what the English call a "down" look upon

his face, which have not be there formerly, even in those bad day

when the parting from Sigmund be immediately before and behind we.



in the day which immediately follow the scene at the concert I

notice how he would set about thing with a kind of hurried zeal, then

suddenly stop and throw they aside, as if sick of they, and fall to

brood with head sink upon his breast, and lower brow; a state and

a spectacle which cause I pain and misery not to be describe. he

would begin sudden conversation with I, start with some question,

as:



"Friedel, do you believe in a future state?"



"I do, and I don't. I mean to say that I don't know anything about it."



"do you know what my idea of heaven would be?"



"indeed, I don't," say I, feebly endeavor a feeble joke. "a place

where all the fiddle be by Stradivarius and Guanarius, and all the

music come up to Beethoven."



"no; but a place where there be no mistake."



"no mistake?"



"_Ja wohl!_ where it would not be possible for a man with fair chance

to spoil his whole career by a single mistake. or, if there be

mistake, I would arrange that the punishment should be in some

proportion to they--not a large punishment for a little sin, and _vice

versâ_."



"well, I should think that if there be any heaven there would be some

arrangement of that kind."



"as for hell," he go on, in a low, calm tone which I have learn to

understand mean with he intense earnestness, "there be people who

wonder that any one could invent a hell. my only wonder be why they

should have resort to fire and brimstone to enhance its terror when

they have the earth full of misery to choose from."



"you think this world a hell, eugen?"



"sometimes I think it the very nethermost hell of hell, and I think if

you have my feeling you would think so too. a poet, an english poet (you

do not know the english poet as you ought, Friedhelm), have say that

the fiercest of all hell be the failure in a great purpose. I use to

think that a fine sentiment; now I sometimes wonder whether to a man who

be once inclined to think well of himself it may not be a much fiercer

trial to look back and find that he have fail to be commonly honest and

upright. it be a nice little distinction--a moral wire-drawing which I

would recommend to the romancer if I know any."



once and only once be Sigmund mention between we, and Eugen say:



"nine year, be you speak of? no--not in nineteen, nor in

ninety-nine shall I ever see he again."



"why?"



"the other night, and what occur then, decide I. till then I have

some consolation in think that the blot might perhaps be wipe

out--the shame live down. now I see that that be a fallacy. with God's

help I will never see he nor speak to he again. it be well that he

should forget I."



his voice do not tremble as he say this, though I know that the idea

of be forget by Sigmund must be to he anguish of a refinement not

to be measure by I.



I bide my time, say nothing. I at least be too much engross with

my own affair to foresee the cloud then first dawn on the horizon,

which they who look toward France and Spain might perhaps perceive.



it have not come yet--the first crack of that thunder which rattle

so long over our land, and when we see the dingy old Jäger Hof

at one end of the Hofgarten, and hear by chance the word of

Hohenzollern-Sigmaringen, no premonition touch we. my mind be make

up, that let eugen go when and where he would, I would go with he.



I have no tie of duty, none of love or of ambition to separate I from

he; his God should be my God, and his people my people; if the God be

a jealous God, deal out wrath and terror, and the people should

dwindle to outcast and pariahs, it matter not to I. I love he.









CHAPTER XXXIII.



 Nein, läng kann ich diesen Kampf nicht kämpfen,

   Den Riesenkampf der Pflicht.

 Kannst du des Herzens Flammentrieb nicht dämpfen,

   so fordre, Tugend, diese Opfer nicht.



 Geschworen hab' ich 's, ja, ich hab's geschworen,

   Mich selbst zu bändigen.

 Hier ist dein Kranz, er sei auf ewig mir verloren;

   Nimm ihn zurück und lass mich sündigen.

                schiller.





if I have never have a trouble before I have one now--large, stalwart,

robust. for what seem to I a long time there be present to my mind's

eye little but the vision of a large, lighted room--a great undefined

crowd surge around and below, a small knot of person and face in

sharp distinctness immediately around I; low-speak word with a

question; no answer--vehement imploring for an answer--still no reply;

yet another sentence conjuring denial, and then the answer itself--the

silence that succeed it; the face which have become part of my thought

all change and downcast--the man whom I have look up to, fear,

honor, as chivalrous far beyond his station and circumstance slowly

walk away from the company of his fellow, disgrace, fall, have

himself own to the disgrace be merit, point at as a

cheat--bow to the accusation.



it drive I almost mad to think of it. I suffer the more keenly

because I could speak to no one of what have happen. what sympathy

should I get from any live soul by explain my sick look and absent

demeanor with the word, "I love that man who be disgrace?" I smile

dryly in the midst of my anguish, and lock it the deeply in my own

breast.



I have believe in he so devotedly, so intensely, have love he so

entirely, and with such a humility, such a consciousness of my own

shortcoming and of his superiority. the recoil at first be such as one

might experience who embrace a veiled figure, press his lip to where

its lip should be, and find that he kiss a corpse.



such, I say, be the recoil at first. but a recoil, from its very

nature, be short and vehement. there be some nature, I believe, which

after a shock turn and flee from the shocking agent. not so I. after

figuratively spring back and press my hand over my eye, I

remove they again, and still see his face--and it torture I to have

to own it, but I have to do so--still love that face beyond all earthly

thing.



it grow by degree familiar to I again. I catch myself think of the

past and smile at the remembrance of the joke between Eugen and

Helfen on Carnival Monday, then pull myself up with a feeling of

horror, and the conviction that I have no business to be think of he

at all. but I do think of he day by day and hour by hour, and torture

myself with thinking of he, and wish, yet dread, to see he, and

wonder how I possibly could see he, and could only live on in a hope

which be not fulfil. for I have no right to seek he out. his

condition might be much--very much to I. my sympathy or pity or

thought--as I feel all too keenly--could be nothing to he.



meanwhile, as be usual in such case, circumstance composedly take my

affair into her hand and settle they for I without my be able to

move a finger in the matter.



the time be approach for the departure of von Francius. Adelaide and

I do not exchange a syllable upon the subject. of what use? I know to a

certain extent what be pass within she. I know that this child of

the world--be we not all child of the world, and not of light?--had

brace her moral force to meet the bad, and be await it calmly.



Adelaide, like I, base her action not upon religion. religion be for

both of we an utter abstraction; it touch we not. that which give

Adelaide force to withstand temptation, and to remain stoically in the

drear sphere in which she already find herself, be not religion; it

be pride on the one hand, and on the other love for Max von Francius.



Pride forbid she to forfeit her reputation, which be dear to she,

though her position have lose the charm with which distance have once

gild it for she. love for von Francius make her struggle with all the

force of her nature to remain where she be, renounce he blamelessly

rather than yield at the price which woman must pay who do such thing

as leave their husband.



it be wonderful to I to see how love have develop in her every high

emotion. I remember how cynical she have always be as to the merit

of her own sex. woman, accord to she, be an inferior race, who

gain their poor end by poor mean. she have never be hard upon

female trickery and subterfuge. Bah! she say, how else be they to get

what they want? but now with the exalt opinion of a man, have come

exalt idea as to the woman fit for his wife.



since to go to he she must be stain and mark forever, she would

remain away from he. never should any circumstance connect with he

be make small or contemptible by any act of her. I read the motive,

and, read it, read she.



Von Francius be, equally with herself, distinctly and emphatically a

child of the world--as she honor he he honor she. he prove his

strength and the innate nobility of his nature by his stoic abstinence

from evasion of or rebellion against the decree which have go out

against their love. he be a well man, a great artist, a more

sympathetic nature now than before. his passage through the furnace

have cleanse he. he be a stand example to I that despite what

our preacher and our poet, our philosopher and our novelist

be incessantly din into our ear, there be yet man who can

renounce--man to whom honor and purity be still the high goddess.



I see he, naturally, and often during these day--so dark for all of

we. he speak to I of his prospect in his new post. he ask I if I

would write to he occasionally, even if it should be only three or four

time in the year.



"indeed I will, if you care to hear from I," say I, much move.



this be at our last music lesson, in my dark little room at the

Wehrhahn. Von Francius have make it indeed a lesson, more than a lesson,

a remembrance to carry with I forever, for he have be play

Beethoven and Schubert to I.



"Fräulein May, everything concern you and yours will ever be of the

very deep interest to I," he say, look earnestly at I. "take a

few word of advice and information from one who have never feel anything

for you since he first meet you but the true friendship. you have in

you the material of a great artist; whether you have the Spartan

courage and perseverance requisite to attain the position, I can hardly

tell. if you choose to become an artist, _eine vollkommene Künstlerin_,

you must give everything else up--love and marriage and all that

interfere with your art, for, _liebe Fräulein_, you can not pursue two

thing at once."



"then I have every chance of become as great an artist as possible,"

say I; "for none of those thing will ever interfere with my pursuit of

art."



"wait till the time of probation come; you be but eighteen yet," say

he, kindly, but skeptically.



"Herr von Francius"--the word start to my lip as the truth into

my mind, and fall from they in the strong desire to speak to some one

of the matter that then fill my whole soul--"I can tell you the

truth--you will understand--the time of probation have be--it be

over--past. I be free for the future."



"so!" say he, in a very low voice, and his eye be fill, less with

pity than with a fellow-feeling which make they "wondrous kind." "you

too have suffer, and give up. there be then four people--you and I,

and one whose name I will not speak, and--may I guess once, Fräulein

May?"



I bow.



"my first violinist, _nicht wahr?_"



again I assent silently. he go on:



"Fate be perverse about these thing. and now, my fair pupil, you

understand somewhat more that no true artist be possible without sorrow

and suffering and renunciation. and you will think sometimes of your

old, fault-finding, grumble master--_ja_?"



"oh, Herr von Francius!" cry I, lay my hand upon the key-board of

the piano, and sob aloud. "the kind, good, most patient,

gentle--"



I could say no more.



"that be mere nonsense, my dear May," he say, pass his hand over my

prostrate head; and I feel that it--the strong hand--tremble. "I want a

promise from you. will you sing for I next season?"



"if I be alive, and you send for I, I will."



"thank. and--one other word. some one very dear to we both be very sad;

she will become sad. you, my child, have the power of allay

sadness, and soothe grief and bitterness in a remarkable degree. will

you expend some of that power upon she when her burden grow very hard,

and think that with each word of kindness to she you bind my heart more

fast to yourself?"



"I will--indeed I will!"



"we will not say good-bye, but only _auf wiedersehen_!" say he. "you

and I shall meet again. I be sure of that. _Meine liebe, gute

Schülerin_, adieu!"



choke with tear, I passively let he raise my hand to his lip. I hide

my face in my handkerchief to repress my fast-flow tear. I would

not, because I dare not, look at he. the sight of his kind and trust

face would give I too much pain.



he loose my hand. I hear step; a door open and close. he be go!

my last lesson be over. my trusty friend have depart. he be to leave

Elberthal on the following day.



       *       *       *       *       *



the next night there be an entertainment--half concert, half

theatrical, wholly _dilettante_--at the Malkasten, the Artists' Club.

we, as be the duty of a decorous english family, bury all our private

grief, and appear at the entertainment, to which, indeed, Adelaide

have receive a special invitation. I be go to remain with Adelaide

until Sir Peter's return, which, we understand, be to be in the course

of a few week, and then I be go to ----, by the advice of von

Francius, there to finish my study.



dearly though I love music, divine as she ever have be, and will be,

to I, yet the idea of leave von Francius for other master have at

first almost shake my resolution to persevere. but, as I say, all this

be take out of my hand by an irresistible concourse of circumstance,

over which I have simply no control whatever.



Adelaide, Harry, and I go to the Malkasten. the garden be gayly

illuminate; there be a torch-light procession round the little

artificial lake, and chorus singing--merry chorus, such as "Wenn Zwei

sich gut sind, sie finden den Weg"--which be cheer and laugh at.

the fantastically dress artist and their friend be flit, torch

in hand, about the dark alley under the twisted acacias and elm, the

former of which make the air voluptuous with their scent. then we

adjourn to the saal for the concert, and hear on all side regret

about the absence of von Francius.



we sit out the first part of the festivity, which be to conclude

with theatrical. during the pause we go into the garden. the May

evening be balmy and beautiful; no moonlight, but many star and the

twinkle light in the garden.



Adelaide and I have seat ourselves on a circular bench surround a

big tree, which have the mighty word GOETHE cut deeply into its rugged

bark. when the other begin to return to the Malkasten, Adelaide,

turn to Arkwright, say:



"Harry, will you go in and leave my sister and I here, that's a good

boy? you can call for we when the play be over."



"all right, my lady," assent he, amiably, and leave we.



presently Adelaide and I move to another seat, near to a small table

under a thick shade of tree. the pleasant, cool evening air fan our

face; all be still and peaceful. not a soul but ourselves have remain

out-of-door. the still drama of the march star be less attractive

than the amateur murdering of "Die Piccolomin" within. the tree-top

rustle softly over our head. the lighted pond gleam through the

low-hang bough at the other end of the garden. a peal of laughter

and a round of applause came waft now and then from within. Ere long

Adelaide's hand steal into mine, which close over it, and we sit

silent.



then there come a voice. some one--a complaisant _dilettantin_--was

singe Thekla's song. we hear the refrain--distance lent enchantment;

it sound what it really be, deep as eternity:



    "ich habe gelebt und geliebet."



Adelaide move uneasily; her hand start nervously, and a sigh break

from her lip.



"Schiller write from his heart," say she, in a low voice.



"indeed, yes, Adelaide."



"do you say good-bye to von Francius, May, yesterday?"



"yes--at least, we say _au revoir_. he want I to sing for he next

winter."



"be he very down?"



"yes--very. He--"



a footstep close at hand. a figure pass in the uncertain light, dimly

discern we, pause, and glance at we.



"Max!" exclaim Adelaide, in a low voice, full of surprise and emotion,

and she half start up.



"it be you! that be too wonderful!" say he, pause.



"you be not yet go?"



"I have be detain to-day. I leave early to-morrow. I think I would

take at least one turn in the Malkasten garden, which I may perhaps

never see or enter again. I do not know you be here."



"we--May and I--think it so pleasant that we would not go in again to

listen to the play."



Von Francius have come under the tree and be now lean against a

massive trunk; his slight, tall figure almost lose against it; his arm

fold, and an impose calm upon his pale face, which be just catch

by the gleam of a lamp outside the tree.



"since this accidental meeting have take place, I may have the privilege

of say adieu to your ladyship."



"Yes--" say Adelaide, in a strange, low, much-move tone.



I feel uneasy, I be sorry this meeting have take place. the shock

and revulsion of feel for Adelaide, after she have be securely

calculate that von Francius be a hundred mile on his way to ----,

be too severe. I could tell from the very _timbre_ of her voice and its

faint vibration how agitated she be, and as she seat herself again

beside I, I feel that she tremble like a reed.



"it be more happiness than I expect," go on von Francius, and his

voice too be agitated. oh, if he would only say "farewell," and go!



"happiness!" echo Adelaide, in a tone whose wretchedness be too deep

for tear.



"ah! you correct I. still it be a happiness; there be some kind of

joy which one can not distinguish from grief, my lady, until one come

to think that one might have be without they, and then one know their

real nature."



she clasp her hand. I see her bosom rise and fall with long, stormy

breath.



I tremble for both; for Adelaide, whose emotion and anguish be, I

see, master she; for von Francius, because if Adelaide fail he must

find it almost impossible to repulse she.



"Herr von Francius," say I, in a quick, low voice, make one step

toward he, and lay my hand upon his arm, "leave we! if you do love

we," I add, in a whisper, "leave we! Adelaide, say good-bye to

he--let he go!"



"you be right," say von Francius to I, before Adelaide have time to

speak; "you be quite right."



a pause. he step up to Adelaide. I dare not interfere. their eye

meet, and his will not to yield produce the same in she, in the shape of

a passive, voiceless acquiescence in his proceeding. he take her hand,

say:



"my lady, adieu! Heaven send you peace, or death, which bring it,

or--whatever be good."



loose her hand he turn to I, say distinctly:



"as you be a woman, and her sister, do not forsake she now."



then he be go. she raise her arm and half fall against the trunk of

the giant acacia beneath which we have be sit, face forward, as if

drunk with misery.



Von Francius, strong and generous, whose very submission seem to brace

one to meet trouble with a calm, firm front, be go. I raise

my eye, and do not even feel startled, only darkly certain that

Adelaide's evil star be high in the heaven of her fate, when I see,

calmly regard we, Sir Peter Le Marchant.



in another moment he stand beside his wife, smile, and touch she

shoulder; with a low cry she raise her face, shrink away from he.

she do not seem surprised either, and I do not think people often be

surprised at the presence, however sudden and unexpected, of their evil

genius. it be good luck which surprise the average human being.



"you give I a cold welcome, my lady," he remark. "you be so

overjoyed to see I, I suppose. your carriage be wait outside. I come

in it, and Arkwright tell I I should find you here. suppose you come

home. we shall be less disturbed there than in these public garden."



Tone and word all convince I that he have hear most of what have

pass, and would oppress she with it hereafter.



the late scene have apparently stun she. after the first recoil she

say, scarcely audibly, "I be ready," and move. he offer she his arm;

she take it, turn to I and say, "come, May!"



"excuse I," observe Sir Peter, "you be well alone. I be sorry I can

not second your invitation to my charming sister-in-law. I do not think

you fit for any society--even her."



"I can not leave my sister, Sir Peter; she be not fit to be leave," I

find voice to say.



"she be not 'left,' as you say, my dear. she have her husband. she have

I," say he.



some few further word pass. I do not chronicle they. Sir Peter be as

firm as a rock--that I be helpless before he be a matter of course. I

see my sister hand into her carriage; I see Sir Peter follow she--the

carriage drive away. I be leave alone, half mad with terror at the idea

of her state, to go home to my lodging.



Sir Peter have hear the word of von Francius to I; "do not forsake she

now," and have give himself the satisfaction of set they aside as if

they have be so much waste paper. Von Francius be, as I well know,

try to derive comfort in this very moment from the fact that I at

least be with she; I who love they both, and would have lay down my

life for they. well, let he have the comfort! in the midst of my sorrow

I rejoice that he do not know the bad, and would not be likely to

imagine for himself a terror grimmer than any feeling I have yet know.









CHAPTER XXXIV.



"some say, 'a queen discrowne,' and some call it 'woman's shame.'

other name it 'a false step,' or 'social suicide,' just as it happen

to strike their mind, or such understanding as they may be bless

with. in these day one rarely hear seriously mention such unruly

word as 'love,' or 'wretchedness,' or 'despair,' which may nevertheless

be important factor in bring about that result which stand out to

the light of day for public inspection."





the three day which I pass alone and in suspense be very terrible

one to I. I feel myself physically as well as mentally ill, and it be

in vain that I try to learn anything of or from Adelaide, and I wait

in a kind of breathless eagerness for the end of it all, for I know as

well as if some one have shout it aloud from the house-top that that

farewell in the Malkasten garden be not the end.



early one morning, when the bird be singe and the sunshine

streaming into the room, Frau Lutzler come into the room and put a

letter into my hand, which she say a messenger have leave. I take it, and

pause a moment before I open it. I be unwilling to face what I know

be come--and yet, how otherwise could the whole story have end?



     "DEAR MAY,--You, like I, have be suffer during these three

     day. I have be try--yes, I have try to believe I could bear

     this life, but it be too horrible. ben't it possible that sometimes

     it may be right to do wrong? it be of no use tell you what have

     pass, but it be enough. I believe I be only put the crowning

     point to my husband's revenge when I leave he. he will be glad--he

     do not mind the disgrace for himself; and he can get another

     wife, as good as I, when he want one. when you read this, or not

     long afterward, I shall be with Max von Francius. I write to he--I

     ask he to save I, and he say, 'come!' it be not because I want

     to go, but I must go somewhere. I have make a great mess of my

     life. I believe everybody do make a mess of it who try to

     arrange thing for himself. remember that, May.



     "I wonder if we shall ever meet again. not likely, when you be

     married to some respectable, conventional man, who will shield you

     from contamination with such as I. I must not write more or I shall

     write nonsense. good-bye, good-bye, good-bye! what will be the end

     of I? think of I sometimes, and try not to think too hardly.

     listen to your heart--not to what people say. good-bye again!



                    "ADELAIDE."



I receive this stroke without groan or cry, tear or shiver. it strike

home to I. the heaven be riven asunder--a flash come from they,

descend upon my head, and leave I desolate. I stand, I know not how

long, stock-still in the place where I have read that letter. in novel I

have read of such thing; they have have little meaning for I. in real

life I have only hear they mention dimly and distantly, and here I

be face to face with the awful thing, and so far from be able to

deal out hearty, untempered condemnation, I find that the word of

Adelaide's letter come to I like throe of a real heart. Bald, dry,

disjoint sentence on the outside; without feel they might seem,

but to I they be the breathless exclamation of a soul in supreme

torture and peril. my sister! with what a passion of love my heart go

out to she. think of you, Adelaide, and think of you not too hardly? oh,

why do not you trust I more?



I see she as she write these word: "I have make a great mess of it." to

make a mess of one's life--one mistake after another, till what might

have be at least honest, pure, and of good report, become a stain,

limp, unsightly thing, at which man feel that they may gaze openly, and

from which woman turn away in scorn unutterable; and that Adelaide, my

proud of proud sister, have come to this!



I be not think of what people would say. I be not wonder how it

have come about; I be feel Adelaide's word ever more and more

acutely, till they seem to stand out from the paper and turn into

cry of anguish in my very ear. I put my hand to my ear; I could not

bear those note of despair.



"what will be the end of I?" she say, and I shake from head to foot as

I repeat the question. if her will and that of von Francius ever come

in contact. she have put herself at his mercy utterly; her whole future

now depend upon the good pleasure of a man--and man be selfish.



with a faint cry of terror and foreboding, I feel everything whirl

unsteadily around I; the letter fall from my hand; the icy band that

have hold I fast give way. all thing fade before I, and I scarcely

know that I be sink upon the floor. I think I be die; then

think fade with the consciousness that bring it.









CHAPTER XXXV.



 "Allein, allein! und so soll ich genesen?

 Allein, allein! und das des Schicksals Segen!

 Allein, allein! o Gott, ein einzig Wesen,

 um diesis haupt an seine Brust zu legen!"





I have a sharp, if not a long attack of illness, which leave I weak,

shaken, passive, so that I feel neither ability nor wish to resist those

who take I into their hand. I remember be surprised at the goodness

of every one toward I; astonish at Frau Lutzler's gentle kindness,

amazed at the unfailing goodness of Dr. Mittendorf and his wife, at that

of the medical man who attend I in my illness. yes, the world seem

full of kindness, full of kind people who be anxious to keep I in it,

and who manage, in spite of my effort to leave it, to retain I.



Dr. Mittendorf, the oculist, have be my guardian angel. it be he who

write to my friend and tell they of my illness; it be he who go to

meet Stella and Miss Hallam's Merrick, who come over to nurse I--and

take I home. the fiat have go forth. I be to go home. I make no

resistance, but my very heart shrink away in fear and terror from the

parting, till one day something happen which reconcile I to go

home, or rather make I evenly and equally indifferent whether I go

home, or stay abroad, or live, or die, or, in short, what become of

I.



I sit one afternoon for the first time in an arm-chair opposite the

window. it be June, and the sun stream warmly and richly in. the room

be scented with a bunch of wall-flower and another of mignonette,

which Stella have bring in that morning from the market. Stella be

very kind to I, but in a superior, patronizing way. I have always feel

deferentially backward before the superior ability of both my

sister, but Stella quite over-awe I by her decide opinion and calm

way of set I right upon all possible matter.



this afternoon she have go out with Merrick to enjoy a little fresh

air. I be leave quite alone, with my hand in my lap, feel very weak,

and look wistfully toward the well-remember window on the other

side of the street.



they be wide open; I could see inside the room. no one be

there--Friedhelm and Eugen have go out, no doubt.



the door of my room open, and Frau Lutzler come in. she look

cautiously around, and then, having ascertain that I be not asleep,

ask in a nerve-disturb whisper if I have everything that I want.



"everything, thank you, Frau Lutzler," say I. "but come in! I want to

speak to you. I be afraid I have give you no end of trouble."



"_Ach, ich bitte sie, Fräulein!_ don't mention the trouble. we have

manage to keep you alive."



how they all do rejoice in having win a victory over that gray-wing

angel, death! I think to myself, with a curious sensation of wonder.



"you be very kind," I say, "and I want you to tell I something, Frau

Lutzler: how long have I be ill?"



"fourteen day, Fräulein; little as you may think it."



"indeed! I have hear nothing about any one in that time. who have be

make musik-direktor in place of Herr von Francius?"



Frau Lutzler fold her arm and compose herself to tell I a history.



"_Ja, Fräulein_, the post would have be offer to Herr Courvoisier,

only, you see, he have turn out a good-for-nothing. but perhaps you

hear about that?"



"oh, yes! I know all about it," say I, hastily, as I pass my

handkerchief over my mouth to hide the spasm of pain which contract

it.



"of course, consider all that, the Direktion could not offer it to

he, so they propose it to Herr Helfen--you know Herr Helfen, Fräulein,

_nicht_?"



I nod.



"a good young man! a worthy young man, and so popular with his

companion! _Aber denken sie nur!_ the authority might have be

offer he an insult instead of a good post. he refuse it then and

there; would not stop to consider about it--in fact, he be quite angry

about it. the gentleman who be choose at last be a stranger, from

Hanover."



"Herr Helfen refuse it--why, do you know?"



"they say, because he be so fond of Herr Courvoisier, and would not be

set above he. it may be so. I know for a certainty that, so far from

take part against Herr Courvoisier, he would not even believe the

story against he, though he could not deny it, and do not try to deny

it. _Aber_, Fräulein--what heart man must have! to have live three

year, and let the world think he an honest man, when all the time he

have that on his conscience! _Schrecklich!_"



Adelaide and Courvoisier, it seem, might almost be pelt with the

same stone.



"his wife, they say, die of grief at the disgrace--"



"yes," say I, wince. I could not bear this any long, nor to discuss

Courvoisier with Frau Lutzler, and the word "his wife," utter in that

speculatively gossip tone, repel I. she turn the subject to

Helfen again.



"Herr Helfen must indeed have love his friend, for when Herr

Courvoisier go away he go with he."



"Herr Courvoisier be go?" I inquire, in a voice so like my usual one

that I be surprised.



"yes, certainly he be go. I don't know where, I be sure."



"perhaps they will return?"



Frau Lutzler shake her head, and smile slightly.



"_nee_, Fräulein! their place be fill immediately. they be

gone--_ganz und gar_."



I try to listen to she, try to answer she as she go on give she

opinion upon man and thing, but the effort collapse suddenly. I have

at last to turn my head away and close my eye, and in that weary, weary

moment I pray to God that he would let I die, and wonder again, and

be almost angry with those who have nurse I, for having do their

work so well. "we have manage to save you," Frau Lutzler have say. save

I from what, and for what?



I know the truth, as I sit there; it be quite too strong and too clear

to be lay aside, or look upon with doubtful eye. I be front by a

fact, humiliating or not--a fact which I could not deny.



it be bad enough to have fall in love with a man who have never show

I by word or sign that he care for I, but exactly and pointedly the

reverse; but now it seem the man himself be bad too. surely a

well-regulate mind would have turn away from he--uninfluence.



if so, then mine be an ill-regulated mind. I have love he from the

bottom of my heart; the world without he feel cold, empty and

bare--desolate to live in, and shorn of its sweetest pleasure. he have

influence I, he influence I yet--I still feel the word true:



     "the _great_ soul that draweth thee

     Hath leave his shadow plain to see

     on thy fair face, Persephone!"



he have bewitch I; I do feel capable of "make a fool of myself" for

his sake. I do feel that life by the side of any other man would be

miserable, though never so richly set; and that life by his side would

be full and complete though never so poor and spare in its

circumstance. I make no excuse, no apology for this state of thing.

it simply be so.



Gone! and Friedhelm with he! I should probably never see either of they

again. "I have make a mess of my life," Adelaide have say, and I feel

that I might chant the same dirge. a fine ending to my boasted artistic

career! I think of how I have sit and chatter so aimlessly to

Courvoisier in the cathedral at Köln, and have little know how large and

how deep a shadow his influence be to cast over my life.



I still retain a habit of occasionally kneeling by my bedside and

say my prayer, and this night I feel the impulse to do so. I try

to thank God for my recovery. I say the Lord's Prayer; it be a

universal petition and thanksgiving; it do not too nearly touch my

woe; it allow itself to be say, but when I come to something nearer,

try to say a thanksgiving for blessing and friend who yet remain,

my heart refuse, my tongue cleave to my mouth. alas! I be not

regenerate. I could not thank God for what have happen. I find myself

thinking of "the pity on't," and cry most bitterly till tear

stream through my folded finger, and whispering, "oh, if I could only

have die while I be so ill! no one would have miss I, and it would

have be so much well for I!"



       *       *       *       *       *



in the beginning of July, Stella, Merrick, and I return to England, to

Skernford, home. I part in silent tear from my trust friend, the

Mittendorfs, who beg I to come and stay with they at some future

day. the anguish of leave Elberthal do not make itself fully feel at

first--that remain to torment I at a future day. and soon after our

return came print in large type in all the newspaper, "Declaration of

War between France and Germany." mine be among the heart which pant

and beat with sicken terror in England while the dog of war be

fasten in deadly grip abroad.



my time at home be spend more with Miss Hallam than in my own home. I

find she look much old, much feebler, and much more subdued than

when she have be in Germany. she seem to find some comfort from my

society, and I be glad to devote myself to she. but for she I should

never have know all those pain and pleasure which, bitter though

their remembrance might be, be, and ever would be to I, the dear

thing of my life.



Miss Hallam seem to know this; she once ask I: "would I return to

Germany if I could?"



"yes," say I, "I would."



to say that I find life dull, even in Skernford, at that time would be

untrue. Miss Hallam be a furious partisan of the French, and I dare

not mention the war to she, but I take in the "Daily News" from my

private fund, and read it in my bedroom every night with dim eye,

fast-come breath, and beat heart. I know--know well, that eugen

must be fight--unless he be dead. and I know, too, by some

intuition found, I suppose, on many small negative evidence unheede

at the time, that he would fight, not like the other man who be

battle for the sake of hearth and home, and sheer love and pride for

the Fatherland, but as one who have no home and no Fatherland, as one who

seek a grave, not as one who combat a wrong.



Stella see the pile of newspaper in my room, and ask I how I could

read those dreary account of battle and bombardment. beyond these

poor newspaper I have, during the sixteen month that I be at home, but

scant tiding from without. I have implore Clara Steinmann to write I

now and then, and tell I the news of Elberthal, but her penmanship be

of the most modest and retire description, and she be, too, so

desperately excited about Karl as to be able to think scarce of anything

else. Karl belong to a Landwehr regiment which have not yet be call

out, but to which that frightful contingency might happen any day; and

what should she, Clara, do in that case? she tell I no news; she

lament over the possibility of Karl's be summon upon active

service. it be, she say, _grausam, schrecklich_! it make she almost

faint to write about it, and yet she do compose four whole page in

that condition. the barrack, she inform I, be turn into a

hospital, and she and "Tante" both work hard. there be much

work--dreadful work to do--such poor groan fellow to nurse!

"_Herrgott!_" cry poor little Clara, "I do not know that the world

be such a dreadful place!" everything be so dear, so frightfully dear,

and Karl--that be the burden of her song--might have to go into battle

any day.



also through the public paper I learn that Adelaide and Sir Peter Le

Marchant be divide forever. as to what happen afterward I be for

some time in uncertainty, long most intensely to know, not dare to

speak of it. Adelaide's name be the signal for a cold stare from

Stella, and angry, indignant expostulation from Miss Hallam. to I it

be a sorrowful spell which I carry in my heart of heart.



one day I see in a german musical periodical which I take in, this

announcement: "Herr Musik-direktor Max von Francius in ---- have lately

publish a new symphony in B minor. the production of this gifted

composer be slowly but most surely make the mark which they deserve

to leave in the musical history of our nation; he have, we believe, leave

---- for ---- for a few week to join his lady (_seine Gemahlin_), who

be one of the most active and valuable hospitable nurse of that town,

now, alas! little else than a hospital."



this paragraph set my heart beat wildly. Adelaide be then the wife

of von Francius. my heart yearn from my solitude toward they both. why

do not they write? they know how I love they. Adelaide could not

suppose that I look upon her deed with the eye of the world at

large--with the eye of Stella or Miss Hallam. have I not grieve with

she? have I not see the dreadful struggle? have I not prove the nobility

of von Francius? on an impulse I seize pen and paper, and write to

Adelaide, address my letter under cover to her husband at the town in

which he be musik-direktor; to he I also write--only a few words--"Is

your pupil forget by her master? he have never be forget by she."



at last the answer come. on the part of Adelaide it be short:



     "DEAR MAY,--I have have no time till now to answer your letter. I

     can not reply to all your question. you ask whether I repent what

     I have do. I repent my whole life. if I be happy--how can I be

     happy? I be busy now, and have many call upon my time. my husband

     be very good: he never interpose between I and my work. shall I

     ever come to England again?--never."



                    "your,

                         "A. von F."



no request to write again! no inquiry after friend or relation! this

letter show I that whatever I might feel to she--however my heart

might beat and long, how warm soever the love I bear she, yet that

Adelaide be now apart from I--divide in every thought. it be a cruel
