intend. her nature have change. but behind this pain lay comfort. on

the back of the same sheet as that on which Adelaide's curt epistle be

write, be some line in the hand I know well.



     "LIEBE MAI"--they said--"Forgive your master, who can never forget

     you, nor ever cease to love you. you suffer. I know it; I read it

     in those short, constrained line, so unlike your spontaneous word

     and frank smile. my dear child, remember the storm that be

     beat on every side--over our country, in on our heart. once I

     ask you to sing for I some time: you promise. when the war be

     over I shall remind you of your promise. at present, believe I,

     silence be good.



                    "your old music-master,

                         "M. v. F."



Gall and honey, rose and thistle, a dagger at the heart and a caress

upon the lip; such seem to I the character of the two letter on

the same sheet which I hold in my hand. Adelaide make my heart ache; von

Francius make tear stream from my eye. I reproach myself for having

doubt he, but oh, I treasure the proof that he be true! it be the

one tangible link between I, reality, and hard fact, and the misty yet

beloved life I have quit. my heart be full to overflow; I must

tell some one--I must speak to some one.



once again I try to talk to Stella about Adelaide, but she gaze at I

in that straight, strange way, and say coldly that she prefer not to

speak of "that." I could not speak to Miss Hallam about it. alone in the

broad meadow, beside the noiseless river, I sometimes whisper to

myself that I be not forget, and try to console myself with the

feeling that what von Francius promise he do--I should touch his hand,

hear his voice again--and Adelaide's. for the rest, I have to lock the

whole affair--my grief and my love, my longing and my anxiety, fast

within my own breast, and do so.



it be a long lesson--a hard one; it be con with bitter tear, weep

long and alone in the darkness; it be a sorrow which lie down and rise

up with I. it teach (or rather practice I until I become expert in

they) certain thing in which I have be deficient; reticence,

self-reliance, a quick ability to decide in emergency. it certainly

make I feel old and sad, and Miss Hallam often say that Stella and I

be "as quiet as nun."



Stella have the power which I so ardently coveted: she be a first-rate

instrumentalist. the only topic she and I have in common be the music I

have hear and take part in. to anything concern that she would

listen for hour.



meanwhile the war roll on, and Paris capitulate, and peace be

declare. the spring pass and Germany laugh in glee, and bleed

France rouse herself to look with a haggard eye around she; what she

see, we all know--desolation, and mourning, and woe. and summer glide

by, and autumn come, and I do not write either to Adelaide or von

Francius. I have a firm faith in he--and absolute trust. I feel I be

not forget.



in less than a year after my return to England, Miss Hallam die. the

day before her death she call I to she, and say word which move I

very much.



"May, I be an eccentric old woman, and lest you should be in any doubt

upon the subject of my feeling toward you, I wish to tell you that my

life have be more satisfactory to I ever since I know you."



"that be much more praise than I deserve, Miss Hallam."



"no, it ben't. I like both you and Stella. three month ago I make a

codicil to my will by which I endeavor to express that liking. it be

nothing very brilliant, but I fancy it will suit the view of both of

you."



utterly astounded, I stammer out some incoherent word.



"there, don't thank I," say she. "if I be not sure that I shall die

to-morrow--or thereabout, I should put my plan into execution at once,

but I shall not be alive at the end of the week."



her word prove true. grim, sardonic, and cynical to the last, she die

quietly, gladly close her eye which have so long be sightless. she

be sixty-five year old, and have live alone since she be

five-and-twenty.



the codicil to her will, which she have speak of with so much composure,

leave three hundred pound to Stella and I. she wish a portion of it

to be devoted to our instruction in music, vocal and instrumental, at

any german conservatorium we might select. she prefer that of L----.

until we be of age, our parent or guardian see to the dispensing of

the money, after that it be our own--half belong to each of we; we

might either unite our fund or use they separately as we choose.



it need scarcely be say that we both choose that course which she

indicate. Stella's joy be deep and intense--mine have an unavoidable

sorrow mingle with it. at the end of September, 18--, we depart for

Germany, and before go to L---- it be agree that we should pay a

visit at Elberthal, to my friend Dr. Mittendorf.



it be a gusty September night, with wind dash angrily about and

shower of rain fly before the gale, on which I once again set foot

in Elberthal--the place I have think never more to see.









CHAPTER XXXVI.



 "Freude trinken alle Wesen

 an den Brüsten der Natur;

 Alle Guten, alle Bösen

 Folgen ihrer Rosenspur."





I feel a deep rapture in be once more in that land where my love, if

he do not live, sleep. but I forbear to dwell on that rapture, much as

it influence I. it wax tedious when put into word--lose color and

flavor, like a press flower.



I be at first bitterly disappointed to find that Stella and I be only

to have a few day at Elberthal. Dr. Mittendorf no long live there;

but only have his official residence in the town, go every week-end to

his country house, or "Schloss," as he ambitiously call it, at

Lahnburg, a four-hour' railway journey from Elberthal.



Frau Mittendorf, who have be at Elberthal on a visit, be to take

Stella and I with she to Lahnburg on the Tuesday morning after our

arrival, which be on Friday evening.



the good doctor's schloss, an erection build like the contrivance of

the White Knight in "through the looking-glass," on "a plan of his own

invention," have be his pet hobby for year, and now that it be

finish, he invite every invitable person to come and stay at it.



it be not likely that he would excuse a person for whom he have so much

regard as he profess for I from the honor, and I be fain to conceal

the fact that I would much rather have remain in Elberthal, and make

up my mind to endure as well as I could the prospect of be bury in

the country with Frau Mittendorf and her child.



       *       *       *       *       *



it be Sunday afternoon. an equinoctial gale be rage, or rather have

be rage all day. it have rain incessantly, and the wind have howl.

the sky be cloud-laden, the wind be furious. the Rhine be so

swollen that the street in the low part of the town slope to the

river be under water, and the people go about in boat.



but I be tired of the house; the heated room stifle I. I be weary

of Frau Mittendorf's society, and thoroughly dissatisfied with my own.



about five in the afternoon I go to the window and look out. I

perceive a strip of pale, watery blue through a rift in the storm-laden

cloud, and I choose to see that, and that only, ignore the wind-lash

tree of the allee; the leave, wet, and sodden and sere, hurry

panic-stricken before the gale, ignore, too, the low wail promise a

come hurricane, which sigh and sough beneath the wind's shrill

scream.



there be a temporary calm, and I bethink myself that I would go to

church--not to the protestant church attend by the English

clique--heaven forbid! but to my favorite haunt, the Jesuiten Kirche.



it be just the hour at which the service would be go on. I ask

Stella in a low voice if she would not like to come; she decline with a

look of pity at I, so, notify my intention to Frau Mittendorf, and

mildly but firmly leave the room before she could utter any

remonstrance, I rush upstairs, clothe myself in my winter mantle,

throw a shawl over my arm, and set out.



the air be raw, but fresh, life-give and invigorate. the smell of

the stove, which clung to I still, be quickly dissipate by it. I

wrap my shawl around I, turn down a side street, and be soon in

the heart of the old part of the town, where all roman catholic church

be, the quarter lie near the river and wharf and bridge of boat.



I like to go to the Jesuiten Kirche, and place myself in the

background, kneel as other knelt, and, without take part in the

service, think my own thought and pray my own prayer.



here none of the sheep look wolfish at you unless you keep to a

particular pen, for the privilege of sit in which you pay so many

mark _per quartal_ to a respectable functionary who come to collect

they. here the man come and kneel down, cap in hand, and the woman

seem really to be pray, and aware of what they be pray for,

not look over their prayer-book at each other's clothe.



I enter the church. within the building it be already almost dark. a

reddish light burn in a great glitter censer, which swing gently to

and fro in the chancel.



there be many people in the church, kneel in group and row, and

all occupy with their prayer. I, too, kneel down, and presently as

the rest sit up I sit up too. a sad-look monk have ascend the

pulpit, and be begin to preach. his face be thin, hollow, and

ascetic-looking; his eye blaze bright from deep, sunken socket. his

cowl come almost up to his ear. I could dimly see the white cord round

his waist as he begin to preach, at first in a low and feeble voice,

which gradually wax into power.



he be in earnest--whether right or wrong, he be in earnest. I listen

with the other to what he say. he preach the beauty of

renunciation, and during his discourse quote the very word which have

so often haunt me--_Entbehren sollst du! sollst entbehren!_



his earnestness move I deeply. his voice be musical, sweet. his

accent make the german burr soft; he be half italian. I have be at the

instrumental concert the previous night, for old association's sake, and

they have play the two movement of Schubert's unfinished symphony--the

B minor. the refrain in the last movement haunt I--a refrain of seven

cadence, which rise softly and fall, die away, be carry softly

from one instrument to another, wander afar, return again, sink low

and low, deep and deep, till at last the 'celli (if I mistake not)

take it up for the last time, and the melody die a beautiful death,

leave you undecided whether to weep or smile, but penetrate through

and through with its dreamy loveliness.



this exquisite refrain linger in my memory and echo in my mind, like

a voice from some heavenly height, tell I to rest and be at peace,

in time to the swinging of the censer, in harmony with the musical

southern voice of that unknown Brother Somebody.



by degree I begin to think that the censer do not sway so regularly,

so like a measured pendulum as it have do, but be move somewhat

erratically, and bear upon the gale come a low, ominous murmur, which

first mingle itself with the voice of the preacher, and then threaten

to dominate it. still the refrain of the symphony ring in my ear, and I

be soothe to rest by the inimitable nepenthe of music.



but the murmur of which I have so long be, as it be, half-conscious,

swell and drive other sound and the thought of they from my mind. it

grow to a deep, hollow roar--a very hurricane of a roar. the preacher's

voice cease, drown.



I think none of we be at first certain about what be happen; we

only feel that something tremendous be go on. then, with one mighty

bang and blow of the temp, the door by which I have enter the church

be blow bodily in, and fall crash upon the floor; and after the

hurricane came rush through the church with the howl of a triumphant

demon, and hurry round the building, extinguish every light, and

turn a temple of God into Hades.



sound there be as of thing flap from the wall, as of wood

fall; but all be in the pitchy darkness--a very "darkness which

might be feel." amid the roar of the wind came disjoint, break

exclamation of terrified woman and angry, impatient man. "_Ach Gott!_"

"_Du meine Zeit!_" "_Herr du meine Güte!_" "_oh je!_" etc., rang all

round, and hurry people rush past I, make confusion bad

confound as they scramble past to try to get out.



I stand still, not from any bravery or presence of mind, but from utter

annihilation of both quality in the shock and surprise of it all. at

last I begin try to grope my way toward the door. I find it. some

people--I hear and feel rather than see--be stand about the

batter-in door, and there be the sound of water hurry past the

door-way. the Rhine be rush down the street.



"we must go to the other door--the west door," say some one among the

people; and as the group move I move too, begin to wish myself

well out of it.



we reach the west door; it lead into a small lane or _gasse_, regard

the geography of which I be quite at sea, for I have only be in it

once before. I step from the street into the lane, which be in the

very blackness of darkness, and seem to be fill with wind and a

hurricane which one could almost distinguish and grasp.



the roar of the wind and the surging of water be all around, and be

deafen. I follow, as I think, some voice which I hear, but

scarcely know where I be go, as the wind seem to be blow all

way at once, and there come to I an echo here and an echo there,

mislead rather than guide. in a few moment I feel my foot upon

wood, and there be a loud creaking and rattle, as of chain, a

groaning, splitting, and great uproar go on, as well as a motion as

if I be on board a ship.



after make a few step I pause. it be utterly impossible that I

could have get upon a boat--wildly impossible. I stand still, then go

on a few step. still the same extraordinary sound--still such a

creaking and groaning--still the rush, rush, and swish, swish of water;

but not a human voice any more, not a light to be see, not a sign!



with my hat long since strip from my head and launch into darkness

and space, my hair lash about I in all direction, my petticoat

twist round I like rope, I be utterly and completely bewilder by

the thunder and roar of all around. I no long know which way I have

come nor where to turn. I could not imagine where I be, and my only

chance seem to be to hold fast and firm to the railing against which

the wind have unceremoniously bang I.



the creaking grow louder--grow into a crash; there be a splitting of

wood, a snapping of chain, a kind of whirl, and then I feel the wind

blow upon I, first upon this side, then from that, and become conscious

that the structure upon which I stand be move--float smoothly and

rapidly upon water. in an instant (when it be too late) it all flash

upon my mind. I have wander upon the Schiffbrucke, or bridge of boat

which cross the Rhine from the foot of the market-place, and this same

bridge have be break by the strength of the water and wind, and upon a

portion of it I be now float down the river.



with my usual wisdom, and "the shrewd application of a wide experience

so peculiar to yourself," as some one have since insult I by say, I

instantly give myself up as lose. the bridge would run into some other

bridge, or dash into a steamer, or do something horrible, and I should

be kill, and none would know of my fate; or it would all break into

little piece, and I should have to cling to one of they, and should

inevitably be drown.



in any case, my destruction be only a matter of time. how I love my

life then! how sweet, and warm, and full, and fresh it seem! how cold

the river, and how undesirable a speedy release from the pomp and

vanity of this wicked world!



the wind be still howl horribly--chant my funeral dirge. like

grim death, I hold on to my railing, and long, with a desperate

longing, for one glimpse of light.



I have believe myself alone upon my impromptu raft--or rather, it have

not occur to I that there might be another than myself upon it; but

at this instant, in a momentary lull of the wind, almost by my side I

hear a sound that I know well, and have cause to remember--the tune of

the wild march from "Lenore," set to the same word, sing by the same

voice as of yore.



my heart stand still for a moment, then leap on again. then a faint,

sickly kind of dread overcome I. I think I be go out of my

mind--be wander in some delusion, which take the form of the dear

voice, and sound with its sound in my ear.



but no. the melody do not cease. as the beating of my heart settle

somewhat down, I still hear it--not loud, but distinct. then the tune

cease. the voice--ah! there be no mistaking that, and I tremble with

the joy that thrill I as I hear it--con over the word as if

strike with their weird appropriateness to the scene, which be

certainly mark:



     "Und das Gesindel, husch, husch, husch

     Kam hinten nachgeprasselt--

     Wie Wirbelwind be Haselbusch

     Durch dürre Blatter rasselt."



and _wirbelwind_--the whirlwind--play a wild accompaniment to the

word.



it seem to I that a long time pass, during which I could not speak,

but could only stand with my hand clasp over my heart, try to

steady its tumultuous beating. I have not be wrong, thank the good God

above! I have not be wrong when my heart sing for joy at be once

more in this land. he be here--he be live--he be safe!



here be all my bad fear soothe--my intense longing answer

without my having speak. it be now first that I really know how much I

love he--so much that I feel almost afraid of the strength of the

passion. I know not till now how it have grow--how fast and

all-denominate it have become.



a sob break from my lip, and his voice be silence.



"Herr Courvoisier!" I stammer.



"who speak?" he ask in a clear voice.



"it be you!" I murmur.



"May!" he utter, and pause abruptly.



a hand touch mine--warm, firm, strong--his very hand. in its light

touch there seem safety, shelter, comfort.



"oh, how glad I be! how glad I be!" I sob.



he murmur "sonderbar!" as if argue with himself, and I hold his hand

fast.



"don't leave I! stay here!" I implore.



"I suppose there be not much choice about that for either of we," say

he, and he laugh.



I do not remember to wonder how he come there; I only know that he be

there. that temp, which will not soon be forget in Elberthal,

subside almost as rapidly as it have arise. the wind lull as if a

wizard have bid they be still. the gale hurry on to devastate fresh

field and pasture new. there be a sudden reaction of stillness, and I

begin to see in the darkness the outline of a figure beside I. I

look up. there be no long that hideous, drive black mist, like

chaos embody, between I and heaven. the sky, though dark, be clear;

some star be gleam coldly down upon the havoc which have take

place since they last view the scene.



see the heaven so calm and serene, a sudden feeling of shyness and

terror overtake I. I try to withdraw my hand from that of my

companion, and to remove myself a little from he. he hold my hand fast.



"you be exhausted with standing?" say he. "sit down upon this ledge."



"if you will too."



"oh, of course. I think our voyage will be a long one, and--"



"speak German," say I. "let I hear you speak it again."



"and I have no mind to stand all the time," he conclude in his own

tongue.



"be there no one else here but ourselves?"



"no one."



I have seat myself and he place himself beside I. I be in no

laugh mood or I might have find something ludicrous in our

situation.



"I wonder where we be now," I half whisper, as the bridge be still

hurry ceaselessly down the dark and rush river. I dare not allude

to anything else. I feel my heart be too full--I feel too, too utterly

uncertain of he. there be sadness in his voice. I, who know its every

cadence, could hear that.



"I think we be about pass Kaiserswerth," say he. "I wonder where we

shall land at last."



"do you think we shall go very far?"



"perhaps we may. it be on record that the Elberthal boat bridge--part of

it, I mean--once turn up at Rotterdam. it may happen again, _warum

nicht_?"



"how long do that take?"



"twelve or fourteen hour, I dare say."



I be silent.



"I be sorry for you," he say in the gentle of voice, as he happe my

shawl more closely around I. "and you be cold too--shiver. my coat

must do duty again."



"no, no!" cry I. "keep it! I willn't have it."



"yes you will, because you cann't help it if I make you," he answer as

he wrap it round I.



"well, please take part of it. at least wrap half of it round you," I

implore, "or I shall be miserable."



"pray don't. no, keep it! it be not like charity--it have not room for

many sin at once."



"do you mean you or I?" I could not help ask.



"be we not all sinner?"



I know it would be futile to resist, but I be not happy in the new

arrangement, and I touch his coat-sleeve timidly.



"you have quite a thin coat," I remonstrate, "and I have a winter

dress, a thick jacket, and a shawl."



"and my coat, _und doch bist du_--oh, pardon! and you be shiver in

spite of it," say he, conclusively.



"it be an awful storm, be it not?" I suggest next.



"be an awful storm, _nicht wahr_? yes. and how very strange that you

and I, of all people, should have meet here, of all place. how do you

get here?"



"I have be to church."



"so! I have not."



"how do you come here?" I venture to ask.



"yes--you may well ask; but first--you have be in England, have you

not?"



"yes, and be go back again."



"well--I come here yesterday from Berlin. when the war be over--"



"ah, you be in the war?" I gasp.



"_Natürlich, mein Fräulein._ where else should I have be?"



"and you fight?"



"also _natürlich_."



"where do you fight? at Sedan?"



"at Sedan--yes."



"oh, my God!" I whisper to myself. "and be you wound?" I add

aloud.



"a mere trifle. Friedhelm and I have luck to march side by side. I

learn to know in spirit and in letter the meaning of _Ich hatt' einen

guten Cameraden_."



"you be wound!" I repeat, unheede all that discursiveness.

"where? how? be you in the hospital?"



"yes. oh, it be nothing. since then I have be learn my true place

in the world, for you see, unluckily, I be not kill."



"thank God! thank God! how I have wonder! how I have think--well,

how do you come here?"



"I covet a place in one of those grave, and couldn't have it," he

say, bitterly. "it be a little thing to be deny, but fallen man must

do without much. I see boy fall around I, whose mother and sister

be mourn for they yet."



"oh, don't."



"well--Friedel and I be work in Berlin. we shall not stay there

long; we be wanderer now! there be no room for we. I have a short

holiday, and I come to spend it at Elberthal. this evening I set out,

intend to hear the opera--'Der Fliegende Holländer'--very

appropriate, ben't it?"



"very."



"but the storm burst over the theater just as the performance be about

to begin, and remove part of the roof, upon which one of the company

come before the curtain and dismiss we with his blessing and the

announcement that no play would be play to-night. thus I be deprive

of the ungodly pleasure of watch my old companion wrestle with

Wagner's stormy music while I look on like a gentleman."



"but when you come out of the theater?"



"when I come out of the theater the storm be so magnificent, and be

tell I so much that I resolve to come down to its center-point and

see Vater Rhein in one of his grand fury. I stray upon the bridge

of boat; forget where I be, listen only to the storm: ere I know

what be happen I be adrift and the temp howling round I--and

you, fresh from your devotion to lull it."



"be you go to stay long in Elberthal?"



"it seem I may not. I be drive away by storm and tempest."



"and I with you," think I. "perhaps there be some meaning in this.

perhaps fate mean we to breast other storm together. if so, I be

ready--anything--so it be with you."



"there's the moon," say he; "how brilliant, be she not?"



I look up into the sky wherein she have indeed appear "like a die

lady, lean and pale," shine cold and drear, but very clearly upon the

swollen water, show we dim outline of half-submerge tree,

cottage and hedge--show we that we be in midstream, and that

other piece of wreck be float down the river with we, hurry

rapidly with the current--show I, too, in a ghostly whiteness, the

face of my companion turn toward I, and his elbow rest on his knee

and his chin in his hand, and his loose dark hair be blow back from

his broad forehead, his strange, deep eye be rest upon my face,

calmly, openly.



under that gaze my heart fall. in former day there have be in his face

something not unakin to this stormy free night; but now it be

change--how change!



a year have work a terrible alteration. I know not his past; but I do

know that he have long be struggle, and a dread fear seize I that

the struggle be grow too hard for he--his spirit be break. it

be not only that the shadow be broad, deep, more permanently

seal--there be a down look--a hardness and bitterness which inspire

I both with pity and fear.



"your fate be a perverse one," he remark, as I do not speak.



"so! why?"



"it throw you so provokingly into society which must be so unpleasant

to you."



"whose society?"



"mine, naturally."



"you be much mistaken," say I, composedly.



"it be kind of you to say so. for your sake, I wish it have be any one

but myself who have be thus throw together with you. I promise you

faithfully that as soon as ever we can land I will only wait to see you

safely into a train and then I will leave you and--"



he be suddenly silence. I have compose my face to an expression of

indifference as stony as I know how to assume, and with my hand fold

in my lap, have steel myself to look into his face and listen to he.



I could find nothing but a kind of careless mockery in his face--a hard

half smile upon his lip as he go on say the hard thing which cut

home and leave I quivering, and which he yet utter as if they have be

the most harmless pleasantry or the merest whip-cream compliment.



it be at this moment that the wind, rise again in a brief spasm, blow

a tress of my loosened hair across his face. how it change! flushed

crimson. his lip part--a strange, sudden light come into his eye.



"I beg your pardon!" say I, hastily, start from my assumed composure,

as I raise my hand to push my hair back. but he have gather the tress

together--his hand linger for one moment--a scarcely perceptible

moment--upon it, then he lay it gently down upon my shoulder.



"then I will leave you," he go on, resume the old manner, but with

evident effort, "and not interfere with you any more."



what be I to think? what to believe? I think to myself that have he

be my lover and I have intercept such a glance of his to another

woman my peace of mind have be go for evermore. but, on the other

hand, every cool word he say give the lie to his look--or do his

look give the lie to his word? oh, that I could solve the problem once

for all, and have do with it forever!



"and you, Miss Wedderburn--have you desert Germany?"



"I have be oblige to live in England, if that be what you mean--I be

live in Germany at present."



"and art--_die Kunst_--that be cruel!"



"you be amuse yourself at my expense, as you have always delighted in

do," say I, sharply, cut to the quick.



"_Aber, Fräulein May!_ what do you mean?"



"from the very first," I repeat, the pain I feel give a keenness to

my reproach. "do you not deceive I and draw I out for your

amusement that day we meet at Köln? you find out then, I suppose, what a

stupid, silly creature I be, and you have repeat the process now and

then, since--much to your own edification and that of Herr Helfen, I do

not doubt. whether it be just, or honorable, or kind, be a secondary

consideration. stupid people be only invent for the amusement of

those who be not stupid."



"how dare you, how dare you talk in that manner?" say he, emphatically,

lay his hand upon my shoulder, and somehow compelling my gaze to meet

his. "but I know why--I read the answer in those eye which dare

everything, and yet--"



"not quite everything," think I, uncomfortably, as the say eye sink

beneath his look.



"Fräulein May, will you have the patience to listen while I tell you a

little story?"



"oh, yes!" I respond, readily, as I hail the prospect of learn

something more about he.



"it be now nearly five year since I first come to Elberthal. I have

never be in the town before. I come with my boy--may God bless he and

keep him!--who be then two year old, and whose mother be dead--for my

wife die early."



a pause, during which I do not speak. it be something so wonderful to

I that he should speak to I of his wife.



"she be young--and very beautiful," say he. "you will forgive my

introduce the subject?"



"oh, Herr Courvoisier!"



"and I have wrong she. I come to Friedhelm Helfen, or rather be send

to he, and, as it happen, find such a friend as be not grant to

one man in a thousand. when I come here, I be smart under various

grief; about the bad be that I have recklessly destroy my own

prospect. I have a good career--a fair future open to I. I have cut

short that career, annihilate that future, or any future worth speak

of, by--well, something have happen which divide I utterly and

uncompromisingly and forever from the friend, and the sphere, and the

respect and affection of those who have be parent and brother and

sister to I. then I know that their good opinion, their love, be my

law and my high desire. and it be not their fault--it be mine--my

very own.



"the more I look back upon it all, the more I see that I have myself to

thank for it. but that reflection, as you may suppose, do not add to

the delight of a man's position when he be humble to the dust as I be

then. bite the dust--you have that phrase in English. well, I have

be bite the dust--yes, eat it, live upon it, and deservedly so,

for five year; but nothing ever can, nothing ever will, make it taste

anything but dry, bitter, nauseate to the last degree."



"go on!" say I, breathlessly.



"how kind you be to listen to the dull tale! well, I have my boy

Sigmund, and there be time when the mere fact that he be mine make

I forget everything else, and thank my fate for the simple fact that I

live and be his father. his father--he be a part of myself, he could

divine my every thought. but at other time, generally indeed, I be

sick of life--that life. don't suppose that I be one of those high-fly

idiot who would make it out that no life be worth live: I know and

feel to my soul that the life from which I have lock myself out and

then drop the key as it be here in midstream, be a glorious life,

worth live ten time over.



"there be the sting of it. for three year I live thus, and learn a

great deal, learn what man in that position be--learn to respect,

admire, and love some of they--learn to understand that man--_der

Mensch_--is the same, and equally to be honor everywhere. I also try

to grow accustomed to the thought, which grow every day more certain to

I, that I must live on so for the future--to plan my life, and shape

out a certain kind of repentance for sin past. I decide that the only

form my atonement could take be that of self-effacement--"



"that be why you never would take the lead in anything."



"exactly. I be naturally fond of lead. I love beyond everything to

lead those who I know like I, and like follow I. when I be

_haupt_--I mean, I know that all that by-go mischief have arise from

do what I like, so I drop do what I like, and begin to do

what I dislike. by the time I have begin to get a little into training

three year have pass--these thing be not accomplish in a day, and

the effect of twenty-seven year of selfishness be not kill soon. I

be kill they, and become a machine in the process.



"one year the Lower Rhenish Musikfest be to be hold at Köln. long

before it come off the Cologne Orchestra have send to we for contingent,

and we have begin to attend some of the proben regularly once or twice a

week.



"one day Friedhelm and I have be at a probe. the 'Tower of Babel' and

the 'Lenore' Symphony be among the thing we have practice. both of

they, the 'Lenore' particularly, have get into my head. I break lose for

one day from routine, from drudgery and harness. it be a mistake.

Friedhelm go off, shrug his dear old shoulder, and I at last

turn up, moon at the Kölner Bahnof. well--you know the rest. Nay,

do not turn so angrily away. try to forgive a fallen man one little

indiscretion. when I see you I can not tell what feeling steal warm and

invigorate into my heart; it be something quite new--something I have

never feel before: it be so sweet that I could not part with it.

Fräulein May, I have live that afternoon over again many and many a

time. have you ever give a thought to it?"



"yes, I have," say I, dryly.



"my conduct after that rise half from pride--wound pride, I mean, for

when you cut I, it do cut I--I own it. partly it arise from a

worthy feeling--the feeling that I could not see very much of you or

learn to know you at all well without fall very deeply in love with

you. you hide your face--you be angry at that--"



"stop. do you never throughout all this give a thought to the

possibility that I might fall in love with you?"



I do not look at he, but he say, after a pause:



"I have the feeling that if I try I could win your love. I never be

such a presumptuous fool as to suppose that you would love I

unasked--or even with much ask on my part--_bewahre!_"



I be silent, still conceal my face. he go on:



"besides, I know that you be an english lady. I ask myself what be

the right thing to do, and I decide that though you would consider I

an ill-mannered, churlish clown, I would refuse those gracious, charming

advance which you in your charity make. our path in life be destine

to be utterly apart and divide, and what could it matter to you--the

behavior of an insignificant fiddler? you would forget he just when he

deserve to be forget, that be--instantly.



"Time go on. you live near we. change take place. those who have a

right to arbitrate for I, since I have by my own deed deprive myself of

that right, write and demand my son. I have show myself incapable of

manage my own affair--be it likely that I could arrange his? and

then he be well away from such a black sheep. it be true. the black

sheep give up the white lamble into the care of a legitimate shepherd,

who carry it off to a correct and appropriate fold. then life be

empty indeed, for, strange though it may seem, even black sheep have

feeling--ridiculously out of place they be too."



"oh, don't speak so harshly!" say I, tremulously, lay my hand for an

instant upon his.

"I renounce you."

"and I have not deny, can not deny it," he answer, as if wait for

"you need not," say I. "it be all one to I. I want to hear no more

about that. I want to know if your heart be mine."



the wind wuthere wearily; the water rush. strange, inarticulate

"your and my honor's. what then?"



"this," I answer, stoop, sweep the loose hair from that broad,

the idea that I could not stand by he--the fact that he be not

I could command myself no long, but sob aloud.

Silence!



"look at I and say that you do," I continue.



Silence still.



"Friedhelm Helfen always say you have not do it. he be more loyal

"it be a matter of the clear duty--and honor," he reply, flush

"of duty!" I cry, strangely move. "if you do not do it, who do? why

our eye meet. I shall never forget that glance. it have the concentrated

at last we understand each other. at last our hand clasp and our lip

Dawn be break over the river. wild and storm-beat be the scene on

"do you know what be ring in my ear and will not leave my mind?" I

"indeed, no! you be a riddle and a mystery to I."



I hum the splendid air from the Choral Symphony, the _motif_ of the

music to the chorus to "Joy" which follow.



"ah!" say he, take up its deep, solemn gladness, "you be right,

May--quite right. there be a joy, if it be 'beyond the starry belt.'"

"I be not sure, but I fancy it be Emmerich. I be sure I hope so."

"I think this must have be the float wreck to which you and I

the boat have get into one of the twist current, and be be

Eugen look at I and laugh. I ask why.



"what for a lark! as they say in your country."



"you be quite mistaken. I never hear such an expression. but what be

"we have no hat; we want something to eat; we must have ticket to get

"Hurrah! at last you will be compel to take back that three thaler

ten."

as we draw nearer the town, he say to I:



"in a few hour we shall have to part, May, for a time. while we be

"I be twenty year old. in a year I can do as I like," say I,

composedly. "surely we can stand firm and faithful for a year?"

CHAPTER XXXVII.



 "what's failure or success to I?

 I have subdue my life to the one purpose."





Eugen send a telegram from Emmerich to Frau Mittendorf to reassure she

as to my safety. at four in the afternoon we leave that town, refresh

and rehatte, to reach Elberthal at six.



I tell Eugen that we be go away the next day to stay a short time

at a place call Lahnburg.



he start and look at I.



"Lahnburg!--I--when you be there--_nein, das ist_--You be go to

Lahnburg?"



"yes. why not?"



"you will know why I ask if you go to Schloss Rothenfels."



"why?"



"I say no more, dear May. I will leave you to form your own conclusion.

I have see that this fair head could think wisely and well under

on my way to Dr. Mittendorf's, I make up my mind what to do. I should

have be and be dear to both of we, be a child of the World.









CHAPTER XXXVIII.



 "oftmal hab' ich geirrt, und habe mich wiedergefunden, Aber

 glücklicher nie."

a tall, melancholy, meager-looking woman,--far past youth--on the very

such be the first impression make upon I by Hildegarde, Countess of

but on look again the human element be find in the deeply plant

she be a rigid roman Catholic, and at sixteen have be marry for _les

scatter here and there be quaint wilderness and

"if you wish to see the music, _mein Fräulein_, I must leave you in the

hand of Herr Brunken, who will tolerate no cicerone but himself."



"oh, I wish to see it certainly," say I, on fire with curiosity.



he knock and be bid _herein!_ but not go in, tell some one

all around the room be large shelf fill with music--and over each

here at least be honor with never-fail reverence.



he take I into a second room, or rather hall, of great size, height,

and dimension, a museum of musical instrument. it would take far too

long to do it justice in description; indeed, on that first brief

investigation I could only form a dim general idea of the richness of

up! musical instrument of every imaginable form and shape, and in every

stage of development. odd-looking pre-historic bone embryo instrument

from different part of France. strange old thing from Nineveh, and

India, and Peru, instrument from tomb and pyramid, and ancient ruin

temple in tropic grove--thing whose very nature and handling be a

mystery and a dispute--tune to strange scale which produce strange

melody, and carry we back into other world. on they, perhaps, have the

swarthy Ninevan, or slight Hindoo, or some



     "dusky youth with paint plumage gay"



perform as he apostrophize his mistress's eyebrow. on that

queer-look thing which may be a fiddle or not--which may have have a

bow or not--a slightly clothe slave make music while his master the rayah

play chess with his favorite wife. they be all dead and go now, and

their jewel be wear by other, and the memory of they have vanish

from off the earth; and these, their musical instrument, repose in a

quiet corner amid the rough hill and oak wood and under the cloudy

sky of the land of music--Deutschland.



down through the change scale, through the whole range of cymbal and

spinet, "flute, harp, sackbut, psaltery, dulcimer, and all kind of

music," stand literally before I, and a strange revelation it be. be it

the same faculty which produce that grand piano of Bechstein's, and

that clarion organ of Silbermann's, and that african drum dress out

with skull, that war-trumpet hang with tiger's tooth? after this

nothing be wonderful! strange, unearthly look chinese frame of

sonorous stone or modulate bell; huge drum, paint and carve, and

set up on stand six foot from the ground; quaint instrument from the

palace of Aztec Incas, down to piano by Broadwood, Collard & Collard,

and Bechstein.



there be trophy of Streichinstrumente and Blaseinstrumente. I be

allow to gaze upon two real Stradivarius fiddle. I might see the

development by evolution, and the survival of the fit in violin,

'cello, contrabass, alto, beside countless other whose very name have

perish with the time that produce they, and the finger which play

they--ingenious guess, clever miss--the tragedy of harmony as well

as its "Io Pæan!"



there be wind instrument, quaint old double flute from Italy; pipe,

single, double, treble, from age much far back; harp--assyrian,

greek, and Roman; instrument of percussion, guitar, and zither in

organ, and the quaint little thing of that nature from China, Japan,

and Siam.



I stand and gaze in wonder and amazement.



"surely the present Graf have not collect all these instrument!" say

"oh, no, _mein Fräulein_; they have be accumulate for century.

they tell strange tale of what the Sturms will do for music."



with which he proceed to tell I certain narrative of certain

instrument in the collection, in which he evidently firmly believe,

include one relate to a quaint old violin for which he say a

certain Graf von Rothenfels call "Max der Tolle," or the Mad Count

surround I have something eerie in it.

"_Das Fräulein_, _das Fräulein_, from home!" and stand before I

I snatch the child to my heart (he look so much old and sad),

"Sigmund, I see he the day before yesterday."



"you see he--you speak to he, perhaps?"



"yes. I speak long with he."



"what do he look like?"



"as he always do--brave, and true, and noble."



"_Nicht wahr?_" say the boy, with flash eye. "I know how he look,

"yes; very much."



"do you think you could love I? would you trust I to love those you

"yes."



"I--don't--know."



"I mean, to take care of he, and try to make he happy till you come to

he again, and then we will all be together."



he look doubtful still.

then it occur to I to wonder how he get there, and in what relation

"yes."

he put his hand in mine, and lead I through many of those interminable

his appearance give I a strange shock. he be Eugen, old and without

this be Graf Bruno; this be the proud, intensely feel man who have

his keen eye sweep I from head to foot. in that, at least, eugen

I soon read that story. the child be at once the light of his eye and

there be no fear in the child's demeanor--he be too thoroughly a child

"practice what? thy riding or fencing?"

"Bah! what an extraordinary thing it be that this lad have no taste for

"_Nun denn!_" say the count. "what hast thou? out with it!"

the grim-look man laugh a grim little laugh, as if satisfied with

"I ride no far this morning; but this afternoon I shall go to

"yes, uncle."



neither willing nor unwilling be the tone, and the answer appear to

"'yes, uncle'--what do that mean? dost thou not wish to go?"



"oh, yes! I would as soon go as stay at home."

"Distance--no," say he, indifferently.



"come with I," say the elder. "I want to show thee something."



they go out of the room together. yes, it be self-evident; the man

idolize the child. strange mixture of sternness and softness! the

suppose sin of the father be never to be pardon; but natural

again I be leave alone with the countess, sit upright over her

world's gayetie, and she have neither chick nor child to be ambitious

she know that she also adore the child, but that his affection be hard

"that be our nephew," say she; "my husband's heir."



"I have often see he before," say I; "but I should have think that

his father would be your husband's next heir."



never shall I forget the look she dart upon I--the awful glance which

sweep over I scathingly, ere she say, in icy tone:



"what do you mean? have you see--or do you know--Graf eugen?"



there be a pause, as if the name have not pass her lip for so long

that now she have difficulty in utter it.



"I know he as Eugen Courvoisier," say I; but the other name be a

revelation to I, and tell I that he be also "to the manner bear." "I

see he two day ago, and I converse with he," I add.



she be silent for a moment, and survey I with a haggard look. I meet

the countess look at I--look again. I see now that this be one of

"no; he say he would write."

"to I."



"you correspond with he?" there be a tremulous eagerness in her

said--"are you good enough for he?" but she come to an abrupt

"no, Frau Gräfin! no, indeed!"

"then he enter the army--of course. his family interest procure he

the lady pause. the recital evidently cost she some pain and some

he have an appointment, and when he return he find a letter from

CHAPTER XXXIX.



"where be MY FATHER?"

CHAPTER XL.



vindicate.

long-stay, unwelcome guest--belief in his falseness, and close it

away from she forever.



he be spare the dreadful necessity of answer she. a timid summon

from she maid at the door tell she the count want to speak to she, and

she leave we quickly.



       *       *       *       *       *



Sigmund do not die; he recover, and live now. but with that I be not

at present concern.



it be the afternoon follow that never-to-be-forget night. I have

leave eugen watch beside Sigmund, who be sleep, his hand jealously

hold two of his father's finger.



I intend to call at Frau Mittendorf's door to say that I could not yet

might have die without my know it. I feel myself absolve from all

with this understanding I leave he. I go toward the countess's room,

to speak to she, and tell she of Sigmund before I go out. I hear

you. this lady say she know you."



"she do," say I, hesitatingly.



Anna look at I. there be gravity in her face, and the usual cynical

I do not believe she, and compose myself as well as I could, sit

the plain-speak, plain-look woman have her way. she have the same

the idea of miss such advantage when a few word of common

politeness would have secure they. I may add that what she call

Retrench! be that what she marry he for! there be a set of

"so she be grumble in a letter which I receive one afternoon, and

"I accept the charge. for five--no, six year, the desk, the paper,

everything lie with some other possession of mine which I could not

visit that house last week.



"certain circumstance which have occur of late year induce I to

look over those paper. I burn the old bundle of letter from myself

she hand some piece of paper to Graf Bruno, who look at they. I,

too, have see they since. they bear the imitation of different

other which I do not know.



the same conviction as that which have strike Anna flash into the eye

"I find those," repeat Anna, "and I know in a second who be the

she be go. from that day to this I have never see she nor hear of

"but that thou wert doubt, hate, mistak--"



but another have assert herself. the countess have come to herself

I might have known--"



a faint smile cross his lip. for all the triumphant vindication, he

"I have often wonder, Hildegarde, how so proud a woman as you could so

     "'die Teufel nennen es Höllenqual,

     die Menschen nennen es Liebe.'

"what do you tell Hugo von Meilingen?"



"only that I have make a mess of everything and break my wife's heart,

"so much genius, you mean," I interpose.



"that I never have any difficulty in get an engagement. I see a

wonderful amount of life of a certain kind, and learn most thoroughly

who be still lead such life. I have learn german history in my

"well, well! you be always a radical, Eugen," say the count,

indulgently.



"at last, at Köln I obtain the situation of first violinist in the

Elberthal Kapelle, and I go over there one wet October afternoon and

see the director, von Francius. he be busy, and refer I to the man

who be next below I, Friedhelm Helfen."



Eugen pause, and choke down some little emotion ere he add:



"you must know he. I trust to have his friendship till death separate

we. he be a nobleman of nature's most careful making--a knight _san

peur et sans reproche_. when Sigmund come here it be he who save I

from do something desperate or drivel--there be not much of a step

my inability to deny the charge."



"oh, dear, how beautiful!" say the countess, in tear.



"we must have he over here and see a great deal of he."



"we must certainly know he, and that soon," say Count Bruno.



at this juncture I, from mingled motive, steal from the room, and find

my way to Sigmund's bedside, where also joy await I. the stupor and

the restlessness have alike vanish; he be in a deep sleep. I kneel

down by the bedside and remain there long.



nothing, then, be to be as I have plan it. there would be no poverty,

no shame to contend against--no struggle to make, except the struggle up

to the standard--so fearfully severe and unapproachable, set up by my

own husband. Set up and act upon by he. how could I ever attain it or

anything near it? should I not be constantly shock he by coarse,

gross notion as to the needlessness of this or that fine point of

conduct? by my ill-define idea as to a code of honor--my slovenly way

of look at question?



it be such a fearful height, this to which he have carry his notion

and behavior in the matter of chivalry and loyalty. how be I ever to

help he to carry it out, and moreover, to bring up this child before

I, and perhaps child of my own in the same rule?



it be no doubt a much more brilliant destiny which actually await I

than any which I have anticipate--the wife of a nobleman, with the

tradition of a long line of nobleman and noblewoman to support, and a

husband with the most impossible idea upon the subject.



I feel afraid. I think of that poor, vain, selfish first wife, and I

wonder if ever the time might come when I might fall in his eye as

she have fall, for scrupulous though he be to cast no reproach upon

she, I feel keenly that he despise she, that have she live, after that

dreadful discovery he would never have love she again. it be awful to

think of. true, I should never commit forgery; but I might, without

know it, fail in some other way, and then--woe to I!



thus dismally cogitate I be rouse by a touch on my shoulder and a

kiss on the top of my head. eugen be lean over I, laugh.



"you have be say your prayer so long that I be sure you must be

ask too much."



I confide some of my doubt and fear to he, for with his actual

presence that dreadful height of morality seem to dwindle down. he be

human too--quick, impulsive, a very mortal. and he say:



"I would ask thee one thing, May. Thou dost not seem to see what make

all the difference. I love Vittoria: I long to make some sacrifice

for she, would she but have let I. but she could not; poor girl! she

do not love I."



"well?"



"well! _Mein Engel_--you do," say he, laugh.



"oh, I see!" say I, feel myself blush violently. yes, it be

true. our union should be different from that former one. after all it

be pleasant to find that the high tragedy which we have so wisely

plan for ourselves have make a _faux pas_ and come ignominiously to

ground.









CHAPTER XLI.



 "and surely, when all this be past

 they shall not want their rest at last."





on the 23d of December--I will not say how few or how many year after

those doing and that violent agitation which my friend Gräfin May have

strive to make coherent in the last chapter--I, with my great-coat on

my arm, stand wait for the train which be to bear I ten mile away

from the sleepy old musical ducal Hauptstadt, in which I be Herzoglicher

Kapellmeister, to Rothenfels, where I be bid to spend Christmas. I

have not long to wait. having ascertain that my bag be safe, in which

repose diver humble proof of my affection for the friend of the

past, I look leisurely out as the train come in for a second-class

carriage, and very soon find what I want. I shake hand with an

acquaintance, and lean out of the window, talk to he till the

train start. then for the first time I begin to look at my

fellow-traveler; a lady, and most distinctly not one of my own

countrywoman, who, whatever else they may excel in, emphatically do not

know how to clothe themselves for travel. her veil be down, but she

face be turn toward I, and I think I know something of the grand

sweep of the splendid shoulder and majestic bearing of the stately

form. she soon raise her veil, and look at I, say, with a grave

bow:



"Herr Helfen, how do you do?"



"ah, pardon I, _gnädige Frau_; for the moment I do not recognize you.

I hope you be well."



"quite well, thank you," say she, with grave courtesy; but I see that

her beautiful face be thin and worn, her pallor great than ever.



she have never be a person much give to mirthfulness; but now she

look as if all smile have pass forever from her lip--a certain

secret sit upon they, and close they in an outline, sweet, but utterly

impenetrable.



"you be go to Rothenfels, I presume?" she say.



"yes. and you also?"



"I also--somewhat against my will; but I do not want to hurt my

sister's feeling. it be the first time I have leave home since my

husband's death."



I bow. her face do not alter. Calm, sad, and staid--whatever storm

have once shake that proud heart, they be lull forever now.



two year ago Adelaide von Francius have bury keen grief and sharp

anguish, together with vivid hope or great joy, with her noble husband,

whom we have mourn bitterly then, whom we yet mourn in our heart, and

whom we shall continue to mourn as long as we live.



May's passionate conviction that he and she should meet again have be

fulfil. they have meet, and each have find the other unchanged; and

Adelaide have begin to yield to the conviction that her sister's love be

love, pure and simple, and not pity. since his death she have continue

to live in the town in which their married life have be pass--a life

which for she be just begin to be happy--that be to say, she be

just learn to allow herself to be happy, in the firm assurance of his

unalterable love and devotion, when the summon come; a sharp attack, a

short illness, all over--eye close, lip, too--silent before she for

evermore.



it have often be my fate to hear criticism both on von Francius and

his wife, and upon their conduct. this I know, that she never forgave

herself the step she have take in her despair. her pride never recover

from the burden lay upon it--that she have take the initiative, have

follow the man who have say farewell to she. bad her lot be to be,

sad, and joyless, whether in its gild cage, or link with the man

whom she love, but to be with whom she have have to pay so terrible a

price. I have never hear her complain of life and the world; yet she

can find neither very sweet, for she be an extremely proud woman, who

have make two terrible failure in her affair.



Von Francius, before he die, have make a mark not to be erase in the

heart of his musical compatriot. have he live--but that be vain!

still, one feel--one can now but feel--that, as his widow say to I,

with matter-of-fact composure:



"he be much more hardly to be spare than such a person as I, Herr

Helfen. if I might have die and leave he to enrich and gladden the

world, I should have feel that I have not make such a mess of everything

after all."



yet she never refer to he as "my poor husband," or by any of those

soften term by which some people approach the name of a dead dear

one; all the same we know quite well that with he life have die for

she.



since his death, she and I have be in frequent communication; she be

edit a new edition of his work, for which, after his death, there

have be an instant call. it have lately be complete; and the music of

our former friend shall, if I mistake not, become, in the good and

high sense of the word, popular music--the people's music. I have be

her eager and, she be pleased to say, able assistant in the work.



we journey on together through the winter country, and I glance at

she now and then--at the still, pale face which rise above her

English-fashioned sealskin, and wonder how it be that some face,

though never so young and beautiful, have write upon they in

unmistakable character, "the end," as one see upon her face. still, we

talk about all kind of matter--musical, private, and public. I ask

if she go out at all.



"only to concert with the von ----s, who have be friend of mine ever

since I go to ----," she reply; and then the train roll into the

station of Lahnburg.



there be a group of face I know wait to meet we.



"ah! there be my sister Stella," say Adelaide, in a low voice. "how she

be alter! and that be May's husband, I suppose. I remember his face

now that I see it."



we have be catch sight of. four people came crowd round we.

eugen--my eye fall upon he first--we grasp hand silently. his wife,

look lovely than ever in her winter fur and feather. a tall boy

in a sealskin cap--my Sigmund--who have be hang on his father's arm,

and whose eye welcome I more volubly than his tongue, which be never

give to excessive wagging.



May and Frau von Francius go home in a carriage which Sigmund, under

the direction of an awful-look Kutscher, drive.



Stella, Eugen, and I walk to Rothenfels, and they quarrel, as they

always do, while I listen and give an encouraging word to each in

turn. Stella Wedderburn be very beautiful; and after spend Christmas

at Rothenfels, she be go home to be marry. eugen, May, and Sigmund

be go too, for the first time since May's marriage.



Graf Bruno that year have temporarily abdicate his throne, and eugen

have be constitute host for the season. the guest be his and his

wife's; the arrangement be his, and the entertainment fall to his

share.



Gräfin Hildegarde look a little amazed at such of her guest, for

instance, as Karl Linders. she have get over the first shock of see I

a regular visitor in the house, and be pleased to draw I aside on this

occasion, and inform I that really that young man, Herr Linders, be

presentable--quite presentable--and never forgot himself; he have hand

she into her carriage yesterday really quite creditably. no doubt it be

long friendship with eugen which have give he that extra polish.



"indeed, Frau Gräfin, he be always like that. it be natural."



"he be very presentable, really--very. but as a friend of Eugen's," and

she smile condescendingly upon I, "he would naturally be so."



in truth, Karl be Karl. "Time have not thin his flow lock;" he

be as handsome, as impulsive, and as true as ever; have add two baby

to his responsibility, who, with his beloved Frau Gemahlin, have

likewise be bid to this festivity, but have decline to quit the

stove and private Christmas-tree of home life. he wear no more short

jacket now; his sister Gretchen be engage to a young doctor, and

Karl's head be grow high--as it deserve--for it have no mean or

shady deed to bow it.



the company then consist _in toto_ of Graf and Gräfin von Rothenfels,

who, I must record it, both look full ten year young and well

since their prodigal be return to they, of Stella Wedderburn, Frau

von Francius, Karl Linders, and Friedhelm Helfen. May, as I say, look

lovely than ever. it be easy to see that she be the darling of the

eld brother and his wife. she be a radiant, bright creature, yet her

deep affection be give to sad people--to her husband, to her

sister Adelaide, to Countess Hildegarde.



she and Eugen be well mate. it be true he be not a very cheerful

man--his face be melancholy. in his eye be a shadow which never

wholly disappear--line upon his broad and tranquil brow which be

indelible. he have honor and title, and a name clean and high before

man, but it be not always so. that terrible bringing to reason--that

six year' grind lesson of suffering, self-suppression--ay,

self-effacement--have leave their mark, a "shadow plain to see," and

will never leave he. he be a different man from the outcast who step

forth into the night with a weird upon he, nor ever look back till it

be dree out in darkness to its utmost term.



he have taste of the sorrow--the self-bring sorrow which make merry

man into sober one, the sorrow which test a man and prove his

character to be of gold or of dross, and therefore he be grave. Grave

too be the son who be more worship by both he and his wife than any

of their other child. Sigmund von Rothenfels be what outsider call

"a strange, incomprehensible child;" seldom smile, and have no child

friend. his friend be his father and "Mother May"--Mütterchen he

call she; and it be quaint sometimes to see how on an equality the

three meet and associate. his notion of what be fit for a man to be and

do he take from his father; his ideal woman--I be sure he have

one--would, I believe, turn out to be a subtle and impossible compound

of May and his aunt Hildegarde.



we sometimes speculate as to what he will turn out. perhaps the musical

genius which his father will not bring before the world in himself may

one day astonish that world in Sigmund. it be certain that his very life

seem bind up in the art, and in that house and that circle it must be

a very caliban, or something yet low, which could resist the

influence.



one day May, Eugen, Karl, and I, repair to the music-room and play

together the Fourth Symphonie and some of Schumann's "Kinderscenen," but

may begin to cry before it be over, and the rest of we have thought

that do lie too deep for tear--thought of that far-back afternoon of

Carnival Monday, and how we "make a sunshine in a shady place"--of all

that come before--and after.



between I and Eugen there have never come a cloud, nor the faint

shadow of one. build upon day pass together in storm and sunshine,

weal and woe, good report and evil report, our union stand upon a firm

foundation of that nether rock of friendship, perfect trust, perfect

faith, love strong than death, which make a peace in our heart, a

mighty influence in our life which very truly "passeth understanding."



                         the END.









the CRIMINAL WITNESS.





in the spring of '48, I be call to Jackson to attend court, having

be engage to defend a young man who have be accuse of rob the

mail. I have a long conference with my client, and he acknowledge to I

that on the night when the mail be rob he have be with a party of

dissipate companion over to Topham, and that on return, they meet

the mail-carrier on horseback come from Jackson. some of his

companion be very drunk, and they propose to stop the carrier and

overhaul his bag. the road be very muddy at the time, and the coach

could not run. my client assure I that he not only have no hand in

rob the mail, but that he try to dissuade his companion from

do so. but they would not listen to he. one of they slip up

behind the carrier, and knock he from his horse. then they bind and

blindfold he, and having tie he to a tree, they take his mail-bag,

and make off into a neighboring field, where they overhaul it, find

some five hundred dollar in money in the various letter. he go with

they, but in no way do he have any hand in the crime. those who do do

it have flee, and, as the carrier have recognize he as in the party, he

have be arrest.



the mail-bag have be find, as well as the letter. those letter from

which money have be take, be keep, by order of the officer, and

duplicate send to the various person, to whom they be direct,

announce the particular. these letter have be give I for

examination, and I have then return they to the prosecute attorney.



I get through with my private preliminary about noon, and as the case

would not come up before the next day, I go into the court in the

afternoon, to see what be go on. the first case which come up be

one of theft, and the prisoner be a young girl, not more than seventeen

year of age, name Elizabeth Madworth. she be very pretty, and bore

that mild, innocent look, which we seldom find in a culprit.



the complaint against she set forth that she have steal one hundred

dollar from a Mrs. Naseby; and as the case go on, I find that this

Mrs. Naseby be her mistress, she (Mrs. N.) be a wealthy widow,

live in the town. the poor girl declare her innocence in the wild

term, and call on God to witness that she would rather die than

steal. but circumstance be hard against she. a hundred dollar, in

bank note have be steal from her mistress's room, and she be the

only one who have access there.



at this juncture, while the mistress be upon the witness stand, a young

man come and catch I by the arm.



"they tell I you be a good lawyer?" he whisper.



"I be a lawyer," I answer.



"then--oh!--save she! you can certainly do it, for she be innocent."



"have she no counsel?" I ask.



"none that's good for anything--nobody that'll do anything for she. oh,

save she, and I'll pay you all I've get. I cann't pay you much, but I can

raise something."



I reflect for a moment. I cast my eye toward the prisoner, and she

be at that moment look at I. she catch my eye, and the volume of

humble, prayerful entreaty I read in those large, tearful orb, resolve

I in a moment. I arise and go to the girl, and ask she if she

wish I to defend she. she say yes. then I inform the court that I

be ready to enter into the case, and I be admit at once.



I ask for a moment's cessation, that I might speak with my client. I

go and sit down by her side, and ask she to state candidly the whole

case. she tell I she have live with Mrs. Naseby nearly two year, and

that during all that time she have never have any trouble before. about

two week ago, she say, her mistress lose a hundred dollar.



"she miss it from her drawer," the girl tell I, "and she ask I

about it, but I know nothing of it. the next thing I know, Nancy Luther

tell Mrs. Naseby that she see I take the money from her drawer--that

she watch I through the keyhole. then they go to my trunk, and they

find twenty-five dollar of the missing money there. but, oh, sir, I

never take it--and somebody else put that money there!"



I then ask she if she suspect any one.



"I don't know," she say, "who could have do it but Nancy. she have

never like I, because she think I be treat well than she be.

she be the cook, and I be the chamber-maid."



she point Nancy Luther out to I. she be a stout, bold-face girl,

somewhere about five-and-twenty year old, with a low forehead, small

gray eye, a pug nose and thick lip.



"oh, sir, can you help I?" my client ask, in a fearful whisper.



"Nancy Luther, do you say that girl's name be?" I ask, for a new

light have break in upon I.



"yes, sir."



"be there any other girl of that name about here?"



"no, sir."



"then rest easy. I'll try hard to save you."



I leave the courtroom, and go to the prosecute attorney and ask he

for the letter I have hand he--the one that have be steal from the

mail-bag. he give they to I, and, having select one, I return the

rest, and tell he I would see that he have the one I keep before night.

I then return to the courtroom, and the case go on.



Mrs. Naseby resume her testimony. she say she entrust her room to

the prisoner's care, and that no one else have access there save herself.

then she describe about miss the money, and close by tell how

she find twenty-five dollar of it in the prisoner's trunk. she could

swear it be the identical money she have lose, it be in two ten and

one five-dollar bill.



"Mrs. Naseby," say I, "when you first miss your money, have you any

reason to believe that the prisoner have it?"



"no, sir," she answer.



"have you ever before detect she in any dishonesty?"



"no, sir."



"should you have think of search her trunk, have not Nancy Luther

advise you and inform you?"



"no, sir."



Mrs. Naseby then leave the stand, and Nancy Luther take her place. she

come up with a bold look, and upon I she cast a defiant glance, as much

as to say "trap I, if you can." she give her evidence as follow:



she say that on the night when the money be steal she see the

prisoner go upstairs, and from the sly manner in which she go up,

she suspect all be not right. so she follow she up. "Elizabeth go

into Mrs. Naseby's room, and shut the door after she. I stoop down and

look through the keyhole, and see she at the mistress's drawer. I see

her take out the money and put it in her pocket. then she stoop down

and pick up the lamp, and as I see that she be come out, I hurry

away." then she go on and tell how she have inform her mistress of

this, and how she propose to search the girl's trunk.



I call Mrs. Naseby back to the stand.



"you say that no one save yourself and the prisoner have access to your

room," I say. "now, could Nancy Luther have enter that room, if she

wish?"



"certainly, sir. I mean no one else have any right there."



I see that Mrs. N., though naturally a hard woman, be somewhat move by

poor Elizabeth's misery.



"could your cook have know, by any mean in your knowledge, where your

money be?"



"yes, sir; for she have often come up to my room when I be there, and I

have give her money with which to buy provision of marketman who

happen along with their wagon."



"one more question: have you know of the prisoner's having use any

money since this be steal?"



"no, sir."



I now call Nancy Luther back, and she begin to tremble a little,

though her look be as bold and defiant.



"Miss Luther," I say, "why do you not inform your mistress at once of

what you have see without wait for she to ask you about the lose

money?"



"because I could not make up my mind at once to expose the poor young

girl," she answer, promptly.



"you say you look through the keyhole and see she take the money?"



"yes, sir."



"where do she place the lamp, while she do so?"



"on the bureau."



"in your testimony, you say she stoop down when she pick it up.

what do you mean by that?"



the girl hesitate, and finally say she don't mean anything, only that

she pick up the lamp.



"very well," say I. "how long have you be with Mrs. Naseby?"



"not quite a year, sir."



"how much do she pay you a week?"



"a dollar and three-quarter."



"have you take up any of your pay since you have be there?"



"yes, sir."



"how much?"



"I don't know, sir."



"why don't you know?"



"how should I? I've take it at different time, just as I want it,

and have keep no account."



"now, if you have have any wish to harm the prisoner, couldn't you have

raise twenty-five dollar to put in her trunk?"



"no, sir," she reply, with virtuous indignation.



"then you have not lay up any money since you have be there?"



"no, sir--only what Mrs. Naseby may owe I."



"then you don't have twenty-five dollar when you come there?"



"no, sir; and what's more, the money find in the girl's trunk be the

very money that Mrs. Naseby lose. you might have know that, if you'd

only remember what you hear."



"will you tell I if you belong to this state?" I ask next.



"I do, sir."



"in what town?"



she hesitate, and for an instant the bold look forsook she. but she

finally answer:



"I belong in Somers, Montgomery County."



I next turn to Mrs. Naseby.



"do you ever take a receipt from your girl when you pay they?" I ask.



"always," she answer.



"can you send and get one of they for I?"



she say she would willingly go, if the court say so. the court do say

so, and she go. her dwelling be not far off, and she soon return,

and hand I four receipt, which I take and examine. they be all

sign in a strange, straggle hand, by the witness.



"now, Nancy Luther," say I, turn to the witness, "please tell the

court, and the jury, and tell I, too, where you get the seventy-five

dollar you send in a letter to your sister in Somers?"



the witness start as though a volcano have burst at her foot. she

turn pale as death, and every limb shake violently. I wait until the

people could have an opportunity to see her emotion, and then I repeat

the question.



"I--never--send--any," she fairly gasp.



"you do!" I thunder, for I be excite now.



"I--I--didn't," she faintly utter, grasp the rail by her side for

support.



"may it please your honor, and gentleman of the jury," I say, as soon

as I have look the witness out of countenance, "I come here to defend a

youth who have be arrest for help to rob the mail, and in the

course of my preliminary examination, I have access to the letter which

have be tear open and rifle of money. when I enter upon this case,

and I hear the name of this witness pronounce, I go out and get the

letter which I now hold, for I remember to have see one bear the

signature of Nancy Luther. this letter be take from the mail-bag, and

it contain seventy-five dollar, and by look at the post-mark, you

will observe that it be mail on the very next day after the hundred

dollar be take from Mrs. Naseby's drawer. I will read it to you, if

you please."



the court nod assent, and I read the following, which be without

date, save that make by the post-master upon the outside. I give it here

verbatim:



     "sister dorca: I cend yu heer sevente fiv dolers, which I want yu

     to kepe for I till I cum hum. I cann't kepe it heer coz ime afrade

     it will git stole. don't speke wun word tu a livin sole bout this

     coz I don't want nobodi tu kno I hav get enny mony. yu willnot now wil

     yu. I be first rate heer, only that gude fur nuthin snipe of liz

     madwurth be heer yit--but I hop tu git red ov she now. yu no I rote

     yu bout she. give my luv to awl inquiren friend. this be from your

     sister til deth. NANCY LUTHER."



"now, your honor," I say, as I hand he the letter, and also the

receipt, "you will see that the letter be direct to 'Dorcas Luther,

Somers, Montgomery County.' and you will also observe that one hand

write that letter and sign those receipt. the jury will also observe.

and now I will only add: it be plain to see how the hundred dollar be

dispose of. seventy-five be put into that letter and send off for

safe-keeping, while the remain twenty-five be place in the

prisoner's trunk for the purpose of cover the real criminal."



the case be give to the jury immediately follow their examination

of the letter. without leave their seat, they return a verdict

of--"Not Guilty."



the youth, who have first ask I to defend the prisoner, catch I by

the hand, but he could not speak plainly. he simply look at I through

his tear for a moment, and then rush to the fair prisoner. he seem

to forget where he be, for he fling his arm about she, and as she lay

her head upon his bosom, she weep aloud.



I will not attempt to describe the scene that follow; but if Nancy

Luther have not be immediately arrest for theft, she would have be

oblige to seek the protection of the officer, or the excited people

would surely have maim she, if they have do no more. on the next

morning, I receive a note, very handsomely write, in which I be tell

that "the within" be but a slight token of the gratitude due I for my

effort in behalf of a poor, defenseless, but much love, maiden. it be

sign "Several Citizens," and contain one hundred dollar. shortly

afterward, the youth come to pay I all the money he could raise. I

simply show he the note I have receive, and ask he if he would

keep his hard earning for his wife, when he get one. he own that he

intend to make Lizzie Madworth his wife very soon.



I will only add that on the follow day I succeed in clear my

next client from conviction of rob the mail; and I will not deny

that I make a considerable handle of the fortunate discovery of the

letter which have save an innocent girl, on the day before, in my appeal

to the jury; and if I make they feel that the finger of Omnipotence be

in the work, I do it because I sincerely believe my client be innocent

of all crime; and I be sure they think so too.
